To receive any announcements from the Chair.

6. Public Forum
Up to 30 minutes is allowed for this item.

Any member of the public or councillor may participate in Public Forum. Public
Forum items must relate to the remit of the committee and should be addressed
to the Chair of the committee.

Members of the public who plan to attend a public meeting at City Hall are
advised that you will be required to sign in when you arrive. Please note that you
will be issued with a visitor pass which you will need to display at all times.

Please also note:

Questions
1. Written public questions must be received by 5.00 pm, at least 3 clear
working days prior to the meeting. For this meeting, this means that
questions must be received at the latest by 5.00 pm on Friday 17 October
2025. Public Questions should be submitted via our webform:
www.bristol.gov.uk/publicforum
Transport & Connectivity Policy Committee – Agenda

2. Any individual can submit up to 3 written questions.
3. Written replies to questions will be available on the Council’s website at
least one hour before the meeting.
4. At the meeting, questioners will be permitted to ask up to 2 oral
supplementary questions.

Statements
1. Written statements must be received at latest by 12.00 noon, at least 2
working days prior to the meeting. For this meeting, this means that
statements must be received at the latest by 12.00 noon on Tuesday 21
October 2025. Public Statements should be submitted via our webform:
www.bristol.gov.uk/publicforum
2. Statements, provided they are no more than 1,000 words in length, will
be circulated to all committee members and will be published on the
Council’s website at least one hour before the meeting.

Petitions
1. Details of the wording of any petitions, and the number of signatories to
petitions must be received at latest by 12.00 noon, at least 2 working days
prior to the meeting. For this meeting, this means that petition details
must be received at the latest by 12.00 noon on Tuesday 21 October
2025. Please email petition details to policycommittees@bristol.gov.uk
2. At the meeting, individuals presenting petitions may be required to read
out the objectives of the petition.

When submitting a question or statement please indicate whether you are planning
to attend the meeting to present your statement ask your question

7. Management and Mitigation of CRSTS Scheme Delivery
To update on the likely short-term impacts of constructing the City Region (Pages 19 - 41)
Sustainable Transport Settlement (CRSTS) schemes and the mitigation measures
being put in place to minimise disruption.

8. Bristol Resident Parking Scheme Expansion Programme
This paper sets out the case for expanding Bristol City Council’s (BCC) Resident (Pages 42 - 68)
Parking Schemes (RPS) programme (formerly Resident Parking Zones). It outlines
how the implementation of RPS can support BCC in better managing parking to
prioritise more sustainable uses of the kerbside, reduce private vehicle trips, and
enhance safety and environmental quality across all wards.
Transport & Connectivity Policy Committee – Agenda

9. CAZ Exemption and Financial Support Package
To seek approval of a time limited exemption package for eligible citizens with (Pages 69 - 140)
Clean Air Zone (CAZ) non-compliant vehicles impacted by the introduction of a
Bus Lane and Bus Gate on Queens Road and Park Street

10. Cycle Hangar Policy
To seek approval to enact a target policy for the delivery of new cycle hangars up (Pages 141 - 182)
to 2030, and to adopt the proposed position. To seek delegated approval to bid
for, accept and commit to any funding in support of the policy.

11. Centre Line Policy
To seek approval from the Transport and Connectivity Committee on a policy (Pages 183 - 198)
position that road centrelines on selected 20mph roads are not reinstated as
default following resurfacing so that road safety benefits are realised by this
maintenance programme, and are only reinstated where determined essential by
the Road Safety Team. Alongside this, the safeguarding process for reviewing and
implementing changes is presented.

12. Vehicle towing contract
To seek approval to reprocure the Highway Maintenance Vehicle Towing (Pages 199 - 209)
Contract. The contract is essential to gain site possession to carryout Statutory
Highway Maintenance and other related works in Bristol’s busy urban
environment. The towing contractor removes parked vehicles which remain in
place despite advanced notification of the work, and places them in a
neighbouring street.

13. Workplace Parking Levy Update report
To update the committee on progress with the Workplace Parking Levy Outline (Pages 210 - 299)
Business Case (OBC) and to outline progress on key project milestones.

14. Quarterly Finance Report: P5/Q2
The Council Revenue and Capital budget for 2025/26 was agreed by Full Council (Pages 300 - 305)
on 25 February 2025. This report presents to the Transport and Connectivity
Committee the financial forecast at Period 5 (P5) against the budget as at the end
of August 2025.
Transport & Connectivity Policy Committee – Agenda

Information note – Officer Executive Decisions
Please note that the Council publishes details of Officer Executive Decisions that have been taken. These
are decisions which do not meet the criteria for a formal key decision to be taken at a Policy Committee
meeting, but are considered to be important enough to be open to public scrutiny. This includes all
Officer Executive Decisions involving a resource commitment of between £100,000 and £500,000 taken in
consultation with the relevant Policy Committee Chair. Officer Executive Decisions are also published
when a key decision has been to a Policy Committee and authority delegated to an officer to take the
decision. Details can be found here:
Officer executive decisions (bristol.gov.uk)
Agenda Item 1
www.bristol.gov.uk

Public Information Sheet
Inspection of Papers - Local Government (Access to Information) Act 1985

You can find papers for all our meetings on our website at www.bristol.gov.uk.

Public meetings for Policy Committees are held at City Hall.

Attendance at Public meetings

Bristol City Council welcomes public attendance and participation at all of its formal committee
meetings. When attending public meetings please behave with courtesy, tolerance and respect at
all times to Councillors, Council Officers and other meeting participants. Attendees who cause
disturbance may be asked to leave or, may be removed from the meeting.

If you’re a member of the press or public and want to attend a public meeting at City Hall, you will
need to sign in when you arrive. You will be issued with a visitor pass which you will need to
display at all times.

Due to the maximum occupancy of the venue, you may be asked to watch the meeting on a screen
in another room.

Other formats and languages and assistance for those with hearing impairment
Other o check with and
You can get committee papers in other formats (e.g. large print, audio tape, braille etc) or in
community languages by contacting the Policy Committee Officer. Please give as much notice as
possible. We cannot guarantee re-formatting or translation of papers before the date of a
particular meeting.

Committee rooms are fitted with induction loops to assist people with hearing impairment. If you
require any assistance with this please speak to the Policy Committee Officer.

Public Forum
Members of the public may make a written statement, ask a question, or present a petition to
most meetings. Please ensure that any submissions made are respectful, factual, and relevant.

• By contributing to the public forum process the participant acknowledges that any content
submitted is at the authors own risk and the Council disclaims any obligation or
responsibility for it.
• Questions, Statements and Petitions should be factually based and should not contain
anything that could be construed as being defamatory, frivolous or offensive. Any

Page 6
www.bristol.gov.uk

submission including such information shall be redacted prior to publication without notice
to the author.
• The Council reserves the right to reject any submission it deems defamatory, frivolous or
offensive at its sole discretion.
• Sensitive personal information may be deleted or redacted
• Officer’s names below Head of Service, will be replaced by the Officer’s job title
• Company names may be deleted or redacted

Your statement or question will be sent to the Committee Members and will be published on the
Council’s website before the meeting.

Public Questions and Statements should be submitted via our webform:
www.bristol.gov.uk/publicforum

Petitions should be e-mailed to policycommittees@bristol.gov.uk

The following requirements apply:

• The statement is received no later than 12.00 noon two working days before the meeting and
is about a matter which is the responsibility of the committee concerned.
• The question is received no later than 5pm three clear working days before the meeting.

Any statement submitted should be no longer than one side of A4 paper. For copyright reasons,
we are unable to reproduce or publish newspaper or magazine articles that may be attached to
statements.

By participating in public forum business, we will assume that you have consented to your name
and the details of your submission being recorded and circulated to the Committee and published
within the minutes. Your statement or question will also be made available to the public via
publication on the Council’s website and may be provided upon request in response to Freedom of
Information Act requests in the future.

We will try to remove personal and identifiable information. However, because of time
constraints we cannot guarantee this, and you may therefore wish to consider if your statement
contains information that you would prefer not to be in the public domain. Other committee
papers may be placed on the council’s website and information within them may be searchable on
the internet.

During the meeting:

• Public Forum is normally one of the first items on the agenda, although statements and
petitions that relate to specific items on the agenda may be taken just before the item
concerned.
• There will be no debate on statements or petitions.

Page 7
www.bristol.gov.uk

• The Chair will call each submission in turn. When you are invited to speak, please make sure
that your presentation focuses on the key issues that you would like Members to consider.
This will have the greatest impact.
• Your time allocation may have to be strictly limited if there are a lot of submissions. This may
be as short as one minute.
• If there are a large number of submissions on one matter a representative may be requested
to speak on the groups behalf.
• If you do not attend or speak at the meeting at which your public forum submission is being
taken your statement will be noted by Members.
• Under our security arrangements, please note that members of the public (and bags) may be
searched. This may apply in the interests of helping to ensure a safe meeting environment for
all attending.
• As part of the drive to reduce single-use plastics in council-owned buildings, please bring your
own water bottle in order to fill up from the water dispenser.

For further information about procedure rules please refer to our Constitution
https://www.bristol.gov.uk/how-council-decisions-are-made/constitution

Webcasting/ Recording of meetings

Members of the public attending meetings or taking part in Public forum are advised that Policy
Committee meetings may be filmed for live or subsequent broadcast via the council's webcasting
pages. The whole of the meeting is filmed (except where there are confidential or exempt
items). If you ask a question or make a representation, then you are likely to be filmed and will be
deemed to have given your consent to this. If you do not wish to be filmed you need to make
yourself known to the webcasting staff. However, the Openness of Local Government Bodies
Regulations 2014 now means that persons attending meetings may take photographs, film and
audio record the proceedings and report on the meeting (Oral commentary is not permitted
during the meeting as it would be disruptive). Members of the public should therefore be aware
that they may be filmed by others attending and that is not within the council’s control.

Our privacy notice can be viewed at www.bristol.gov.uk/about-our-website/privacy-and-
processing-notices-for-resource-services

Those who attended the meeting were invited to ask up to two supplementary questions.

Q6, Q7, Q8 – Melissa Topping
In response to follow-up questions, it was clarified that data has been collected before the start of the
trial period as well as during. The start date of 14/04/25 had been identified as when the vast majority of
the scheme had been installed. Data will be collated, analysed and evaluated following the end of the six
month trail. Release of the data is planned for December 2025 and a report to the Committee in the
Spring of 2026.

The WECIL report about accessibility in the local area was being evaluated and responses prepared in
response to recommendations as well as continued dialogue. The EQIA reflects many different
individuals, groups and other forms of feedback and would continue to be updated.

Q10 Tristan Beckett
Officers confirmed that a Traffic Regulation Order would be required to introduce a yellow box to the exit
from Pile Marsh onto Blackswarth Road. The junction would be assessed following the conclusion of the
trial period, along with the impacts of the scheme as a whole.

Q11, Q12, Q13 Satwant Phull
It was clarified that the engagement consultation exercise for EBLN had contained a free text option. The
trial would continue with a decision regarding the future of the scheme in coming months.

Q14, Q15, Q16 – Dan Ackroyd
Officers would respond in more detail outlining the process leading to the design of the junction
connecting Richmond Hill/Queens Road (ACTION). It was confirmed that the Active Travel England Panel
and Guidelines had not raised concerns in relation to the junction.

Q20 – Bristol Cycling Campaign
Officers accepted a meeting with Bristol Cycling Campaign and Bristol Walking Alliance to consider the
rationale behind the alteration in design. It was requested that the Road Safety audit of that junction be
provided in advance of that meeting (ACTION)

Q28, Q29, Q30 – Matt Sanders
It was confirmed by officers that yellow warning signs are guidance, providing additional information to
users but not a legal requirement. The signs were designed to comply with the Traffic Signs manual and
guidance. If there are any site specific changes to the design this will become known at installation phase.

Officers took away the information regarding the length of time that the yellow signs have been in the
City Centre.

7 Road Safety Task and Finish Group

The Committee considered a report that proposed the establishment of a task and finish group for the
development of the Road Safety Strategy, and associated Terms of Reference.

It was noted that this was a non key decision report.

The report was introduced and outlined by officers and Councillor Wilcox as the proposed Chair of the
Group. Through discussion, Members raised the following points:

- Full Council supported ‘Vision Zero’ in July 2023, aiming for zero road deaths or serious injuries.
- An average of 5 people a day are killed on the roads, with little year on year reduction.
- The current Road Safety Strategy (2014 -2024) is due for renewal to reflect new challenges and
developments including consideration of new transport modes: e-scooters, delivery vehicles,
autonomous vehicles and sustainable traffic. Target completion for the new strategy was March
2026.
- There was debate over whether the project should be officer led or Councillor led through a Task
and Finish Group. Concerns were that the involvement of Councillors could slow progress, or the
alternative view that Councillors can provide valuable insights on local accident hot spots and
issues raised through casework.
- There could be scope to influence the consultation on autonomous vehicles (a response had been
submitted from the Public Safety and Protection Committee).
- It was important to include a wide range of stakeholders such as the gig economy (e.g
Independent Workers Union). A stakeholders list would be reviewed at the beginning of the
process.

Following the discussion, the report recommendations were moved by Councillor Plowden and seconded
by Councillor Wilcox.

On being put to the vote the recommendations were CARRIED unanimously and it was APPROVED;

1. That the Committee for Transport and Connectivity noted the report and further details supplied in
Appendix A and approved the recommended approach and Terms of Reference for the Road Safety
Task and Finish Group.
2. That the Committee for Transport and Connectivity agreed the frequency and duration of meetings
to inform the Terms of Reference

Page 13
policycommittees@bristol.gov.uk

8 Active Travel Fund tranche 5 and 6 bid

The Committee considered a report that sought approval to accept and spend £2,286,807 funding from
Active Travel Fund tranche 5 and 6. This would be used to develop and deliver several active travel
projects.

It was noted that this was a key decision as it involved over £500k and impacted 2 or more wards.

Officers introduced the report clarifying the following:

The active travel programme of schemes includes the proposal to remove motorised traffic from Prince
Street Bridge as part of this funding stream. Other development projects included walking and cycling
improvements alongside the A4 Bath Bridges, extension of the secure cycle parking programme and two
new school streets.

With reference to the Prince Street Bridge, delivery was now proposed for March 2028 (previously 2027).
The change allows further time to mitigate concerns about implementation, particularly in relation to
nearby roadworks. Further modelling will be undertaken as part of the scheme development.

The Members asked questions and commented as follows:

 The Chair highlighted that the closure designs for Prince Street Bridge were not irreversible and
could be used flexibly in response to unplanned events on the network. The bridge cannot
support HGVs or buses, so could not form part of a full diversionary route.
 Members welcomed the consideration of improvements to the Bath Road corridor for pedestrians
and cyclists. It was suggested that the scheme should explore reallocating road space along the
existing road/ bridges. The project would consider the wider context of Temple Meads, no.2 bus
corridor and Temple Quay.
 Prince Street Bridge is primarily used by pedestrians. Although vehicular traffic is low, diverted
traffic may still have an impact
 It was clarified that the Cumberland Road Bus Gate had been introduced to support public
transport improvements and as a requirement of the Clean Air Zone. The bus gate itself did not
affect the westbound journeys to Spike Island. Further modelling will assess the impact of the
Prince Street Bridge closure, including cumulative effects on Spike Island.
 Future designs changes to Prince Street Bridge would be presented to a Policy Committee Chairs
Briefing inviting the Policy Committee members and Ward Councillors. The Chair could then
decide that it should return to the full Committee if appropriate. (ACTION)
 It was suggested that key risks should be presented earlier in future reports for clarity.
 It was suggested that improvements to the Bascule Bridge appeared to have reduced reliance on
Prince Street Bridge. The longevity and resilience of the Bascule Bridge should be factored into
plans.

Page 14
policycommittees@bristol.gov.uk

 Members raised concerns about the geographical spread of active travel investment and it was
considered that current scoring tended to favour central areas with higher footfall. It was hoped
that the Movement Plan would help identify any gaps in the current network, especially in
economically disadvantaged areas.
 Pavements should be considered part of active travel network. Poor pavement conditions can be
just as limiting as other barriers to walking.

Following the discussion, the report recommendations were moved by Councillor Plowden and seconded
by Councillor Rippington.

On being put to the vote the recommendations were CARRIED unanimously. It was APPROVED that;

That the Committee for Transport and Connectivity
1. Approved the acceptance of the next tranches of the Active Travel Fund of £2,286,807 for Bristol
which will fund active travel project delivery and development in Bristol until the funding deadline
of March 2027.
2. Endorsed the specific schemes or initiatives that will be funded through this allocation which are:
Prince Street Bridge design and delivery, A4 Bath Bridges walking and cycling development project,
delivery of two School Streets, delivery of secure cycle storage, and behaviour change initiatives.

Contractual:
3. Authorised the Executive Director Growth and Regeneration in consultation with the Chair of
Transport and Connectivity Committee to take all steps required to spend the funding to deliver
active travel projects, including procuring and awarding contracts above the key decision
threshold, in-line with the maximum budget envelopes and as outlined in this report.
4. Authorised the Executive Director Growth and Regeneration to invoke any subsequent
extensions/variations specifically defined in the contract(s) being awarded, up to the maximum
budget envelope outlined in this report.

9 Avon Fixed Bridge

The Committee considered a report that sought approval of spend to assess the design and works
required to keep Avon Fixed Bridge safely operational, ensuring the Authority is discharging its statutory
duty in this matter.

It was noted that this was a key decision affecting 2 or more wards.

The Head of Highways introduced and outlined the report.

In response to Member questions, officers confirmed that the Avon Fixed Bridge had been constructed in
the 1960s using methods that would not meet modern day standards. Any future design would include
future proofing to accommodate changing vehicles and pressures.

Page 15
policycommittees@bristol.gov.uk

The proposed structural assessment would seek to establish the current condition of the bridge and
inform future planning. It was expected to take 6-12 months to complete and would be designed so that,
if critical repairs are identified, the same scaffolding erected for assessment can be utilised for repairs.
Solutions such as those used on Hammersmith Bridge are being reviewed as potential methods for
intervention.

The bridge is an asset for which Bristol City Council is responsible for repair and maintenance. If
additional funding is required, officers would explore regional and national grant opportunities.

Plans for the bridge will be considered in the context of active travel aspirations and the Western Harbour
development. Officers are working closely with the Western Harbour team to ensure any proposals are
coordinated and complimentary.

Following the discussion, the report recommendations were moved by Councillor Plowden and seconded
by Councillor Morris.

On being put to the vote the recommendations were CARRIED unanimously. It was APPROVED that:

That the Committee for Transport and Connectivity:

1. Approved the spend of £500,000 to Assess the bridge and design the works required to keep Avon
Fixed Bridge safely operational, ensuring the Authority is discharging its statutory duty in this
matter. Refer to evidence base for full details.
2. Approved the proposal to undertake a full category 3 structural assessment of the Avon Fixed
Bridge as outlined in this report.
3. Authorised the Executive Director Growth and Regeneration in consultation with the Chair of
Transport and Connectivity Committee to take all steps required to the spend the funding to
undertake the full category 3 structural assessment for the Avon Fixed Bridge and designing the
subsequent required works, including procuring and awarding contracts which may be over the key
decision threshold, as outlined in this report.

into the City Region Sustainable Transport Settlement (CRSTS) overprogramming programme as

11 Q1 Corporate Risk Report 2025/26

The Committee considered the report which provided an update on current significant strategic risks,
relevant to the Transport and Connectivity Committee, to achieving the Council’s objectives as set out in
the Corporate Strategy 2025 – 2030 and summarising progress in managing the risks and actions being
taken as at quarter 1 2025-26.

It was noted that this was a report for information.

The Director Economy of Place introduced and outlined the report to the end of quarter 1. The two main
risks presented were previously received in April and July 2025. It was highlighted that some of the risk
ratings were likely to be adjusted in future reports, specifically in reference to the condition of the
Harbour Assets and CRSTS future planning.

Risks were reviewed on a monthly basis with the highest rated risks presented to senior leadership and
the Policy Committee. Officers agreed to consider how to provide a picture of the lower rated risks and
risks managed sufficiently to make the Committee aware.

With reference to CRR58:
 Specifically in respect of the harbour walls, it was confirmed that the risk had been adjusted from
‘likely’ to ‘almost certain’. The risk tolerance rating was also further explained.
 It was suggested that the risk could be broken down into separate elements within ‘Highway,
Traffic, Parking and Docks infrastructure assets’ as the bracket spanned a wide range of assets
with differing risk.
 Officers confirmed that information regarding risk on carriageways, such as condition survey data
that informs the road maintenance program, can be found online at webpage Department for
Transport funding and report Local highways maintenance transparency report. More detail was
due to be released at the end of October.

Page 17
policycommittees@bristol.gov.uk

With reference to CRR43:
 A report would be presented to the next meeting of this Policy Committee to update on the likely
impacts of constructing the City Region Sustainable Transport Settlement (CRSTS) schemes.
 The Chair continues to challenge directors of the authorities as well as companies to collaborate
effectively in managing the transport network during disruption periods. Emphasis was placed on
communication with bus passengers and the wider travelling public.

The Transport and Connectivity Committee noted the report and the report they received at the 10 July
meeting.

12 Policy Committee Work Programme Setting - Outcome Report

The Committee considered the report which noted the outcomes of the six monthly work programme
setting workshop held in June 2025. The report was presented for information with the following
discussion points noted:

The Committee discussed the potential for insourcing elements of the highways maintenance contract. It
was suggested that the procurement contract does not preclude a narrow cohort of work being
undertaken in house if required and ‘white lining’ was highlighted as a possible area that could benefit
from in house provision. The Committee requested that this topic remain under consideration, with a
future discussion to consider the pros and cons.

The Transforming Cities Region Forward Plan, now including maintenance, was highlighted as an
important document to help understand how schemes interact and align with each other.

An options paper on municipal bus companies would be provided to the next workshopping session
subject to officer resource.

The Committee for Transport and Connectivity noted the work programme update.

Meeting ended at 7.30 pm

Agenda Item 7

Non-key Decision Committee Report

PURPOSE: For information

COMMITTEE: Transport and Connectivity Committee

DATE: 23 October 2025

TITLE: Management and Mitigation of CRSTS Scheme Delivery
Officer presenting the report: Shaun Taylor. Job title: Head of Highways
Committee Chair: Cllr Ed Plowden

Executive Director lead: John Smith: Executive Director for Growth & Regeneration

Purpose of Report:
To update on the likely short-term impacts of constructing the City Region Sustainable Transport
Settlement (CRSTS) schemes and the mitigation measures being put in place to minimise disruption. The
deadline for CRSTS schemes is end of March 2027 but we have recently been informed that the
Department for Transport (DfT) would permit some limited construction past this date and up to March
2028, although their expectation is still that most funding must be spent by March 2027.
Evidence Base / Context
Between 2014 and 2024, Bristol saw an 11.4% population increase and is projected to exceed half a million
residents by 2030. To support this growth Bristol is investing in schools, housing, health and transport
infrastructure. The One City Plan integrates population growth with its vision to be carbon neutral by 2030
as it recognises that Bristol must overcome the challenge of limited road space by radically rethinking how
people and goods move around the city. Modal shift is essential to accommodate all these new journeys
and Bristol University undertook a study that showed that a shift to 55% active travel, 25% public transport
and 20% car use is required.

Bristol is making significant investments to expand sustainable transport networks and accelerate the
rollout of electric vehicle infrastructure. The CRSTS is part of this work and is delivering a transformational
investment in Bristol to support sustainable travel, reduce carbon emissions and improve connectivity. The
projects focus on improving main transport corridors and works in the city centre to improve bus reliability
and make walking and cycling a natural choice for short and medium journeys. As part of this investment
funding has been allocated for maintenance and infrastructure repairs to carriageways, footways and
includes lighting and traffic signals upgrades to help keep Bristol moving.

Several schemes must be delivered on these key routes into the city and throughout the central area as
part of CRSTS by March 2028. Owing to the funding conditions the construction periods of some schemes
will be constructed at the same time, but these will be carefully selected to ensure the impact on each
other is minimal. On completion, capacity for general traffic will have been reduced in some areas to make
way for sustainable modes. Until modal shift occurs at a level to match the reduced capacity congestion is
possible. The works themselves will reduce capacity and traffic levels may reduce through modal shift
throughout delivery which has been seen on previous major schemes such as Greater Bristol Bus Network

1
Version January 2024 Page 19
OFFICIAL
(GBBN) and Metrobus.

The Highway Authority oversees roadworks, utility companies, district heat network, developments,
incidents and highway projects as part of its routine operations. While the scale of investment and activity
planned for the coming years is unprecedented, the authority has proactively developed and implemented
mitigation measures to help minimise disruption. The attached presentation outlines these measures and
explains key principles guiding this work, including careful coordination and scheduling of each project to
reduce their collective impact on the wider network.

This approach combines several strategies: making full use of existing resources like the Operations Centre,
which serves as a hub for traffic monitoring and a control room for major incidents, realigning project
schedules to prevent key schemes from overlapping, and postponing projects such as Bond Street (to start
in early 2027 and complete by March 2028) where necessary to avoid conflicts. Additionally, there is a
proactive campaign to encourage public transport and active travel, including fare promotions, expanded
Park & Ride and other bus services, and the introduction of new routes. Discussions will also take place with
WECA to seek funding for additional roles in Bristol’s Operations Centre such as First bus and WECA
representatives.

The Council is also launching the ‘Bristol on the Move’ website www.bristolonthemove.com to provide up
to date information about the CRSTS transport corridor projects, to keep people informed of changes
during construction. The site will provide links for traffic information and has advice and support on how to
switch travel modes.

Officer Recommendations:
That the Transport and Connectivity committee:
1. note this report and accept that Bristol is entering a period of unprecedented works that may cause
delays and congestion to support the growth of the city and that work is underway to mitigate the
impact. Refer to the slide pack to see how the works will be mitigated.
2. note that the CRSTS schemes follow the Bristol transport policy and the themes identified in the
One City Plan to promote and deliver sustainable transport to reduce the impact of positive city
growth.

Corporate Strategy alignment:
Priority 4: Making it easier, greener and safer to travel into and around Bristol

1. Work with partners to improve accessible, reliable, affordable and sustainable transport choices.
2. Focus on areas with less reliable transport options to connect people and opportunities within and
beyond the city.
3. Maintain essential city infrastructure for safety, longevity and accessibility.

City Benefits:
1. Deliver Bristol’s transport policy
2. Provide improved sustainable transport measures for Bristol citizens
3. Ensure possible short and medium term impacts of delivering schemes are understood
4. Encourage modal shift for those that are able to use sustainable modes

Appendix A

Management and Mitigation of CRSTS Scheme Delivery:

Prior to March 2028, Bristol will deliver a range of transformative projects schemes such as the A4
Portway, A4 Bath Road, A37/A4018 Corridor, Redcliffe Roundabout, Bedminster Bridges, Bond Street /
Newfoundland Circus, Union Street/Rupert Street as well as several smaller interventions. The primary
focus of these schemes is to deliver a more sustainable, accessible, and vibrant city by prioritising
improvements for buses, cyclists, and pedestrians, as well as enhancing the overall public realm. While
construction is possible up to March 2028 due to a change of position from the DfT, they have also
stated that they still expect most expenditure to happen by March 2027 so much of our delivery plan
will not change. We are however proposing to delay the Bond St scheme to mitigate traffic impacts.

By introducing dedicated bus lanes and gates, high-quality cycle infrastructure, and better pedestrian
facilities, these schemes will make sustainable travel easier and more attractive for everyone. All these
improvements are closely aligned with national, regional, and local transport policies, as well as
Bristol’s One City Plan, supporting our shared vision for a greener, healthier and more connected city.
Bristol’s road network is a valuable and limited resource. By reallocating road space to support
sustainable and high-capacity modes such as walking, cycling and public transport we can create a
more efficient, accessible and healthier city for everyone. Any reallocation to sustainable modes will
require a reduction to other general modes such as the car, van and other freight. This reduction in
general capacity relies on those that are able, to switch to sustainable modes of transport through
modal shift, which in turn will free up road space for those that require motorised transport, such as
disabled people and many delivery drivers and tradespeople.

As Bristol’s population continues to grow, promoting modal shift early will help manage future demand
on our transport network, reducing the number of projected vehicles joining the road. As such
investing in sustainable transport infrastructure is essential to enable this modal shift, without which
congestion will only continue to worsen.

Several schemes currently being developed for delivery are designed to support and encourage modal
shift to achieve the full benefits modelled. Without this modal shift the highway network will be over
capacity leading to congestion and delays at levels greater than are currently seen.

Bus patronage has been steadily increasing except for the pandemic years; and this positive trend
demonstrates growing demand for sustainable travel. However, private car and light goods vehicle
numbers have also returned to pre-pandemic levels, even with changes in office use. By delivering
these new schemes, there is a real opportunity to inspire and facilitate further modal shift.

Experience from schemes within Bristol and around the world shows that mode shift does occur when
road space is rebalanced and traffic capacity is reduced. The closure and transformation of Queens
Square to traffic over 30 years ago and the more recent closure of Bristol Bridge both indicate that
reallocating road space can reduce traffic without causing long term congestion. All the interventions
planned have been appraised using Department for Transport guidance and collectively show that they
can have a positive impact, and may even encourage more people to visit the City Centre.
1
Page 22
OFFICIAL
It is important to recognise that achieving the necessary modal shift is key to realising the full benefits
of the CRSTS schemes. If modal shift does not occur as planned, it is possible that delays will be
experienced around the city on completion of the CRSTS schemes owing to reduction in general traffic
capacity. However, these delays are expected to ease as more people choose sustainable travel options
or discover alternative routes.

Changes made to the City Centre and Temple Circus in recent years, with similar changes planned for
Bedminster Bridges, reduce the impacts of incidents as they reduce the likelihood of grid lock events
and enable better predictability of the network. By introducing measures such as banned turns and bus
gates, we can prioritise sustainable transport and improve overall network reliability.

While these restrictions reduce the number of alternate routes available during incidents or planned
works, schemes are being thoughtfully designed with flexibility in mind. Where possible, schemes are
being designed with the ability to reinstate certain movements should they be required to facilitate
other necessary works such as maintenance to Utility apparatus. This approach ensures that we
continue to balance the benefits of a more sustainable, predictable network with the need for
resilience and adaptability.

With limited time left to deliver the CRSTS programme and most schemes likely to take 12-18 months
to deliver, there may be overlap in the implementation of the schemes. Work is underway to identify
which schemes can be carried out concurrently and which must be done consecutively to ensure Bristol
can still thrive during this unprecedented period of change. The reasons for the end of programme
delivery are primarily owing to design time, business case sign offs and the approvals processes. Every
effort will be made to ensure schemes run to programme and suitable traffic management methods
are used to reduce the impacts of the works.

It should also be noted that other works will be going on in this time and beyond, notably, Utility works
and district heating. Works are planned to repair the M32 viaduct and are expected to have substantial
impacts on capacity of the M32 and surrounding areas for several years. While these works are not
expected to impact capacity until 2028, they will need to be managed with a view to facilitating mode
shift as part of the works. The ongoing capacity restrictions may assist with some of the mode shift
required across the city if well managed and BCC is engaging proactively with National Highways to
manage the potential impact, which will be felt citywide and acutely in East Bristol.

Risks or sensitivities identified:

Criticism is likely to be directed at the Authority through the period of delivery and the impacts it will
have on Bristol PLC throughout the works. Similar situations have occurred previously with major
transport schemes such as the Greater Bristol Bus Network and Metrobus programmes although not
on this scale. A robust response to these challenges has been developed making it clear that Bristol’s
transport policy is well established and the way to avoid delays and congestion is through modal shift
and embracing the investment in sustainable transport.

The Traffic Management Act 2005 (part 2) requires the Traffic Manager (Shaun Taylor as per the proper
officers list Part 3 of the constitution) to secure the expeditious movement of traffic on the Authority’s
road network and facilitate the expeditious movement of traffic on the road networks of other
2
Page 23
OFFICIAL
Authorities so far as may be reasonably practicable having regard to their other obligations, policies
and objectives. Further, the Traffic Manager is also required to identify things, including future
occurrences, which are causing, or have the potential to cause, road congestion or other disruption to
the movement of traffic on their road network and consider possible action that could be taken in
response to, or in anticipation of, anything so identified.

As part of this responsibility, elements of these schemes have been carefully assessed for their
potential to impact traffic flow to both Bristol’s road network and National Highways network. While
some temporary congestion or disruption may occur until such times as traffic flows are reduced
through modal shift, these schemes are designed to support long-term improvements in network
efficiency and reliability by encouraging more sustainable travel choices.

Managing Cumulative Impacts
Concerns have been raised about the cumulative impacts of multiple road works on traffic flows.
However, the city centre is primarily a destination with limited through trips and the schemes are
generally separated so cumulative impacts are limited to adjacent projects.

The following summarises the approach for each of the 6 major city centre projects:

Temple Way: First to start; limited impacts expected due to available space and capacity.
Triangle/Park Street: Localised impacts from the Triangle scheme; parking loss during
construction, but road closures are unlikely. Early closure of Park Street helps mitigate
impacts.

Page 31
Bedminster Bridges: impacts expected; banning turns early and existing bus lanes will help
manage traffic.
Redcliff Roundabout: Ample space available; some impacts on bus services and traffic
delays.
Union Street: Likely to affect bus services and may result in the loss of some bus lanes, with
some impact on general traffic.
Bond Street: Some impact expected. Works are planned to start later (March 2027) to
further reduce overlap.

Careful planning and separation of schemes mean that citywide cumulative impacts are limited,
with most disruption managed locally and mitigated through targeted measures.

Agenda Item 8

Committee Report

KEY DECISION OR FOR INFORMATION: Key decision over £500k

COMMITTEE: Transport and Connectivity Committee

DATE: 23 October 2025

TITLE: Bristol Resident Parking Scheme Expansion Programme

Ward(s): all

Officer presenting the report: Adam Crowther Job title: Head of City Transport

Committee Chair: Cllr Plowden

Executive Director lead: John Smith: Executive Director for Growth & Regeneration

Proposal origin: Committee Chair

Purpose of report

This paper sets out the case for expanding Bristol City Council’s (BCC) Resident Parking
Schemes (RPS) programme (formerly Resident Parking Zones). It outlines how the
implementation of RPS can support BCC in better managing parking to prioritise more
sustainable uses of the kerbside, reduce private vehicle trips, and enhance safety and
environmental quality across all wards. A costed delivery plan underpins the report, providing
the basis for drawing down funding from relevant funding streams.

Executive summary

RPS expansion is being proposed now to help tackle increasing pressure on parking, support
cleaner air, and make neighbourhoods safer and fairer for everyone. This will in turn make
parking easier and the proposed expansion of the RPS programme directly supports Bristol’s
wider strategic goals, including the ambitions set out in the One City Plan.

By helping to reduce car use, lower traffic volumes, and make it easier for people to choose
walking, cycling, or public transport, the scheme plays an important role in the city’s
aspirations to become carbon neutral by 2030. It also supports the aims of reducing carbon
emissions by cutting down on commuter traffic and improving air quality in residential areas.

Background

In November 2007, Bristol City Council (BCC) agreed in principle to introduce RPS around the
city centre to address commuter parking pressures. Consultations began in early 2008,
involving neighbourhood panels and wider public engagement to identify early adopter zones
by mid-2008. Initial implementation was planned for late spring 2009, with a wider rollout
targeted for completion by the end of 2012.

RPS expansion – October 2025 Page 42
OFFICIAL
OFFICIAL
In March 2013, plans were announced for an expanded RPS programme, which was
implemented by spring 2014 in the following areas (see figure 1 below):

• Bedminster East
• Bower Ashton
• Clifton East
• Clifton Village
• Clifton Wood and Hotwells
• Cotham
• Cotham North

• Redcliffe

• St Pauls

blue areas in map below):

• St Andrews
• St Werburgh’s
• Ashley Down
• Bishopston
• Windmill Hill
• Ashton
• Totterdown
• Bedminster West

OFFICIAL
OFFICIAL
However, following the initial rollout completed in 2014, the expansion of new RPSs was
halted in 2016.

RPS expansion – October 2025 Page 44
OFFICIAL
OFFICIAL
The roll out of RPS followed the completion of the Greater Bristol Bus Network and the Cycling
City projects and since 2013 bus patronage has increased by 45%. Walking and cycling rates
have also been increasing in Bristol which has the highest mode share for walking and cycling
trips of all the core cities in the UK. Reviews were undertaken six months after implementation,
with an opportunity to tweak the details but no significant calls to remove them. The RPS
review commenced in early 2017 and was completed by the end of 2018, covering the
2017/18 period.

Policy Context

In 2023, BCC commissioned a review of RPS effectiveness to better align the scheme with
wider ‘Liveable Neighbourhoods’ objectives. These include promoting more sustainable uses
of kerbside space and reducing private vehicle use. Liveable Neighbourhood boundaries
provide a logical framework for RPS implementation, offering operational alignment and
delivering shared outcomes.

A suite of new policies are currently in development to help meet the goals set out in the One
City Plan, including a:

• Transport Movement Plan
• Kerbside & Parking Strategy
• Road Safety Strategy
• Investigating a Workplace Parking Levy

On 24 October 2024, the Transport and Connectivity (T&C) Policy Committee approved the
Terms of Reference for a Kerbside and Parking Task and Finish Group, with the following
objectives identified for further development:

• Safer and more accessible streets
• Healthier streets
• Accessible streets
• Resilient streets
• Welcoming streets

RPS play a key role in delivering these objectives by reducing traffic volumes, cutting vehicle
emissions, and improving air quality, all of which bring about significant health benefits for the
public. Notably, junctions are among the most common sites for collisions; the introduction of
double yellow lines at these points improves visibility for both drivers and pedestrians, thereby
reducing road danger. Issues also arise when there are no restrictions in place to protect road
corners which can reduce sight lines and create issues for turning vehicles. RPS act as a
mechanism for accelerating mitigations to these issues by managing parking e.g. introducing
double yellows at corners.

The Task & Finish Group agreed that RPS would not be in its scope and recommended that a
report is brought to committee.

In addition, parking controls support environmental goals by linking permit costs to vehicle
emissions. Higher-emission vehicles are charged more, providing an incentive for residents to

RPS expansion – October 2025 Page 45
OFFICIAL
OFFICIAL
switch to cleaner, low-emission models. Evidence suggests that areas with parking controls
tend to have higher ownership of low-emission vehicles and lower overall car ownership, which
correlates with reduced car usage.

Challenges

While RPS have been part of Bristol’s transport strategy for over a decade and are now a well-
established feature of the city, challenges remain. Each new RPS must be carefully designed
to reflect the needs of its local area, particularly in locations impacted by large venues such as
sports stadia or municipal facilities like swimming pools. Thorough public consultation will be
essential to ensure schemes are appropriately tailored.

There is also a growing need to review the current operation of RPSs across Bristol. This may
include updates to pricing structures, permit criteria, and the overall effectiveness of existing
schemes to ensure they remain fit for purpose and aligned with current policy goals. In
addition, there may be a need to split existing RPS areas into smaller sections to reflect the
local operational requirements.

Consultation

Engagement for new RPS will utilise a variety of methods to ensure meaningful community
involvement. These may include drop-in events, surveys, workshops, and online platforms to
gather a broad range of feedback. For example, engagement on the Totterdown Square RPS
could follow a co-design approach piloted in the East Bristol Liveable Neighbourhood project,
where early community feedback was actively used to shape design options. This collaborative
model ensures that local residents and businesses have a genuine influence on scheme
development, helping to build trust and create solutions tailored to local needs.

Each new RPS that is introduced will be subject to intermittent operational reviews to assess
the impact of the schemes and to recommend any changes that may be required.

Prioritisation

A committee report was submitted to the 15 May 2025 Transport and Connectivity Committee
to update on current proposals for new RPS schemes and to consider how additional RPS
schemes might be brought forward. The following criteria was agreed to enable prioritisation of
schemes:

a. Presence of Liveable Neighbourhood scheme
b. Proximity to existing RPS
c. Proximity to major trip generators
d. Proximity to city centre
e. Local demand
f. Potential strategic impact

Officers have since carried out a high level assessment along wider programme alignment to
determine the first phases of the RPS expansion which are listed in the delivery plans below.
We will also allow for an assessment of new RPS schemes that are identified via public
RPS expansion – October 2025 Page 46
OFFICIAL
OFFICIAL
feedback, ward councillors and the council’s ‘street improvement tool, which will be brought to
committee in 26/27 to obtain authorisation to begin development work in 2027/28.

Finance

RPS that are initially funded through a Clean Air Zone (CAZ) allocation are expected to
become financially self-sustaining over time and have the potential to generate a surplus of
between £100,000 and £300,000 per year per RPS. Schemes include Pay & Display elements
and will generate some income that will cover the costs of delivering and operating the
schemes. Any surplus will go back into transport schemes and revenue as well as repaying the
original CAZ investment.

The price of the permit for a property’s first car is set to cover the operational costs of running
an RPS, with the surplus being derived in the main from Pay & Display income with a
contribution also coming from permit costs for 2nd and 3rd car permits.

It is expected that the capital and operational costs for a typical RPS will be paid back within 2-
4 years depending on the amount of permits, pay and display bays and available kerbside
space. This funding model ensures that while the initial costs are covered by CAZ and then
repaid, the schemes ultimately deliver a return to the programme and support its long-term
sustainability.

Any income from the schemes should fund the costs of implementing and managing the zones
with surplus income from pay and display elements to be ringfenced for transport investment.
Work is currently under way through a proposed Transport Investment Plan (due to come to
the committee in Spring 2026) to determine levels of income from various transport
sources,how these might vary over time and then how to manage that income to deliver wider
strategic transport objectives.

Any future investment that funds/part funds the implementation of an/any RPS will reduce the
Clean Air Zone (CAZ) allocation listed within the below delivery plans. Additional kerbside
interventions will also be explored by utilising the space within RPS areas which could include
incorporating additional electric vehicle charging points and car club bays.

£700,000 has been allocated for RPS in Windmill Hill, Bedminster, and Totterdown through
Section 106 developer contributions. This also acts as match-funding for the South Bristol
Liveable Neighbourhoods programme supported by City Regional Sustainable Transport
Settlement (CRSTS).

Delivery Plan

The below delivery plans were developed based on the criteria identified at the 15 May
Transport and Connectivity Committee. The development work for the South Bristol Liveable
Neighbourhood forms the basis of the first phase of RPS rollout as the project area aligns with
the previously published RPS areas in the outer ring as highlighted in blue in figure 1 (Ashton,
Bedminster West, Windmill Hill, Totterdown).

The second phase focuses on completing the outer ring in the north of the city in Ashley
Down, St Andrews and Bishopston. St Werburghs has been removed from high priority based
on councillor and resident feedback and replaced with a scheme to the east of the existing
Easton & St Phillips RPS which has been named Upper Easton. This is likely to cover the area

RPS expansion – October 2025 Page 47
OFFICIAL
OFFICIAL
around Stapleton Road but the proposed RPS boundaries will need to be agreed with local
stakeholders and the ward councillors before going out to further consultation

Proposals for further RPSs that are not currently identified by name in the below delivery plans
will come forward through applying the agreed prioritisation criteria and through avenues such
as councillor feedback and parking pressures through new developments. There is capacity to
initiate their development in 2027/28, but as the volume is not yet known costs have not yet
been modelled beyond this financial year.

The costs for implementing the RPS identified in the below delivery plans are estimates based
on bench marking from cities across the UK. However, the final costs are expected to vary
depending on the complexity and operational requirements of each RPS.

The council would look to review new RPS following a minimum of 12 months of the scheme
being fully operational

Parking charges on the downs

As laid out in a paper on RPS at the 2024 December Transport and Connectivity Committee
there are concerns that parking on the Downs is abused (particularly the 5 hour limited waiting
bays) resulting in limited turnover of spaces. There are also problems with van dwellers
parking all day around the Downs, although it should be noted that static caravans/unhitched
trailers cannot be issued a Penalty Charge Notice (PCN) as they are not registered vehicles.

Enforcement of the restrictions is challenging given the resources required to cover the whole
area and the nature of the restrictions which gives a very limited window of opportunity in
which to operate. The limited waiting restrictions apply Monday to Friday only, providing no
turnover of spaces on busy weekends. In addition, the 5-hour limit was agreed due to the
proximity to the Zoo and to reflect their desire for restrictions to accommodate Zoo visitors.

With the closure of the Zoo, it would be appropriate to review the existing restrictions and
develop an RPS in Henleaze and Westbury, and the detailed proposals will be subject to
consultation

Risks

There are a number of identified risks associated with the proposed expansion of the Resident
Parking Scheme (RPS). One key risk is the potential for local opposition in some areas; whilst
some residents may object to the principle, many will wish to ensure the detailed design is
appropriate. To mitigate this, a phased implementation approach allows for detailed
engagement with affected communities and refinement of proposals based on local feedback.
Where appropriate, consultation will be aligned with other related programmes, such as
Liveable Neighbourhoods, to ensure a coordinated and transparent approach.

A further risk relates to the Council’s capacity to enforce expanded RPS areas effectively. This
will be mitigated through appropriate resourcing and operational planning, supported by
revenue generated from permit income. This approach is intended to ensure that enforcement
remains effective and proportionate, without placing additional strain on existing services.

Another risk is that additional neighbourhoods will then become priorities due to becoming
proximate to one of the newly created RPS. This is mitigated by the delivery programme
anticipating that capacity will be needed for rolling additional zones.

Officer Recommendations:

1.That the Transport and Connectivity Committee approves the undertaking of development
and implementation work for the RPS areas identified in the delivery plans subject to the
necessary consultation and approvals.

2. That the Transport and Connectivity Committee approves the total allocation of £7,502,000
for the RPS expansion programme of which £4,999,000 will be allocated from CAZ for
development work and implementation of the areas identified in the delivery plans (subject to
the necessary consultation and approvals), with a presumption that this money will be
reimbursed to the CAZ funds.

3. Authorises the Executive Director Growth and Regeneration in consultation with the Chair of
the Transport and Connectivity Committee to award the project, in-line with the procurement
routes and maximum budget envelopes outlined in this report.

4.That the Executive Director Growth and Regeneration is authorised to progress scheme
development, including public consultation and permit system implementation consistent with
existing RPS.

transport, improving environmental quality, enhancing road safety, and promoting equitable
access to neighbourhood spaces.

Professional comments section:

1. Finance Advice: This paper sets out the case for expanding Bristol City Council’s (BCC)
Resident Parking Schemes (RPS) programme (formerly Resident Parking Zones). This
report is requesting the use of £7.5 million across financial years 25/26, 26/27 and 27/28 for
the development and implementation of several resident parking schemes across Bristol.

The report proposes the use of £1.7 million of CRSTS funding from the South Bristol Liveable
Neighbourhood scheme, £0.8 million in section 106 receipts and £5 million from CAZ income.
Each of these estimates is inclusive of 10% of contingency. The Scheme is expected to
become financially self-sustaining over time and potentially generate a surplus of between
£100,000 and £300,000 per year per RPS, with revenue from the schemes reinvested to
repay the original CAZ investment as well as potentially rolling out future RPS and/or
transport improvements across the city.

Finance recognises a risk that the combined authority doesn’t approve the South Bristol
Liveable Neighbourhood scheme and that the funding shortfall would need to be covered

RPS expansion – October 2025 Page 52
OFFICIAL
OFFICIAL
using further CAZ funding. The section 106 receipts have been agreed with the section 106
team.
It is estimated by the team that the CAZ funding will be replenished in the medium term with
the additional income to be received from the resident parking schemes, however finance
has not seen calculations to quantify the timeframe for this replenishment. For the time being,
CAZ funding is available to be used for this scheme, however work is currently being done
to model CAZ income to be received over coming years. Clarity is also needed around
income utilisation within transport, once the scheme starts generating surplus income.

Finance Business Partner: Vivi Sargioti, Finance Business Partner – Growth &
Regeneration, 01 October 2025

2. Legal Advice:

Resident Parking Schemes are promoted under the Road Traffic Regulation Act 1984 (the
Act). Matters to be considered when determining what parking places are to be designated
include (but are not limited to) both the interests of traffic and those of the owners and
occupiers of adjoining property. In particular the Council shall have regard to:

(a)the need for maintaining the free movement of traffic;
(b)the need for maintaining reasonable access to premises; and
(c)the extent to which off-street parking accommodation is available in the neighbourhood or
the provision of such parking accommodation is likely to be encouraged there by the
designation of parking places under this section.

Appropriate surveys and public consultation should therefore be undertaken to inform the
scheme design to be promoted and to satisfy the various legislative considerations to be
taken into account when formalising proposals for a scheme.

The Act and accompanying regulations also set out the public consultation requirements
following the promotion of a scheme and prior to implementation, and these should be
followed.

Caselaw has clarified that the Act is not a revenue raising Act and, in deciding whether or
not to make an order that allows for parking charges for on-road parking, a local authority
should not have regard to the manner in which the legislation would permit any surplus
charges to be spent.

Legal Team Leader: Dawn Bodill – Specialist Solicitor. 26 September 2025.

Step 1: What do we want to do?

What is the purpose of the proposal?
The proposal seeks to introduce a Resident Parking Scheme (RPS) in targeted areas across Bristol such as
Bristol Ashton Vale , Bedminster , Malago Vale, Perrett Park & Totterdown East, Totterdown, Windmill
Hill to do the following. This outlines how the implementation of RPSs can support BCC in better
managing parking to prioritise more sustainable uses of the kerbside, reduce private vehicle trips, and
enhance safety and environmental quality across all wards.

RPS expansion is being proposed now to help tackle increasing pressure on parking, support cleaner air,
and make neighbourhoods safer and fairer for everyone.
The proposed expansion of the RPS programme directly supports Bristol’s wider strategic goals,
including the ambitions set out in the One City Plan.

By helping to reduce car use, lower traffic volumes, and make it easier for people to choose walking,
cycling, or public transport, the scheme plays an important role in the city’s aspirations to become
carbon neutral by 2030. It also supports the aims of reducing carbon emissions by cutting down on
commuter traffic and improving air quality in residential areas.

In 2023, BCC commissioned a review of RPS effectiveness to better align the scheme with wider ‘Liveable
Neighbourhoods’ objectives. These include promoting more sustainable uses of kerbside space and
reducing private vehicle use. Liveable Neighbourhood boundaries provide a logical framework for RPS
implementation, offering operational alignment and delivering shared outcomes.

Manage high parking demand

Reduce congestion and improve air quality

Enhance safety for all road users

Improve access for residents, particularly those with mobility needs

The scheme is aligned with Bristol City Council’s commitments under the Climate Emergency
Declaration, Transport Strategy, and Equality and Inclusion Strategy (2023–2027).

How have you involved communities and groups that could be affected?

meeting at Ashton Gate.

There has been perceptions survey around the South Bristol Liveable Neighbourhoods (SBLN) that links
with the RPS and this was sent to key stakeholders in early January 2024 with two reminders. These
responses were recorded. In terms of ambitions for the project, the below three categories were seen as
most important to respondents (either ‘essential’ or ‘high importance’):
• streets that are easy to cross
• good access and parking
• easy, convenient and safe to walk

The interactive map received 6,081 comments from 1,562 different users between 5 July and 30 October

have been reported to the relevant designing team. The points plotted on the map are as follows:

People categorised their comments into the following issues and are ranked in order of highest number

• Speeding traffic
• Problems with car parking

• Lack of crossings
• Personal safety/security concerns

How will engagement with stakeholders continue?
Ongoing dialogue through public meetings, online surveys, and stakeholder workshops in the areas
where RPS will be taking place. Engagement for these measures will utilise a variety of methods to
ensure meaningful community involvement. These may include drop-in events, surveys, workshops, and
online platforms to gather a broad range of feedback. For example, engagement on the Totterdown
Square RPS could follow a co-design approach piloted in the East Bristol Liveable Neighbourhood project,
where early community feedback was actively used to shape design options. This collaborative model
ensures that local residents and businesses have a genuine influence on scheme development, helping
to build trust and create solutions tailored to local needs. This EqIA will be updated throughout the life
of the project to reflect consultation feedback and outcomes of them.

The purpose of this Environmental Impact Assessment is to help you develop your proposal in a way that is
compliant with the council’s policies and supports the council’s strategic objectives under the One City Climate
Strategy, the One City Ecological Emergency Strategy and the latest Corporate Strategy.
This assessment should be started at the beginning of the project proposal process by someone with a good
knowledge of the project, the service area that will deliver it, and sufficient influence over the proposal to make
changes as needed.
It is good practice to take a team approach to completing the Environmental Impact Assessment. See further
guidance on completing this document. Please email environmental.performance@bristol.gov.uk early for advice
and feedback.

1.1 What are the aims and objectives/purpose of this proposal?
Briefly explain the purpose of the proposal and why it is needed. Please use plain English, avoiding jargon and
acronyms.
The aim of this proposal is to introduce or revise a Resident Parking Scheme (RPS) in selected areas of Bristol. The
RPS seeks to prioritise residential access to limited kerbbside parking, manage demand for space, reduce
commuter parking, encourage sustainable transport use, and improve local air quality. This supports Bristol’s aim
to transition to a net-zero carbon, car-light city while creating safer, healthier, and more accessible
neighbourhoods

enhancements or mitigation actions are listed, they should be assigned to staff and appropriately resourced.

An RPS in Bristol can bring substantial environmental benefits but must be managed carefully to avoid unintended
consequences (e.g. displacement of vehicles to boundary areas or increased traffic in neighbouring zones). Close
integration with active travel infrastructure and local transport planning is essential
