Agenda Item 9

Committee Report

KEY DECISION OR FOR INFORMATION: Key decision affects 2 or more wards

COMMITTEE: Transport and Connectivity Committee

DATE: 23 October 2025

TITLE: CAZ Exemption and Financial Support package

Ward(s): Hotwells & Harbourside
Central

Officer presenting the report: Adam Crowther Job title: Head of Strategic City Transport

Committee Chair: Councillor Ed Plowden

Executive Director lead: John Smith: Executive Director for Growth & Regeneration

Proposal origin: BCC Staff

Purpose of Report:

1. To seek approval of a time limited exemption package for eligible citizens with Clean Air
Zone (CAZ) non-compliant vehicles impacted by the introduction of a Bus Lane and Bus
Gate on Queens Road and Park Street as described in this report. The report will also be
highlighting the existing CAZ financial support scheme available to citizens impacted by the
installation of the Bus Lane and Bus Gate. The exemption would be initially limited to one
year from the Bus Lane and Bus Gate opening at the Northern end of Park St and be for
eligible citizens in the area (set out in Figure 1 below and Appendix A2) that would otherwise
be forced to drive through the CAZ and incur the charge or drive through the Bus Lane and
Bus Gate. This is referred to as the “preferred option” throughout this report. This exemption
will be kept under review after the first year.

Figure 1 below identifies the area between the Northern end of Park Street (where the Bus
Lane and Bus Gate are proposed) and the CAZ boundary which is to the south of Great
George Street. Citizens who need to access this area will, under the current proposals, be
required to pay a CAZ charge if they have a CAZ non-compliant vehicle.

Officers have considered how to mitigate this impact and as a result this Committee paper
sets out a proposal to offer an exemption through the Bus Lane and Bus Gate.

Figure 1:
Template approved March 25
Page 69
OFFICIAL
Evidence Base

1. The boundary of the CAZ is currently on Park Street to the south of the junction with Great
George Street (see Appendix A1). This means that anyone who needs to access the area
to the north of the boundary can currently do so from the top of Park Street via Queens Road,
thus avoiding paying the CAZ charge.

2. The consequence of the proposed Bus Lane and Bus Gate intervention (see Appendix A1)
as part of the City Region Sustainable Transport Settlement (CRSTS) Park
Street/Triangle/Queens Road proposals is that those citizens who need to access the area
will need to travel through the CAZ via St Georges Road, thus incurring a charge if they have
a CAZ non-compliant vehicle. The bus gate intervention is necessary for the removal of the
through traffic on Park Street which is a key requirement of the scheme to realise
improvements to bus journey times & reliability, active travel and public realm.

3. In response to this, the Council is proposing a package of initially time limited exemptions to
enable, as far as reasonably practicable, access through the Bus Lane and Bus Gate at the
top of Park Street for those eligible citizens who have a CAZ non-compliant vehicle. In
addition, citizens will be able to access the existing CAZ financial support scheme, more
details of which can be found on Bristol City Councils website: Clean Air Zone financial
support

4. The CAZ has been in operation since November 2022 and has achieved a 10% reduction in
NO2 emissions across Bristol and 13% lower in the CAZ. Almost 9 out of 10 journeys in the
CAZ are now compliant. The map in Figure 1 shows those properties that would be eligible
for the temporary Bus Lane and Bus Gate exemption if their vehicles are CAZ non-compliant.
The area consists of 438 properties. Of which 270 are residential and 168 are commercial.

Template approved March 25
Page 70
OFFICIAL
5. The following groups of Citizens will be eligible for an exemption through the Bus Lane and
Bus Gate if they have a CAZ non-compliant vehicle:
• Residents in the area defined in Figure 1
• Visitors to these residential properties (including delivery drivers and tradespeople)
where these are known to the resident
• Vehicles belonging to businesses operating from properties within the area defined
in Figure 1. These are operational vehicles insured for business use and will not
cover staff/commuters
• Informal carers
• Professional carers

These are the citizens (local residents and businesses) who are most significantly impacted by
the proposed scheme and is why we are advising the preferred option in this paper.

Other groups who may be impacted but will not be eligible for an exemption (but who will be
able to access the existing CAZ financial support scheme) include:
• Business owners/employees (commuters)
• Customers/delivery drivers/tradespeople visiting the area
• Visitors to St Georges Hall
• The Georgian House Museum
• Brandon Hill Park
• Blue Badge holders

There is no easy way to identify these citizens. These citizens have the option to pay the daily
CAZ fee or park outside of the CAZ; for example, West End car park which is 500m away from
St Georges Hall as well as the new disabled parking bays proposed on Triangle South which
are about 350m away from St Georges Hall.

6. Alternative options have been considered, and these are set out below. Appendix A3
provides a summary of the different users impacted by the “preferred option” and the
proposed exemption and financial support measures. The financial support measures are
under review to be expanded to offer more citizens the potential of taking advantage of the
financial support. The current threshold for being prioritised for financial support is £30,000
and is currently under review to keep in pace with inflation.

7. Citizens who live or work within the boundary identified in Figure 1 have been sent
communications prior to this Committee Meeting setting out the preferred option, along with
details of the package of exemptions and the financial support available. Subject to the
outcome of this Committee Paper, the next step is to consult on the draft traffic regulation
orders (TROs) in late quarter three/early quarter four of the 2025/2026 financial year.

8. The Equalities Impact Assessment (EQIA) is covered by the following EQIAs: Clean Air Zone
EQIA 2022 and the CRSTS A37/A4018 Work Package 3 - Park Street, Triangle and Queens
Road EQIA. These EQIA’s completed are shared as appendices of this committee paper,
and protected characteristics will be reviewed on a case-by-case basis in line with the
exemption application process. Blue Badge holders visiting the defined area in Figure 1, for
example visiting St George’s Hall, with CAZ non-compliant vehicles will have West End Car
Park as an alternative which is 500m away from St George’s Hall. In addition, new disabled
parking is proposed on Triangle South is about 350m away from St Georges Hall. Both West
Template approved March 25
Page 71
OFFICIAL
End Car Park and Triangle South are accessible without driving through the CAZ or the Bus
Lane or Bus Gate on the Northern end of Park St. Finally, there’s the financial support
measures that are on offer, for which all Blue Badge holders are prioritised.

Alternative options considered:

1. Do Nothing
An alternative option to do nothing was considered. As a result, non-compliant vehicles
would need to access the area via St George's Road and would incur the CAZ Charge.
The main reason this option was not pursued was the risk of the Council being subject to
legal challenge for forcing residents to pay the CAZ charge if they own non-compliant
cars. It would have the possibility of attracting negative publicity and complaints from
those who are disadvantaged by the scheme. The position would have to be maintained
even in the event of adverse publicity. More positively this option could encourage more
people to upgrade their vehicle to a compliant one and or encourage modal shift. This
could also deter drivers of non-compliant vehicles from visiting the area. This option was
not pursued further due to the preferred option being able to mitigate the negatives of this
option.
2. Temporary time limited Bus Lane and Bus Gate Exemption for compliant and
non-compliant CAZ
An option for a temporary time limited Bus Lane and Bus Gate exemption for compliant
and non-compliant CAZ vehicles to qualifying citizens in the affected catchment was
considered. It is recommended that this option isn’t pursued because of implications for
enforcement, the additional administrative work required, and setting a precedent.
3. Business CPZ (Central Parking Zone) permanent permit
An option to create a Business CPZ using the existing CPZ permits in the affected area
as the basis for grounds for exemption. This option was not pursued as it would create a
permanent CAZ exemption setting a new precedent where all CAZ exemptions have
previously been temporary. They have been temporary with the hope of giving time to
citizens to explore alternative modes of transport or switch over to CAZ compliant
vehicles. This option would create a long-term need of BCC resource to administer, and
monitor said exemptions as resident move in and out of the catchment.
4. CAZ Exemption
A CAZ exemption was considered to allow access through the CAZ for Non-compliant
vehicle in the catchment that would be forced to drive through the CAZ or Bus Gate. This
exemption would open access to all of the CAZ for non-compliant vehicles to ensure that
they are not financially penalised by the scheme. This option would undermine the CAZ
and cause detrimental impacts to the council’s obligations to achieve CAZ compliance in
the shortest time possible. Setting up this exemption would be difficult to implement for
all use cases and poses the question if we cannot offer exemption to all should it be
offered to any. There would be a large cost and time pressure added to the Parking Team
and would have long term effect on the team’s resources and is not recommended.
5. Bus Lane and Bus Gate Removal
Template approved March 25
Page 72
OFFICIAL
An option to remove the Bus Lane and Bus Gate from the top of Park Street was
considered. This option would have a significant impact on reducing the benefits of the
scheme. This would encourage the retention of the status quo allowing through traffic
down Park St, making this a non-viable option.
6. Bus Gate Relocation
An option to relocate the Bus Gate further down Park St to facilitate access for the citizens
with non-compliant vehicles affected by the current location of the Bus Gate. With the
objective of keeping the through traffic off Park St, this would still reduce the benefits of
the scheme by drawing more vehicles onto Park St. They would be given access to park,
load and have a negative impact on the buses and cyclists with the increased movement.
Therefore, this is not a recommended option.
7. Delay Implementation
An option to delay the implementation of the scheme until after the CAZ is
decommissioned was considered. This would result in the CRSTS funding of the scheme
no longer viable and no longer buildable within its allocated funding timeframe. The
benefits of the scheme would be delayed. The date of CAZ being decommissioned is
unknown, and it is unknown as to whether a different scheme will follow on from the
decommissioning of CAZ. Therefore, this is not a viable option to explore further.

Officer Recommendations: -

That the Committee for Transport and Connectivity:

1. Approves the preferred option of offering a time limited exemption, one year from date of
opening of the Bus Lane and Bus Gate, and financial support package for those eligible
citizens with non-compliant vehicles who are impacted by the introduction of a Bus Lane and
Bus Gate at the Northern end of Park Street.

Corporate Strategy alignment:

The proposal contained within this report is aimed at addressing an issue with the wider Park
Street/Triangle/Queens Road Project. This Project aligns with Corporate Strategy Priority 4
“Making it easier, greener and safer to travel into and around Bristol, and Priority 5 “Accelerating
climate action and nature recovery. By improving public transport infrastructure, the reliability of
bus services is improved, and opportunity to increase frequency and facilitate additional services
is safeguarded. Making improvements to walking and cycling infrastructure will encourage the
uptake of active travel. By considering the residents directly disadvantaged by the scheme and
offering them financial support and time to change their vehicles and modes of transport. The
project team are ensuring an equitable Bristol during the transition to fulfilling priority 4 and
priority 5.

City Benefits:

1. The proposal to retain the bus gate in its existing location will lock in the wider benefits of
the Park Street/Triangle/Queens Road scheme.
2. The package of exemptions will benefit those qualifying citizens who have a CAZ non-
compliant vehicle.

Professional comments section:

1. Finance Advice: This report is requesting a package of time-limited exemptions for the
proposed bus gate and lane on Park Street. As part of development of work package 3 of
the Stockwood to Henbury Corridor programme, it has been identified that the closing of Park
Street to cars would mean that some residents would be forced to enter the CAZ. As a result,
rather than change the scheme, this report is suggesting a one-year exemption from bus
gate / lane charges for residents. It is estimated that it will cost approximately £15,000 in
revenue costs for initial set up, approximately £5,000 of additional capital recharges for
system set up and consultancy costs and £7,500 for business support across the first 8-12
weeks. It is however noted that these are internal estimates based on staff time multiplied by
anticipated employee grade, alongside high-level estimates for external costs. No estimate
for reduction in CAZ income as a result of these exemptions is available, however it is not
expected to be material.
Template approved March 25
Page 74
OFFICIAL
The scheme avoids redesigning the infrastructure and mitigates legal and reputational risks.

Finance Business Partner: Vivi Sargioti, Finance Business Partner Growth &
Regeneration, 30 September 2025

2. Legal Advice: The Bus Gate proposals for Park Street will involve some local residents and
business’s having to access their destinations via the CAZ, with any non-compliant vehicles
incurring the CAZ charge where previously they could access their destinations without
entering the CAZ and incurring any charges.

Procedural fairness means that people directly affected by any administrative acts, decisions
or proceedings be given adequate notice of what is proposed so that they are able to make
representations on their behalf prior to any decision being taken.

Those directly affected by the preferred option have been given prior notice of what is being
proposed, together with details of any support that will be available through the transitional
period of one year after the Bus Gate is operational, with sufficient time and detail to enable
them to consider the preferred option and be able to make representations to committee if
they would like to do so.

The preferred option is an appropriate and proportionate approach allowing those affected
sufficient time and opportunity to acquire a CAZ compliant vehicle or to switch to alternative
modes of transport.

Legal Team Manager: Joanne Mansfield 28 August 2025

3. Implications on IT: I can see no implications on IT arising from this report.

IT Team Leader: Alex Simpson – Lead Enterprise Architect – 10 September 2025

team and if this additional work is unable to be absorbed within the current structure,
recruitment processes will be undertaken in line with our policies and procedures.

HR Partner: Niki Rodgers, HR Manager – 12 September 2025

Equality Impact Assessment [version 2.9]
Title: Clean Air Zone EqIA 2022
(post FBC update of “Improving Public Health: Bristol Clean Air Zone (CAZ) – Full business cases” EqIA)
☐ Policy ☐ Strategy ☐ Function ☐ Service ☒ New
☒ Other [please state]: Directive from Government ☐ Already exists / review ☐ Changing
Directorate: Growth & Regeneration Lead Officer name: Stephen Peacock
Service Area: Transport Lead Officer role: SRO

Step 1: What do we want to do?
The purpose of an Equality Impact Assessment is to assist decision makers in understanding the impact of proposals
as part of their duties under the Equality Act 2010. Detailed guidance to support completion can be found here
Equality Impact Assessments (EqIA) (sharepoint.com).
This assessment should be started at the beginning of the process by someone with a good knowledge of the
proposal and service area, and sufficient influence over the proposal. It is good practice to take a team approach to
completing the equality impact assessment. Please contact the Equality and Inclusion Team early for advice and
feedback.

1.1 What are the aims and objectives/purpose of this proposal?
Briefly explain the purpose of the proposal and why it is needed. Describe who it is aimed at and the intended aims /
outcomes. Where known also summarise the key actions you plan to undertake. Please use plain English, avoiding
jargon and acronyms. Equality Impact Assessments are viewed by a wide range of people including decision-makers
and the wider public.
Bristol City Council is wholly committed to enabling clean air in the city and to achieving this as quickly as possible,
whilst making sure that we put in place measures that will mitigate the impact on people with protected
characteristics (and/or from low income households).

Due to the scale of the issue in urban areas nationally, Her Majesty’s Government in 2017 formally directed 24
local authorities (including Bristol City Council) to submit plans for how they will achieve compliance to the legal
NO2 limits and how they would implement these plans by the end of March 2021.

Local authorities were therefore required to model various options for achieving clean air and to take forward the
option that delivers compliance against the NO2 legal limits within the shortest possible time, reduces exposure
the quickest and ensures that compliance is not just possible but likely.

From 1st July 2019 Bristol City Council carried out a six-week public consultation on two options for achieving NO2
compliance: option 1: a Medium CAZ C charging scheme with additional non -charging measures and mitigations;
option 2, a Small Area Diesel Car Ban with mitigations.

Following consultation and further technical analysis on both options, officers recommended that we progress
with a ‘Hybrid’ option, which merged options 1 and 2 described above. This was the option that achieved
compliance in line with our legal obligations.

Since the Council submitted the OBC in November 2019, further modelling and evaluation work has continued as
part of refinement work and there have been regular discussions between Bristol City Council and JAQU. The
main discussions being:

1. Technical questions relating to the Diesel Ban, in particular the area of the ban and assumptions around
behaviour change.
Page 80
2. Additional technical work that has been carried out that now shows a compliance date for the Hybrid
scheme of 2023.
3. Matters relating to the ability to deliver Diesel Ban as Government had not provided the appropriate
powers to Bristol City Council to implement a ban by passing the necessary secondary legislation and,
4. The Joint Air Quality Unit (JAQU) requiring further modelling work to ensure that other viable options are
being considered by the Council, should they be required. This included modelling a medium CAZ C with a
small CAZ D. Further analysis and sensitivity testing had been continuing on this option to ensure that the
option that is put forward in the Full Business Case fully complied with the Government Direction of
achieving compliance with the legal NO2 limits in the shortest possible time.

The further modelling work enabled good progress to be made on refining the data and had enabled us to move
forward positively. The most important aspect to note is that as a result of this additional refinement work, the
compliance year moved from 2025 to 2023. This will bring improved health to Bristol in a much shorter timescale
than originally expected.

On the 13th March 2020 a formal Direction was received from the Rt. Hon Rebecca Pow who wrote to Mayor Rees
stating the following:

To ensure delivery of NO2 compliance in the shortest possible time, I attach to this letter a Ministerial Direction
requiring Bristol City Council to:

• Implement a charging Clean Air Zone Class C with additional measures as soon as possible and at least in
time to bring forward compliance to 2023. I expect this to begin to be in place by 1 April 2020 at the
latest; and
• Submit to JAQU a Full Business Case by 18 September 2020 at the latest.

‘In the event that your preferred option of a medium CAZ C with additional measures, which could be either a
diesel ban or small area CAZ D, is either not deliverable or is shown through further modelling to not deliver
compliance in the shortest possible time, I expect you to pursue an alternative option for compliance, including a
medium size class D CAZ.’

COVID-19 impact

It is important to note that having received the Government Direction and letter from the Rt. Hon Rebecca Pow
MP on the 13th March 2020, the situation regarding COVID-19 escalated and further Government advice was
subsequently provided.

In continuing discussions with JAQU throughout this period, Bristol City Council continued to assess the situation
and monitor the impacts of COVID-19. During this time JAQU issued a statement from the Joint Heads of
Department stating that no Clean Air Zones will be implemented before January 2021 in recognition of the
challenges and unknown entities that the situation presented.

Despite the challenges that lockdown (which commenced on the 23 March 2020) presented, council officers made
significant progress in continuing with the additional required modelling and technical assessments as set out in
the letter from Minister Pow on the 13th March 2020. These were all submitted in line with the requirements
placed upon us.

Following the submission of the required information and subsequent discussions with JAQU officers, the Council
was issued with a new Direction on 20 August 2020 which required the Council to:

Implement the local plan for nitrogen dioxide (NO2) compliance, specifying a Medium Charging Clean Air Zone
Class C with small Charging Clean Air Zone Class D and additional measures, subject to provision of further
evidence to be submitted.

4—(1) The authority must take steps to implement the local plan for NO2 compliance for the areas for which it is
responsible.
(2) The authority must ensure that the local plan Page
for NO2 compliance
81 is implemented so that—
(a) compliance with the legal limit value for nitrogen dioxide is achieved in the shortest possible time, and by
2023 at the latest.
(b) exposure to levels above the legal limit for nitrogen dioxide are reduced as quickly as possible.

Duty to submit additional documentation

5.—(1) The authority must submit to the Secretary of State further options appraisal (including transport and air
quality modelling) by 4 December 2020 at the latest, in order to provide assurance that the local plan for
NO2 compliance will deliver compliance in the shortest possible time and by 2023 at the latest.

(2) The revised air quality modelling provided under paragraph (1) must demonstrate the green recovery
measures and the applicable class of charging Clean Air Zone, in the medium and small zone, appropriate
behavioural assumptions, and what (if any) additional measures, or adjustments to the local plan for NO2
compliance would need to be implemented by the authority to deliver compliance in the shortest possible
time.

Duty to prepare and submit a full business case

6.—(1) The authority must as part of its feasibility study continue with the work necessary to prepare a full
business case for the area for which it is responsible.
(2) The full business case must be submitted to the Secretary of State as soon as possible and by 26 February
2021 at the latest.

Submission of the full business case to the Secretary of State

8. When submitting its full business case, the authority must provide the Secretary of State with the following
information—
(a) the date on which it is proposed that the scheme identified in the full business case will start to be
implemented, which must be by 29 October 2021 at the latest.

There is a requirement to have undergone the necessary procurement activities, to have installed infrastructure
and be ready to go live with the CAZ from this date. In order to achieve this, the FBC must be submitted well in
advance to allow for the continued dialogue with JAQU to take place, agreeing the way forward at every gateway
review. The Direction is as a direct result of intense engagement with JAQU which is continuing in order to satisfy
both parties that the Bristol Clean Air Zone is the right strategic fit for Bristol that will meet the legal
requirements.

Post COVID-19 emerging situation

The Outline Business Case (OBC) that was submitted to JAQU in November 2019 included a set of proposals that
offered the most appropriate measures for Bristol at that time and that were aligned with the Mayor’s strategic
vision for the future development and direction of the city. However, the situation changed dramatically, without
warning, due to the global pandemic caused by COVID-19.

The council has reviewed the impact of the pandemic and the inevitable change to the project baseline that this
presents, the baseline which included previously modelled results and data analysis having been set in 2018 has
now dramatically changed. The methodologies, modelling and assumptions that have been used up until the
outbreak of COVID-19 are now outdated and do not take into account the effects of COVID-19 on our city. As
such, consideration was given to how we move forward in a way that delivers clean air and improved health
benefits to Bristol, recognising the unprecedented times we are in while also seeking to protect and enhance our
economy.

In July 2020 the Government announced funding of £2bn to create ‘a new era’ for cycling and walking. It has been
a long -held ambition for Bristol to create more liveable neighbourhoods that are free from traffic congestion and
pollution, giving more space to residents and businesses and improving walking and cycling journeys. This
ambition has become more important than ever in the context of COVID-19.
Page 82
Officers have been in continual dialogue with JAQU to consider the most suitable manner in which Bristol can
respond to the pandemic and take into account the new environment. Our bold plans to reallocate through traffic
away from the city centre in response to COVID-19 is testament to the level of ambition we hold in transforming
how people get around the city. In a short space of time and with the help of enabling legislation from
Government, we have made some radical changes to some of the most polluting areas in Bristol.

We closed Baldwin Street to help insulate and promote local bus services and ensure that residents are
encouraged to adopt public transport and active modes of travel. Other measures include the closure of Bristol
Bridge to through traffic other than buses, motorbikes, taxis and cyclists, new segregated cycle routes on Park
Row, Upper Maudlin St and Lewin’s Mead. Access to the central hospital and Bristol University has also been
improved with new segregated cycle routes.

The new proposals seek to build on these measures and develop further schemes to ensure we are successful in
improving air quality on our most polluted corridors, making schemes permanent or extended as required.

There is evidence that air quality improved during the first lockdown as movement around the city reduced and
travel behaviour changed. The Council’s preferred approach is to build on these behaviour changes and to
encourage citizens and businesses to sustain the recent, less polluting travel behaviour that had been seen during
the pandemic, without needing to implement a charging CAZ. The Council has supported this with improvements
to roads around the city that make it easier to walk, cycle or use public transport. On September 1st 2020, the
Mayor issued a call to action during a Council Cabinet Meeting, stressing that in order for there to be a reasonable
chance of avoiding a charging zone, people would need to modify their travel behaviour. He said:

“We need to use this opportunity for people to transition onto public transport. We can have
conversations with our bus providers to facilitate that and make sure people can do it in a safe way. But,
that transition also helps us build the longer-term case for the mass transit system that we’re also bringing
through. This really is a call to action. This is not something that the council can deliver alone; this is about
us as a city collectively engaging in behaviour change in the way we move around. If we collectively
engage in that behaviour change, we can get ourselves to compliance in the shortest possible time in a
way that does not further compound the economic woes faced by households and businesses in Bristol.”

Whilst we cannot say from the data being gathered that traffic levels and associated pollution levels will definitely
return to pre COVID levels, we also don’t have sufficient evidence to say otherwise. This data has now been
submitted and will be reviewed by the science team at JAQU as part of a full technical review of all the data
submitted. JAQU will subsequently conclude what measures Bristol are required to implement and, on that basis,
in order to comply with legal duties, Bristol would have no option but to propose a charging zone to meet our
legal duties if that is deemed the preferred approach by JAQU.

A further 6-week consultation took place from October 8th 2020. This was extended due to the impact of Covid-
19, to the 13th December 2020. There were 2 options consulted on: a dual zone consisting of a Medium CAZ C
(charging all commercial non-compliant vehicles) with a small CAZ D (charging all non-compliant vehicles including
private cars) and a standalone option of a small CAZ D (results explained on page 8 of this report). This data
alongside a revised Distributional Equalities Impact Assessment was used to prepare this EqIA.

Indices of Multiple Deprivation Deprivation data reveals that whilst the small CAZ are amongst the least
deprived nationally, in terms of employment deprivation, there are a few
pockets of income deprivation of varying degrees on the east side of the
zone.

Vehicle registration data reveals that there are more than 350 non-
compliant cars and LGVs registered in low-income areas within the small
CAZ boundary.

As well as potentially thousands of non-compliant vehicles registered to
low-income households elsewhere in Bristol and the West of England but
travelling into the CAZ. That said, imposition of a CAZ does not preclude
travel into the CAZ; instead households have options for altering their
travel patterns/behaviours and taking advantage of financial support
where eligible.
Additional comments:

The fact that we now need to take account of the radical changes that have occurred as a result of the pandemic
inevitably means that the baseline data has changed as a result of changed working patterns, altered and travel
behaviours; these changes must now be factored into the evidence base for future decision making. In order to
do this, we agreed a new three-staged modelling approach with JAQU to update the baseline and provide the
most current evidence to make decisions.

A methodology has been adopted that carries out modelling and sensitivity testing (reports that test the
modelling results against different scenarios) as well as capturing real time information from existing air quality
monitoring units and the automatic number plate recognition (ANPR) data. As an essential element of the
proposals, additional new air quality monitoring units will be installed to capture as much accurate and real time
data as possible.

This new methodology is largely due to an emerging change in air quality readings. During the periods of
lockdown and post lockdown, air quality and traffic levels were continuously monitored at our five continuous
sites and 100 diffusion tubes sites. While the improvement in air quality during this time was a welcome effect of
the lockdown, in order to define an area as being compliant (within the legal limits set), the air quality is
measured using an annual measurement: annual mean. Therefore, it is not possible to officially state that Bristol
had become air quality compliant at that time.

We manage air pollution levels in order to protect public health. Reducing air pollution leads to a reduction in
both morbidity and mortality.

Air pollution affects the whole of the city and health impacts from poor air quality will be experienced outside
the AQMA.

Page 88
The figure below shows the fraction of deaths (%) considered to be attributable to nitrogen dioxide in Bristol
wards in 2013.

Impacts of Charging Zones if implemented

London introduced an Ultra-Low Emissions Zone (ULEZ) in April 2019 which charges most vehicle types including
cars, taxis, buses, motorcycles and vans - the same area that London’s pre-existing congestion charging zone
covered.

Bath & North Somerset introduced a CAZ C in March 2021 which charges most vehicle types, including taxis,
private hire vehicles, vans (including pick-ups and some camper vans), light goods vehicles, buses, coaches and
heavy goods vehicles that do not meet the required emission standards. Cars and motorcycles are not required
to pay.

Birmingham introduced a CAZ D in June 2021 which, like Bristol, is one of the most extensive Clean Air Zones and
charges cars, buses, coaches, taxis, private hire vehicles (this includes Uber), heavy goods vehicles (lorries), vans
and minibuses that do not meet the required emission standards. Motorcycles are not charged.

At launch the percentage of vehicles not subject to the daily fee in Birmingham was slightly better than the
modelling suggested – at around 85%.

• Low income workers to apply for a temporary exemption of 1 year and residents in the zone 2 years.
• People with disabled passenger tax class on their vehicles are permanently exempt nationally.
Birmingham did not offer exemptions to Blue Badge holders, however, they do offer exemptions to
wheelchair accessible taxis.
• Birmingham also provided support for Taxi and Hackney Carriage drivers who do not speak English in the
form of PowerPoint presentation explaining CAZ in a number of different languages.

The second consultation received responses from a wide range of groups and residents. Overall, the survey
received 4,225 responses, of which 3,760 (89%) were self-completed online and 465 (11%) were self-completed
using paper surveys.

3,431 responses (81%) were received from postcodes within the Bristol City Council area, 214 (5%) responses
were from South Gloucestershire, 105 (2%) were from North Somerset, and 37 (1%) were from Bath & North-East
Somerset (B&NES). 49 (1%) responses were from further afield, 23 (less than 1%) respondents provided
unidentifiable postcodes, and 366 (9%) did not provide a postcode.

Deprivation - The home location of respondents in Bristol was compared with nationally published information on
levels of deprivation across the city to review if the responses received include a cross-section of people living in
more deprived and less deprived areas. The comparison looked at levels of deprivation in 10 bands (known as
‘deciles’) from decile 1 (most deprived) to decile 10 (least deprived). The response rate from the most deprived
parts of Bristol (deciles 1, 2, 3 and 4) is less than the proportion of citizens living in those areas. The proportion of
respondents in deprivation decile 5 closely matches the proportion of Bristol citizens living in deprivation decile 5.
Response rates from the least deprived parts of the city (deciles 7 to 10) are higher than the proportion of Bristol
citizens living in those areas. Although, the more deprived areas were under-represented as a proportion of the
population, the large number of responses in all deciles enables meaningful comparison of the views of people
living in the most deprived and least deprived areas.

• Age - The most common age of respondents was 35-44 years (23%), followed by 25-34 (20%) and 45-
54 (19%). The proportion of responses in the age categories 35-44, 45-54, 55-64 and 65-74 were higher
than these age groups’ proportion of the population in Bristol. Survey responses from children (under 18),
young people aged 18-24 and people aged 85 and older were underrepresented. In each age category, the
proportions of all respondents and Bristol respondents were very similar.

• Sex - 41% of all responses were from women and 58% were from men. 0.4% were from people who
preferred to self-describe (0.6% for Bristol respondents).

• Disability - The proportion of disabled respondents (8%) matched the proportion of disabled people living
in Bristol (. The proportion of disabled respondents living in Bristol was slightly lower (7%).

• Ethnicity –
− proportions of White British respondents (85%) and White British respondents from Bristol (84%) are
higher than the proportion of White Bristol people in the Bristol population.
− response rates from White Irish (2%) and Other White respondents (8%) were also higher than the
proportion of these groups living in Bristol.
− responses rate from Gypsy / Roma / Traveller people (0.1%) closely matches proportion of citizens in
the Bristol population.
− response rates from Black/Black British, Asian/Asian British, and Mixed Ethnicity citizens was lower
than expected based on Bristol populationPage data by90
ethnicity. We were unable to use some
engagement approaches that have been successful in previous consultations to target
underrepresented communities due to COVID-19 restrictions.

• Religion/faith - People with no religion (67% of respondents and 70% of Bristol respondents) responded in
higher proportions that people of no religion in Bristol’s population. Christians (27%), Muslims (0.8%),
Hindus (0.4%) and Sikhs (0.2%) were under-represented compared to the proportions of these faiths living
in Bristol. The proportion of Jewish respondents (0.4%) was higher than the Bristol population, as were
Buddhists (1%) and people of other faith (2%).

2.4 How have you involved communities and groups that could be affected?
You will nearly always need to involve and consult with internal and external stakeholders during your assessment.
The extent of the engagement will depend on the nature of the proposal or change. This should usually include
individuals and groups representing different relevant protected characteristics. Please include details of any
completed engagement and consultation and how representative this had been of Bristol’s diverse communities. See
https://www.bristol.gov.uk/people-communities/equalities-groups.
Include the main findings of any engagement and consultation in Section 2.1 above.

If you are managing a workforce change process or restructure please refer to Managing change or restructure
(sharepoint.com) for advice on consulting with employees etc. Relevant stakeholders for engagement about
workforce changes may include e.g. staff-led groups and trades unions as well as affected staff.
A full second consultation programme was designed and planned to ensure that people in the West of England
region understood the issues surrounding air quality as well as the potential solutions.

Due to COVID-19 and the restrictions at the time, all the engagement work was carried out remotely via email,
telephone and video conferencing. Under normal circumstances the engagement process would have been
managed in a different way. It would normally entail face to face meetings and door knocking shifts where the
team would visit each business, speak to the relevant person, give them physical copies of the information and
guide them through the consultation on an iPad; as with the first consultation.

The Transport Engagement and Active Travel team led on the business engagement element of the Clear Air Zone
(CAZ) second consultation, due to the expertise within the team. The Business Engagement Officers work with
businesses to encourage investment in sustainable travel modes both for their fleets and for their employees by
providing expert advice, free support and signposting them to the key offers. The advice and support range from
match funded grants, electric bike loans, and workplace travel audits, to staff engagement events, personalised
travel planning and bike maintenance sessions.

The Travel Advisors within the team led on the phone calls and emails as instructed by the Business Engagement
Officers. The team sent initial emails explaining the CAZ consultation and detailing the current sustainable travel
support. Officers sent 1,005 businesses one or more emails with information about the Traffic Clean Air Zone, a
call to action to complete the consultation and information about the Access West support. The Travel Advisors
then telephoned all business where a response had not been obtained. This enabled officers to reach more
businesses as the initial data gathering exercise often returned general email addresses. By telephoning the team
could often get to speak to the correct person or obtain an email address for them.

As part of this work the Business Engagement Officers also held a range of virtual meetings with the larger
employers in the city to delve a little deeper into the details of CAZ and what that will mean for their
organisations. The officers answered questions, talked through any concerns and have agreed to continue these
meetings to provide ongoing support whilst the details of the CAZ are developed.

Overall, the team put in over 650 hours of officer time supporting businesses during the consultation. The officers
have since supported on the paper copy data input and the coding of the free text analysis which contributed to
another 100 hours of officer time.

Page 91
Briefings were held with several groups including Business West (with 55 businesses joining), University Hospital
Bristol NHS Trust, Southmead Hospital, University of Bristol, University of West of England, Bristol Workplace
Travel Network, waste contractors, and neighbouring councils. We also contacted 1,385 businesses.

Other activities include an online campaign, radio adverts and live reads (Ujima and BCFM), adverts in hyperlocal
publications and newsletters, toolkit including suggested social posts, press release and newsletter content to key
stakeholders including councillors.

2.5 How will engagement with stakeholders continue?
Explain how you will continue to engage with stakeholders throughout the course of planning and delivery. Please
describe where more engagement and consultation is required and set out how you intend to undertake it. Include
any targeted work to seek the views of under-represented groups. If you do not intend to undertake it, please set
out your justification. You can ask the Equality and Inclusion Team for help in targeting particular groups.
− To further address the needs of disabled people who will be impacted by the Clean Air Zone and share
information about available support we will continue to work with the Bristol Disability and Equality Forum,
meet regularly with the Disability Equality Commission, and liaise with other disabled people led stakeholders
as appropriate.

− Bristol Disability Equality Forum have raised concerns including that there isn’t enough financial support
available, and that there is a lack of sufficient public transport alternatives, while there is an exemption period
for blue badge holders this only lasts until March 2023, unaffordable entry charges for non-compliant vehicles,
and all forms of the Clean Air Zone information must be fully accessible.

− To mitigate this as far as possible in the circumstances, we have proposed a package of support within the
confinement of the grant funding that includes financial support to blue badge holders on low incomes (less
than £27,000 a year) that regularly enter the zone, regardless of whether they meet the required number of
working hours. Eligibility will be assessed on a case-by-case basis. There is a £2,000 limit per vehicle that is
fixed and determined by Government across all Clean Air Zones, and this cannot be increased. The local Blue
Badge exemption has already been extended from December 2021 to March 2023, no other local authority
offers this exemption, and any further exemption will likely impact our ability to meet the legal mandated
timeframe for meeting air quality compliance. The council recognises the issue with buses, particularly Dial A
Ride transport, we are working with WECA, to support them with their public transport responsibility to meet
the needs of all citizens.

− We have taken advice from Neighbourhoods and Community teams, and Department for Work and Pensions
and are we are holding ‘CAZ clinics’ targeted towards disabled people. These are being held at accessible
locations such as libraries and job centres to promote and reach out to people to ensure they know what
support is available. Job centres have received training by us and are targeting those on job seekers
allowance.

− We have developed online EasyRead materials and also shared with the Department of Work and Pensions.
There is ongoing review of relevant web pages and resources to make sure they are accessible and
understandable. We have agreed to produce a paper exemption form for those who do not have access to the
internet.

− Providing British Sign Language translations of CAZ social media and website videos

3.1 Does the proposal have any potentially adverse impacts on people based on their
protected or other relevant characteristics?
Consider sub-categories (different kinds of disability, ethnic background etc.) and how people with combined
characteristics (e.g. young women) might have particular needs or experience particular kinds of disadvantage.
Where mitigations indicate a follow-on action, include this in the ‘Action Plan’ Section 4.2 below.
GENERAL COMMENTS (highlight any potential issues that might impact all or many groups)
Accessibility impacts are likely to be mixed. Households on low income, particularly those with children and women are more
likely to be impacted by the Clean Air Zone, as they are more likely to be driving a non-compliant vehicle. Women more often
have the childcare and carer responsibilities. Impacts will be disproportionately felt by some ethnic minorities, disabled
residents and less so for older people.
Whilst there will be some mitigations in place for some disabled people, some of these will be temporary and therefore not
all disabled people will benefit from them for the duration of the scheme, such as those with Blue Badges or those who do
not work and therefore are not eligible to apply for financial assistance.
Affordability impacts are likely to be negative across the socio-economic and business groups that directly interact with CAZ
area, especially where there are charges for non-compliant cars or any restrictions on specific movements. Impacts are
disproportionately felt by the second most and least income deprived communities.
Impacts also fall on businesses operating non-compliant LGVs and HGVs who are either based in the CAZ area or based
elsewhere but operate within central Bristol and hence also interact with the CAZ area. Using TUBA vehicle operating cost
benefits as a proxy for affordability indicates that the impacts are generally positive across the city as a whole, and
distributional assessment of this impact is reasonably even, with a slightly greater proportion in middle-income areas than
either the most or least deprived areas.
There will be direct impacts on the costs of operation for LGV/HGV reliant businesses, where their operations interact with
the CAZ area.
Trips by non-compliant LGV/HGV reliant businesses are reasonably spread around the city.
Modelling carried out as part of the Full Business Case suggests that approximately 30% of vehicles driving into the zone do
not currently meet the emissions standard, and therefore will be charged. Bristol City Council issues around 18,000 Blue
Badges each year. A number of these will either have:
- a disabled tax class vehicle which will be covered by a national permanent exemption
- or a Motability vehicle, of which the majority are newer compliant vehicles
- Blue badge holders can apply for a local temporary exemption that will be valid until end of March 2023
Distribution of non-compliant car ownership is bending towards lower income groups. Hence the package of financial support
that is on offer is predominantly targeted at this group as a first priority.

3.2 Does the proposal create any benefits for people based on their protected or other
relevant characteristics?
Outline any potential benefits of the proposal and how they can be maximised. Identify how the proposal will
support our Public Sector Equality Duty to: Page 97
✓ Eliminate unlawful discrimination for a protected group
✓ Advance equality of opportunity between people who share a protected characteristic and those who don’t
✓ Foster good relations between people who share a protected characteristic and those who don’t

All protected characteristics
Nitrogen dioxide levels brought about by compliance with the Ministerial Direction should have an
overall positive impact on people within all protective characteristic groups. It is recognised as a
contributing factor in the onset of heart disease and cancer. This represents about 8.5% of deaths in the
administrative area of Bristol being attributable to air pollution.

Young people
A reduction in No2 levels will ultimately save lives. Evidence suggests that poor air quality can cause
permanent lung damage in babies and young children and exacerbates lung and heart disease in older
people . A 2017 report into the health effects of air pollution in Bristol concluded that around 300
premature deaths each year in the City of Bristol can be attributed to exposure to nitrogen dioxide
(NO2)is associated with several adverse health impacts.

Older people
A reduction in hospitalisation and overall improvement in longer term conditions. Older people are
disproportionately affected by air pollution with spikes in particulate matter associated with increased
strokes and hospitalisation. Many are already living with cardiovascular diseases, COPD and other
breathing problems. Exposure to air pollution has also been associated with cognitive decline in older
people.

As a result, general improvements will benefit this age group. Their relative geographical distribution is
not strongly aligned to polluted areas or potential charging zones.

Disability
Some disabled people, for example those with breathing difficulties are more vulnerable to air pollution.
There is growing evidence that long-term exposure to outdoor air pollution is thought to increase the
risk of developing COPD loss of lung function and increased respiratory symptoms. Reducing N02 should
see general health benefits for all people suffering with respiratory illnesses.

Pregnancy / Maternity
Emerging evidence suggests that air pollution can affect the growth of the unborn baby and may be
linked to premature birth (Impact of London's road traffic air and noise pollution on birth weight:
retrospective population-based cohort study, British Medical Journal 2017.) (RCPCH and RCP Report
Every Breath We Take). Therefore, the reduction in nitrogen dioxide levels brought about by compliance
with the Ministerial Directions should have a positive impact on people within this protective
characteristic group.

Race
Black and minoritised communities make up a larger proportion of the population living in the more
polluted areas – the Air Quality Management Area - than the city as a whole, and therefore it is
reasonable to assume that they experience greater exposure to air pollution. Successful interventions to
improve air quality should contribute to improving the citywide health of Black, Asian and minority
ethnic people.

Socio-Economic (deprivation)
Page 98
People living in the most deprived areas within the zone should see a positive impact on health due to
the benefits of a reduction in nitrogen dioxide levels brought about by compliance with the Ministerial
Directions.

Sex/religion/gender reassignment
We do not have evidence to indicate that people are differentially exposed or vulnerable to air pollution
on the basis of their: sex; marriage / civil partnership; gender reassignment; or religion and belief.

Step 4: Impact

4.1 How has the equality impact assessment informed or changed the proposal?
What are the main conclusions of this assessment? Use this section to provide an overview of your findings. This
summary can be included in decision pathway reports etc.
If you have identified any significant negative impacts which cannot be mitigated, provide a justification showing
how the proposal is proportionate, necessary, and appropriate despite this.
Summary of significant negative impacts and how they can be mitigated or justified:
Consultation around the CAZ proposal have helped to inform some partial mitigations that have been put in place
wherever possible to support people who may be disproportionately affected on the basis of their protected
characteristics within the restrictions of the grant funding provided by Government. In identifying potential
mitigation targets, exemptions for groups with protected characteristics have been set out. In summary these are:
• National (permanent)
• Disabled passenger vehicles (with tax class 85)
• Disabled vehicles (with tax class 78)
• Local (temporary until March 31 2023)
• Vehicles used by Blue Badge holders
• Vehicles of residents living inside the zone
• Registered community transport vehicles (Section 19 and 21 permits)
• Home to school travel
• Home to school transport (buses, minibuses and coaches
• Vehicles of families with personal travel budgets (PTBs), whose journey to school is through the zone

In identifying potential mitigation targets, it has endorsed the inclusion of mitigation measures, such as a loan and
grant scheme to enable people on low-income with polluting vehicles to enable alternative transport solutions
under the scheme. This may also include some groups with protected characteristics. The financial support
includes: £2,000 towards upgrading to a compliant vehicle if you earn less than £27,000 and work in the zone or
for Blue Badge holders on low incomes (less than £27,000 a year) that regularly enter the zone, regardless of
whether they meet the required number of working hours. Eligibility will be assessed on a case-by-case basis.
Mobility credits up to £100 for bus taster tickets, eScooter credit, bike loans and car club credit
The Government limit for funding for cars is £2k and vans up to £4.5k. This can be used for purchase, adaptations
or retrofit.

Although we have addressed and implemented mitigations wherever practically possible, there are still likely to be
some disproportionate impacts for citizens on the basis of their characteristics and circumstances (detailed in
Section 3 above) for which we are not able fully mitigate, but which we think are justifiable on the basis that the
overall CAZ scheme is reasonable, proportionate and necessary.
Summary of positive impacts / opportunities to promote the Public Sector Equality Duty:
Cleaner Bristol by reducing N02 levels across the city – positive impact on health of children, older people, those
with respiratory diseases, and those living in areas of socio-economic deprivation. Potential reduction in demand
on NHS services in Bristol due to fewer hospitalisations

4.3 How will the impact of your proposal and actions be measured?
How will you know if you have been successful? Once the activity has been implemented this equality impact
assessment should be periodically reviewed to make sure your changes have been effective your approach is still
appropriate.
As part of the full business case, an Evaluation & Monitoring Plan was drawn up. This laid out how the project’s
benefits will be monitored through the sensor network in order to show that air pollution levels have reached
legal compliance in the directed timeframe. Through the network of sensors, the council will be able to monitor
air quality improvements by area, and evaluate the degree to which lower income neighbourhoods, and areas
with a higher proportion of residents with protected characteristics, are improving compared to the mean. This
will be carried out by the Air Quality Team.

The monitoring of the exemptions will be determined by the number of people applying for exemptions. The
exemption portal opened 26/09/22 for people to apply in advance of the CAZ launch.

The monitoring of the financial and accessibility impacts upon groups with protected characteristics are difficult to
monitor independently, as the future Quality of Life Survey results on accessibility and transport will be affected
by a range of inter-dependent factors outside the scope of this project. We will be able to assess the support
provided, from the number of people taking up loans and grants, and mobility credits.

Evaluation and Monitoring plan Appendix 38 of the CAZ FBC:

2021%2016.00%20Cabinet.pdf?T=9).

Step 1: What do we want to do?
The purpose of an Equality Impact Assessment is to assist decision makers in understanding the impact of proposals
as part of their duties under the Equality Act 2010. Detailed guidance to support completion can be found here
Equality Impact Assessments (EqIA) (sharepoint.com).
This assessment should be started at the beginning of the process by someone with a good knowledge of the
proposal and service area, and sufficient influence over the proposal. It is good practice to take a team approach to
completing the equality impact assessment. Please contact the Equality and Inclusion Team early for advice and
feedback.

1.1 What are the aims and objectives/purpose of this proposal?
Briefly explain the purpose of the proposal and why it is needed. Describe who it is aimed at and the intended aims /
outcomes. Where known also summarise the key actions you plan to undertake. Please use plain English, avoiding
jargon and acronyms. Equality Impact Assessments are viewed by a wide range of people including decision-makers
and the wider public.

Over the last 10 years we have made changes to the road network to improve bus journey times and
to encourage more walking and cycling. With the climate emergency and 2030 carbon neutral
targets we need to propose radical changes to the road network that will make real differences to
transform bus travel and encourage walking and cycling.

This work package is part of a wider ambitious project to improve how people travel across the city
along key transport routes, making it easier to connect people to jobs and leisure opportunities,
anticipating growing population and supporting the city’s health and economic growth.

The aim is to make it easier and more convenient to use the bus, walk and cycle wherever possible,
rather than use private cars. This project aims to make walking and cycling more attractive and to
give priority to buses through infrastructure improvements. This would reduce air pollution to
improve the health of everyone.

This project therefore looks at the longer-term aspirations to grow bus travel and First West of
England have committed to work with the West of England Combined Authority (WECA) and Bristol City
Council (BCC). This will ensure that the city and bus operators can work together to improve journey
times, increase passenger numbers, and expand the network.

Over the last few years cycling and walking levels have remained high compared to other major
cities and Bristol has seen growth in bus use. COVID-19 has presented extra challenges – bus travel
has by necessity, substantially reduced during the lockdown. At the same time cycling has seen a
significant increase.
Page 102
Without significant investment in walking, cycling and bus infrastructure it will be difficult to
encourage people to drive less and only use cars when essential, particularly as we recover from the
coronavirus pandemic. Investment is needed to tackle high levels of traffic congestion and reduce
levels of air pollution.

Funding is currently available via the City Regional Sustainable Transport Settlement (CRSTS),
administered by West of England Combined Authority (WECA), to make sustainable transport
improvements along certain strategic corridors throughout Bristol.

Our Objectives

This project, as part of the CRSTS funding, seeks to promote public transport and active travel along the
immediate area of Park Street, The Triangle and Queens Road.

The main objectives of this project are to:

• Improve Bus Priority
o This project will seek to implement a new bus gate on the north end of Park Street, with a
banned left turn coming from Canons Road and a bus lane leading into Park Street from
the South.
o This will reduce traffic levels and allow for buses to pass quicker and more efficiently
through the area, encouraging public transport usage
o Smaller provisions throughout the scheme such as altering existing zebra crossings and
implementing signal infrastructure which prioritises buses.
o Improvement to existing bus stops, notably on Queens Road, with a build out and new
formalised bus stop outside of the University.
• Make several public realm improvements along the route,
o Reallocation of road space adjacent to Queens Road, creating a new multi-use ‘Plaza’
space, with added trees, planters and seating.
o This will create point closures on Richmond Hill and Park Plaza
o Formalised permanent closure of University Road, implementing public realm area with
trees, Sheffield stands for cycles, planters, and seating.
o Point closure on Berkely Avenue, with public realm scheme, including planters, seating
areas and potential space for businesses to use the space for tables and chairs.
o Park Street Avenue to be closed to motorised vehicles (outside of specific time zones to
allow for loading for businesses). Public realm scheme to be implemented, including
planters, seating, business table and chairs, and cycle parking.
o Widened footways allowing for low-level planters along Park Street, creating the potential
for business tables and chairs.
o It must be noted that these changes in public realm will not restrict anyone’s ability to
be dropped off at the door of their property or amenities – furthermore there will be
increased disabled parking and loading bays.
• Improve provisions for Active Travel with the implementation of a segregated cycle-way compliant
with LTN 1/20 guidance, with traffic reductions and widened footways along the route improving and
providing safe 8-80 pedestrian and cyclist provision. This includes:
o The introduction of a segregated cycle-way throughout the scheme:
▪ This connects to the Whiteladies Road Flooding Scheme proposed withflow cycle-
way, becoming a two-way segregated cycle-way heading south through the
proposed public-realm scheme ‘Plaza’, with provisions for access to Richmond Hill
and Park Plaza which are now closed to through traffic.

Page 103
This two-way segregated cycle-way continues along the north side of the Triangle,
▪
allowing cyclists a more direct route through the Triangle section, separated from
traffic.
▪ On the eastern side of the Triangle, the cycle-way becomes a segregated withflow
cycleway, with provisions to connect to Park Row developments, and for ease of
access onto Park Street which is now closed to through traffic, reducing traffic and
creating a safer space for cyclists.
▪ Note, parking is now switched to the eastern side of the Park Street from the west,
reducing the risk of ‘car-dooring’ and improving cyclist safety.
o Widened and continuous footways:
▪ Footways are widened throughout the scheme, most notably on and throughout
the length of Park Street, on Queens Road, on Triangle West, and adjacent to the
‘Plaza’ area.
▪ This allows for a safer and more welcoming environment for pedestrians to
navigate.
▪ Continuous footways allow for pedestrians to navigate the footpath and area
more efficiently and safetly, reallocating space away from motorised traffic.

Indices of Multiple Deprivation

o The Indices of Deprivation are a unique measure of
relative deprivation at a small local area level (Lower-
layer Super Output Areas) across England and have
been produced by the Ministry of Housing,
Communities and Local Government and its
predecessors in similar way since 2000. The Indices of
Deprivation 2019 (IoD2019) is the most recent release.
o The Indices provide a set of relative measures of
deprivation for small across England, based on seven
different domains, or facets, of deprivation:
• Income Deprivation
• Employment Deprivation
• Education, Skills and Training Deprivation
• Health Deprivation and Disability
• Crime
• Barriers to Housing and Services
• Living Environment Deprivation
Page 111
o Deprivation is measured in a broad way to encompass
a wide range of aspects of an individual’s living
conditions.
o Each of the domains above are constructed from a
basket of different data datasets, or indicators. As far
as is possible, each indicator is based on data from the
most recent time point available. It must be noted that
the last time this survey was completed was 2019.

2.3 Are there any gaps in the evidence base?
Where there are gaps in the evidence, or you don’t have enough information about some equality groups, include an
equality action to find out in section 4.2 below. This doesn’t mean that you can’t complete the assessment without
the information, but you need to follow up the action and if necessary, review the assessment later. If you are
unable to fill in the gaps, then state this clearly with a justification.
For workforce related proposals all relevant characteristics may not be included in HR diversity reporting (e.g.
pregnancy/maternity). For smaller teams diversity data may be redacted. A high proportion of not known/not
disclosed may require an action to address under-reporting.
Out-of-date data

Data from the 2021 census was most likely impacted by Covid-19 and related lockdowns. Transport and
commuting data in particular is likely to be inaccurate in 2023 and so this would be useful to re-assess.
This also applies to the indices of multiple deprivation study, carried out in 2019, and the Bristol quality
of life survey, conducted in 2023. The Ward profile tool uses more up to date data in areas, with
population estimates coming from 2022/23, using the census as a baseline for the estimates.

Seldom-heard-groups

Demographic data reveals groups that are sometimes seldom-heard-from in decision making. All three
wards have data similar to the Bristol average but are still important to note and consider for
stakeholder engagement.

Through extensive engagement with key stakeholders, we may begin to identify gaps in our information
gathering. Some topics we will explore include local challenges and solutions to accessing sustainable
transport modes and the barriers faced by people from some of the protected characteristics: for
example, the Quality of Life survey data does not include ward information for the category of “%
pregnant or who have given birth in past 6 months, who have suffered discrimination or harassment”.
This is the sort of question that would be useful to explore in the context of encouraging sustainable
transport. This group could be disproportionately affected if they don’t have access to a vehicle, so
reliable and safe public transport is important for this group.

Public Consultation Process (2021-2022)

• An initial public engagement was held in early 2021, followed by a formal public engagement
published in 2022.
• The public engagement, titled: ‘Consultation Report on improvements to the number 2 bus route
(A37/A4018)’ was held to engage the public on several proposals throughout the whole of the
number 2 bus route. It was agreed the team would hold a consultation process from 29 November
2021 until 28 January 2022 and it was for 8 weeks rather than the normal 6-week period as this
Page 115
covered the Christmas period. The consultation covered proposals for the entire transport corridor,
but due to the length of the corridor the consultation was broken down into 3 sections known as the
north, central and south sections.
• The overall work in which the public consultation focuses on was later divided into 5 distinct work-
packages, with the Park Street, Triangle and Queens Road works (Work Package 3) drawing evidence
from the ‘Central’ section of the report
• A summarisation of the results of the report are as followed:
o Queens Road - 62% of respondents agreed and strongly agreed with the proposed
changes
o Triangle - 52% of respondents agreed and strongly agreed with the proposed changes
o Park Street - 45% of respondents agreed and strongly agreed and 49% disagreed and
strongly disagreed with the proposed changes
o College Green - 52% of respondents agreed and strongly agreed with the proposed
changes

Step 3: Who might the proposal impact?
Analysis of impacts must be rigorous. Please demonstrate your analysis of any impacts of the proposal in this

Also include details of existing issues for particular groups that you are aware of and are seeking to address or

Equality Impact Assessments (EqIA) (sharepoint.com)

3.1 Does the proposal have any potentially adverse impacts on people based on their
protected or other relevant characteristics?
Consider sub-categories and how people with combined characteristics (e.g. young women) might have particular
needs or experience particular kinds of disadvantage.
Where mitigations indicate a follow-on action, include this in the ‘Action Plan’ Section 4.2 below.
GENERAL COMMENTS (highlight any potential issues that might impact all or many groups)
The scheme designs aim to address barriers for people who want to access quality public transport and
active travel infrastructure, particularly those with protected characteristic groups. Inclusive
infrastructure needs to consider people of all ages and ability.

Access to quality public transport infrastructure is a necessity for a clean and healthy society in which car
and motor use is reduced and people are able to use reliable bus travel as an alternative to motorised
vehicles. In order to achieve this, we must utilise bus prioritisation in order to reallocate road space in
order to limit delay and make bus travel a more attractive option. Many people in protected groups rely
on bus transport to access work/ education and further economic opportunity. This is crucial for
economic growth within the city. Examples of this include younger people, in which there is significant
transport poverty, as young people are less likely to own a car and are more likely to rely on public
transport and active travel options. Further to this, some older people who can no longer drive due to
health reasons rely on public transport heavily, as this group may be reliant on public transport when
travelling into the central area for goods and services. This is detailed in the following sections.

We also have an ambition to make walking, wheeling and cycling the natural choices for short journeys
or as part of a longer journey with supporting objectives to increase cycling and walking levels. Following
guidance such as Cycle Infrastructure Design LTN 1/20 and Inclusive Mobility, this supports the delivery
of high-quality walking, wheeling and cycle infrastructure to deliver this ambition and objective; and
reflects current good practice, standards and legal requirements. The development of scheme designs
also learn from local best practice, previous delivery and historic discussions and decisions. Active travel
improvements should not negatively affect anyone, although removal of parking for road space
reallocation may impact those who rely on motor vehicles. Interventions such as new or improved
crossings anecdotally help a lot of people who use the facility but especially groups such as some older
people, black, Asian and minoritised ethic and disabled people.

On the other hand, a reduction in carriageway allocation and road closures could reduce access and
lengthen journey times for the same groups who rely on cars and struggle to use active travel or public
transport modes. Altered street configuration could create issues for Disabled people’s access along
footways and familiarity with surroundings. We will seek to provide strict design criteria for pavement
licencing to ensure that pavements remain a safe and uninterrupted space for people to use.

Page 117
A reduction in both parking spaces outside shops and in local centres could affect access for Disabled
people to services, although the scheme seeks to retain and increase Disabled parking. However, a
reduction in overall parking could affect access for pregnant people and some older people, who are
more likely to rely upon a private car to access shops and services. This can have a knock-on effect on
walking distances and the requirement for provision to break up journeys (seating/resting
places). Providing well designed, conveniently located disabled parking within the immediate zone of
influence, is crucial and parking has been allocated around residential areas and areas where amenities
are located. The changes in the scheme will not restrict anyone’s ability to be dropped off outside of
their property or amenities – there may be increased diversion, but access is not limited. Disabled
organisations were consulted throughout the planning and development of the designs so that
removal/relocation of parking was fully considered. Further to this, access to amenities and people’s
houses was also discussed, and this influenced the design.

Wayfinding and legibility for navigating the Triangle may also be challenging but can be addressed
through upgrades to signage.

Engagement & Communication

We are aware of the barriers to engagement in the process of consulting on the scheme if businesses,
residents and visitors are digitally illiterate. Only 47.8% of people in Bristol aged 65+ say they are
comfortable using digital services, compared to 81.8% overall. We need to use a range of
communication channels and combine conventional engagement methods, such as telephone
interviews, radio and print, with virtual platforms and interactive tools, such as online interactive maps
and surveys, to reach a representative audience. This is particularly true of engaging with market traders
during any consultation activity.

It is vital that all communications are in plain English and that Easy Read versions are available (or on
request if appropriate). People who do not speak English as a main language will require local updates
and information in plain English, and alternative languages/formats to address the risk of misinformation
being spread e.g., through social media. This is being met through the location, language and design of
tangible communications within the public realm.

Black, Asian and minoritised ethnic-led small businesses may lack information about the support
available to them from the government particularly taxi drivers, restaurants, cafes and hotels. Equally,
those from more deprived parts of the city may not necessarily be engaged in the scheme or feel they
can contribute in a meaningful way on balance.

Age: Children Does your analysis indicate a disproportionate impact? Yes ☒ No ☐
Potential impacts: • Almost one third of children are in poverty, a greater proportion than for any
other age group. This increases to nearly 50% for lone-parent families.
• The availability and affordability of transport can contribute to children’s access
to important resources.
• Active travel presents an opportunity to promote health and wellbeing among
children. This is particularly important for children who are more likely to
develop childhood obesity due to further intersectional characteristics.
• The effects of air pollution are particularly significant for the health of children.

Page 118
• Children from a lower socio-economic background are also more likely to be
exposed to high levels of pollution due to living in densely populated urban
areas.
Mitigations: - The implementation/improvement of active travel elements throughout the
scheme which re-prioritise road-space towards active travel alternatives
presents an opportunity to promote health and wellbeing among children. This
is particularly important for children who are more likely to develop childhood
obesity due to further intersectional characteristics.
- Reallocation of road space also greatly reduces traffic numbers, lowering
pollution levels.
- Safety is also improved with segregation on cycle-ways and widened and
continuous footways, increasing confidence for children and young people in
using the cycle network.
- Reallocation of road-space through bus gates/bus lanes helps to decrease
journey time and increase reliability. This makes buses a more attractive travel
option.
- Creation of public realm spaces which children can use.

disorder and anti-social behaviour.

Page 119
• Access to appropriate forms of transport can help some older people avail

• Research also suggested that uptake of shared mobility services is lower
amongst some older people and some disabled people. This is related to
barriers such as the lack of on-demand accessible options, unfamiliarity with
the technology needed to book services and inability to use digital payment on
a smartphone, and not being comfortable with unfamiliar ride hailing drivers.
• Volunteer transportation systems can more easily serve some older and
disabled people due to higher client engagement, lower costs and higher user
familiarity with the service providers.
• Some older people in the 80 to 90 age groups tend disproportionately to be
women living alone.
• Some older people become more reliant on taxis and lifts from family and
friends as a transport mode, providing a supplement to the publicly accessible
fixed-route bus and rail system.
• Research from Age UK has found that an improved provision of active transport
(including walking and cycling) could disproportionately benefit some older
people. Increased provision of active transport is likely to improve the amount
of physical activity, which is linked to better cognitive performance, better
mental health outcomes and reduce overall morbidity and mortality.
• Currently only 8% of men and 3% of women over the age of 65 in the UK cycle, a
much lower proportion compared to both the general population in the UK and
those over the age of 65 in European countries.
• A reduction in parking spaces could impact some older people who are more reliant
in their private vehicles to access services.
