Disability Does your analysis indicate a disproportionate impact? Yes ☒ No ☐
Potential impacts: • Undertaking an analysis of current transport trends among Disabled people it
is important to note that Disabled people are not a homogenous group, their
needs and abilities can vary greatly depending on the nature and severity of
their Disability.
• Families that include a Disabled person have always been at greater risk of poverty
(JRF 2017: 25)
• Disabled people face a range of challenges in relation to mobility and various
modes of transportation.
• Primarily, key obstacles relate to a lack of accessible infrastructure, at stops,
stations and other locations, as well as in use of vehicles themselves.
• Where people are unable to rely on public transport either due to structural
barriers or because of geographical location, they are likely to increasingly rely
on more expensive services such as taxis and private hire vehicles (PHVs) –
affecting the affordability of travel.
• Accessible and inclusive information relating to routes and tickets is also a key
challenge. Adequate information, alongside staff presence and assistance can
help to make Disabled passengers feel safer when travelling, as well as making
journeys easier and more stress-free.
Page 121
• Active travel modes for Disabled people are reliant on well marked shared
spaces and clear pedestrian routes, where these are present, modes such has
walking and cycling can have both mental and physical health benefits for
Disabled people.
• Appropriate transport provision enables Disabled people to participate in their
community, maintain social networks, and access employment, education,
healthcare and other services.
• The unemployment rate in the UK for Disabled people was 6.7% in 2019,
despite this rate having reduced, it is still nearly double the national
unemployment rate. Evidence shows that difficulty in accessing transport is the
second most common barrier to work among disabled people.
• While Disabled people tend to travel less than non-Disabled people, many are
nonetheless reliant on public transport. There can be large variances in a
person’s travel patterns depending on their Disability and its severity. For
example, according to the Department for Transport’s ‘Disabled people’s travel
behaviour and attitudes to travel’ report, having a learning or physical
Disability correlates strongly to travel by bus. Around 60% of Disabled people
have no access to a car and use the bus around 20% more than their non-
Disabled counterparts.
• Disabled people are more likely to report negative and problematic journey
experiences, alongside limited awareness of viable alternatives. For some Disabled
people, the attitude of staff and other passengers, as well as the unpredictability of
public transport (both timings and capacity), prevents them from using public
transport. For some neurodivergent people, a lack of routine or unexpected events
can become overwhelming, leading to high levels of stress and anxiety.
• A reduction in both parking spaces outside shops and in local centres could
affect access on Disabled people, who are more likely to rely upon a private car
to access shops and services.
• Altered street configuration could create issues for Disabled people’s access
along footways and familiarity with surroundings.
• Low noise vehicles (scooters/electric vehicles) could have implications on the
visually and hearing impaired and can thus compromise the perception of safety.
• Reduced parking may potentially impact some Disabled people who rely on their
own private vehicle to access services and amenities.
Mitigations: • Some Older Disabled people or people who have a long-term health condition
might are more reliant on staff on public transport to help enable them to
undertake a journey. Improved bus service reliability can support people from
this group to use the local services.
• Appropriately designed and conveniently located Disabled parking within the
immediate zone of influence will be increased with improved access.
• To improve appropriately designed walking and cycling infrastructure to comply
with national guidance following LTN1/20 to encourage all ages and abilities,
including segregated cycleways and widened footways.

Sex Does your analysis indicate a disproportionate impact? Yes ☒ No ☐
Potential impacts: Women
• A lack of adequate public transport creates barriers to women accessing
employment and educational opportunities. This is related to their patterns of
participation in the labour market.
• Less women across the UK hold a driving license compared to men (67% versus
77%). Women also tend to not have access to a car, particularly during the day as
they either cannot afford one, or the family car is being used by a partner.
• Kamruzzaman and Hine (2012) highlighted that an understanding of access to
activity spaces can shed light on the gendered dynamics of social exclusion. For
example, women had more transport constraints than men, as childcare
constraints meant they were less likely to take longer journeys. They were also less
likely to travel at night or on weekends due to perceptions of safety, stemming
from a lack of transport during these periods
• Having less access to private means of transport such as bicycles, motorcycles and
cars, women are inclined to take work closer to home, often in the informal sector,
which may limit their opportunities for finding better paid or higher skilled
positions. This may be exacerbated by a limited availability of part-time work or
work which fits around school hours.
• Research evidences that gender inequality in cycling is common, with low levels of
cycling among women compared to men. This could be due to cultural factors that
remain in place despite an increase in the promotion of active travel. Promoting
gender quality and normalising cycling culturally could benefit women in increasing
the numbers of those cycling regularly.
• Caring responsibilities also tend to disproportionately fall to women and often
require making multiple short journeys during a day – for example, to drop off
Page 123
children at school, visit family members and shop for food – which creates an
additional challenge if private transport is not available. In such cases public
transport services may not sufficiently interconnected, requiring journeys with
several changes and a long commuting time. A reduction in parking may
disproportionately impact women due to them having to incorporate other things
into their daily commute to work (trip chaining).
• When involved in a road accident, women are also more likely to fall casualties
than men.
• While fewer women tend to have access to private transport, women make greater
use of taxis and PHVs in comparison to men, increasing with older age. This is
despite challenges around costs and affordability as well as personal safety when
using a PHV or taxi as passengers can feel vulnerable and concerned due to
travelling with strangers.
• Feelings of personal safety and security are thus a recognised barrier to women
using public transport.
Men
• Younger men aged 16-19 are more likely to be victims of crime on the public
transport network compared to men of all other age groups.
• Even though men tend to undertake fewer trips per year when travelling, they tend
to travel further distances. Private vehicle use and ownership is also higher
amongst men, with evidence showing differences in driving habits, as well as a
higher propensity to be employed in sectors that require driving, such as freight and
logistics and public transport.
• Men are in fact more likely to be involved in road traffic accidents across all
transport modes this is also due to their higher propensity to use certain transport
modes.
• Younger men are also more likely to be road casualties.
• With pedestrians, female pedestrians account for just over half of journeys made by
foot (52%), but men make up the majority of pedestrian casualties (57%).

Mitigations: • Bus stops regularly throughout the residential area and community areas.
• Implementation of new and maintenance of existing Appropriate CCTV/lighting
measures which contribute to the perception of safety for public transport users
and those who use active modes will be in scope throughout development and
engagement.
• Appropriate surveillance such as CCTV and active surveillance, as well as
appropriate lighting in public realm areas to promote safety
• Continued conversations with the Police and representative from Bristol night-time
economy regarding safety in the area.
• The scheme is designed to improve the quality, safety, and accessibility of the active
travel route.
• Walking, cycling, and wheeling are intended to be more attractive options for
parents with young children e.g., trips to school.
• The provision of improved infrastructure to support cycling should have a positive
impact on women, who have lower levels of uptake compared to men
• A reduction in overall parking could affect access for carers, who are more likely to
rely upon a private car to access shops and services. This can have a knock-on effect
on walking distances and the requirement for provision to break up
journeys (seating/resting places). Providing well designed, conveniently located
parking within the immediate zone of influence, is crucial and parking has been
allocated around residential areas and areas where amenities are located. The
changes in the scheme will not restrict anyone’s ability to be dropped off outside of
their property or amenities – there may be increased diversion, but access is not

Potential impacts: • Black and Minority Ethnic-led small businesses may lack information about the
support available to them from the government particularly taxi drivers,
restaurants, cafes and hotels. Equally, those from more deprived parts of the city
may not necessarily be engaged in the scheme or feel they can contribute in a

• Black, Asian, and minority ethnic households consistently have the highest rates of
poverty, and White British households have the lowest.
• Adults from Asian, Black or other ethnic groups took substantially fewer trips per
person in 2017 than those from white or mixed groups.
• There is some disparity when looking at figures for people from a Black, Asian and
minority ethnic background in relation to walking and cycling. DfT walking and
cycling statistics suggest that people from a mixed ethnicity background were most
likely to walk for travel once a week.
• In terms of cycling, DfT data suggests that Black and Asian adults are least likely to
cycle.

Page 126
• It has been highlighted in research that people from a Black, Asian and minority
ethnic background fear racial attacks when using public transport, thus potentially
causing a barrier to their use of transport networks.
• Higher level of air pollution exposure is linked to the high proportion of Black, Asian
and minority ethnic communities living in densely populated urban areas where air
pollution is highest.
• Access to transport for some people is tied closely to geography, and infrequent
public transport services, particularly in the evening and at weekends, can impact
the type of employment people are able to access and can, for example, affect the
ability to undertake shift work. Research has found that this was particularly the
case for ethnic minority groups concentrated in more deprived areas.

Mitigations: • By providing safe, accessible, and direct infrastructure to support and prioritise
active and sustainable modes of travel, people from Black, Asian, and minority
ethnic groups can be disproportionately positively impacted by regarding the
uptake of these modes, given they are currently disparity in comparison with white
groups.

Potential impacts: There is no current evidence to suggest that this protected characteristic group might
experience transport differently today.
Mitigations: N/A
OTHER RELEVANT CHARACTERISTICS

Page 127
Socio-Economic Does your analysis indicate a disproportionate impact? Yes ☐ No ☐
(deprivation)
Potential impacts: • People who depend more on the bus network for work tend to be lower paid, live in
more deprived areas, and are more likely to turn down jobs due to transport issues,
than those on higher incomes, who tend to use cars and trains more often.
• Income was found to be one of the defining aspects of socio-economic inequality.
Transport costs and affordability are central to the impact of transport on
inequality. If transport is too expensive, then people are not able to make the
journeys they need to get into work or move into education and training that could
improve their prospects.
• Those who depend more on the bus network to participate in the labour market
tend to be lower paid, reside in areas of deprivation, and are more likely to turn
down employment due to transport limitations. It is important to have a reliable,
accessible bus route in this area to connect those on lower income to the city
centre for essential services such as doctors, employment and education.
• Affordability of public transport is one of the key barriers for people living on low
incomes, such as people who are unemployed, in insecure or low paid work, and
people who live in deprived areas.
• Evidence from the Joseph Rowntree Foundation also highlights that residents in
low-income neighbourhood often find commuting options constrained by
unaffordable or unreliable public transport, especially when combined with the
prospect of low-paid or unsecure employment. Low-income jobs such as cleaning,
or security roles may require early starts or late finishes when public transport is
not available. Furthermore, peripheral sites of employment, such as retail,
commercial and industrial parks are hard to access using the public transport
system, making people living in low-income neighbourhoods more reliant on
private transport. Ensuring the bus runs from early in the morning until late in the
evening will ensure those with lower income who rely on this service can access
employment and educational needs.
• Key vehicles for addressing poverty include welfare and public support, education,
cost of living interventions, employment, and social support (e.g. health and social
care services, family relationships (Joseph Rowntree Foundation 2016).
• Access to work is greatly improved by more accessible and affordable public
transport opportunities. Transport is important in obtaining a job, keeping a job, or
getting a better job. Improving provision for cycling can also have a positive impact
on employment opportunities.
• Cycling is regarded as a good way to widen travel horizons for disadvantaged
individuals.
• Support in paying for transport is a way in which cities can support people living in
poverty to access and maintain work.
• Lower income households have higher levels of non-car ownership – female heads
of house, children, younger and some older people, people from a Black, Asian and
minority ethnic background and Disabled people are often concentrated in this
statistic.
• There are geographical inequalities in the provision of transport and as a result
differences in access to employment, healthcare, education, and other amenities
occur. Often these are located in areas that already have good transport links or are
due to have new transport hubs opening nearby. However, residential areas may
have a wider scale of provision compared to areas of employment. The lower level
of car ownership, combined with limited public transport services in many
peripheral social housing estates, exacerbates issues around access to services,
education and employment.
• Evidence suggests that people living in deprived areas face unequal access to
certain modes of transport. Research has found that only a small number of
deprived areas are served by the rail network, instead mostly being accessibly by
Page 128
local buses. Where there are train stations, they are often perceived as rundown
and secluded, leading to feelings of fear about using them.
• People living in deprived neighbourhoods are significantly more likely to feel unsafe
and believe that crime is a significant problem in the areas that they are living.
• A 2018 study into pedestrian safety revealed that children who live in deprived
areas are at a greater risk of being involved in a road related accident (as both a
passenger and a pedestrian) when compared to other children. Children living in
the most deprived quintile are six times as likely to be involved in an accident than
those living in the least deprived quintile. Rates of Killed or Seriously Injured
casualties in relation to miles walked for people in the most deprived quintile is
over double that of those living in the least deprived (0.58 and 0.28 casualties per
million miles walked).
• There is major disparity between people living in deprived areas and communities
in more affluent areas regarding the exposure of individuals to polluted air.
• Increasing promotion and provision of active transport directly benefits people who
reside in deprived areas by improving the local air quality and improving their
health and wellbeing. For example, obesity rates for children are highest amongst
those in deprived areas.
• Public transport has the potential to increase access to employment and education,
in return creating economic prosperity. However, this is based on ensuring that
transport networks connect more deprived areas to centres of employment and
education.
• Ensuring feelings of safety are increased will encourage more people to participate
in active travel modes and use public transport that is available. Safety can be
improved by the provision of quality lighting, clear sightlines and where appropriate
surveillance. Furthermore, concerns around road safety can be reduced through
appropriate education, signs and road markings amongst other things.

Mitigations: • As the scheme prioritises public transport, and lower cost and free modes of
transport (i.e., walking and cycling), the interventions aim to reduce transport
inequality and the impacts of transport poverty on people’s ability to access
employment, education, essential services, and leisure activities.
• Access to work is greatly improved by more accessible transport opportunities.
Transport is important in obtaining a job, keeping a job, or getting a better job.
• Lower income households have higher levels of non-car ownership – female heads
of house, children, younger and some older people, people from a Black, Asian and
minority ethnic background and disabled people are often concentrated in this
statistic. By prioritising active and sustainable transport options, the scheme seeks
to address transport poverty and reduce transport inequalities.
• Increasing promotion and provision of active transport directly benefits people who
reside in deprived areas by improving the local air quality and improving their
health and wellbeing. For example, obesity rates for children are highest amongst
those in deprived areas.
• Through the reduction of vehicle traffic and the prioritisation of local trips being
made by active and sustainable modes, evidence which shows that children who
live in deprived areas are at greater risk of being involved in a road related accident
(as both a passenger and a pedestrian) when compared to other children can be
addressed.
• Rates of Killed or Seriously Injured casualties in relation to miles walked for people
in the most deprived quintile is over double that of those living in the least deprived
(0.58 and 0.28 casualties per million miles walked). By removing the ability for non-
local vehicle traffic to pass through the scheme area, the interventions
implemented should support a reduction in this disparity.
• Schemes such as the £2 price cap on all adult bus tickets are particularly beneficial
for those that are within this group
Page 129as it makes taking the bus more accessible.
Carers Does your analysis indicate a disproportionate impact? Yes ☒ No ☐
Potential impacts: • Disabled people may find it more difficult by the lack of bus stops so a further
distance to get the bus could make getting the bus more inaccessible.
• As with Age, Disability and Pregnancy and Maternity – policies which aim to change
or limit driving or parking can have a disproportionate impact on people who are
reliant on having their own transport to provide care for someone else.
• Being a carer can be a huge barrier to accessing services and maintaining
employment. Studies show around 65% of adults have provided unpaid care for a
loved one, that women have a 50% likelihood of being an unpaid carer by the age of
46 (by age 57 for men), and that young carers are often hidden and may not
recognise themselves as carers.
• Reduced parking has the potential to impact carers who are visiting clients.
Mitigations: • Bus stops regularly throughout the residential area and community areas.
• General mitigations above regarding socio-economic deprivation are also relevant
to this characteristic.
• A reduction in both parking spaces outside shops and in local centres could affect
access for Disabled people and consequently carers to services, although the
scheme seeks to retain and increase Disabled parking. However, a reduction in
overall parking could affect access for Disabled people and therefore carers, who
are more likely to rely upon a private car to access shops and services. This can have
a knock-on effect on walking distances and the requirement for provision to break
up journeys (seating/resting places). Providing well designed, conveniently located
parking within the immediate zone of influence, is crucial and parking has been
allocated around residential areas and areas where amenities are located. The
changes in the scheme will not restrict anyone’s ability to be dropped off outside of
their property or amenities – there may be increased diversion, but access is not
limited. Disabled organisations were consulted throughout the planning and
development of the designs so that removal/relocation of parking was fully
considered. Further to this, access to amenities and people’s houses was also
discussed, and this influenced the design.

the length of Park Street, on Queens Road, on Triangle West, and adjacent to the
‘Plaza’ area.

navigate.

more efficiently and safetly, reallocating space away from motorised traffic.

The EqIA process has helped to identify potential impacts and the need for planned monitoring and
subsequent adjustment of measures in line with feedback from equalities stakeholders and citizens.

The scheme will require the removal or relocation of vehicle parking. In areas where Disabled parking
bays are located and may be subject to change, we will engage with the relevant groups to assess the
impact and develop options which mitigate any negative impact with these groups. However, general
use parking, which may be used by some older people or pregnant people with reduced mobility who

meetings. Disabled organisations were consulted throughout the planning and development of the
designs so that removal/relocation of parking was fully considered. Further to this, access to amenities
and people’s houses was also discussed, and this influenced the design.

in some deprived areas of the city, where levels are low compared to the Bristol average. Therefore,
there is an opportunity to gather evidence on how targeted interventions in areas which suffer from
poor health could be replicated across Bristol in the future. The scheme will be:

4.3 How will the impact of your proposal and actions be measured?
How will you know if you have been successful? Once the activity has been implemented this equality impact
assessment should be periodically reviewed to make sure your changes have been effective your approach is still
appropriate.

The monitoring and evaluation of schemes post-implementation is crucial for data-led evidence to test their
success against the original objectives. This is known as ‘legacy’ and whilst evidence is gathered on changing travel
behaviours, traffic collisions, air quality improvements etc, evidence is also collected to assess positive and
negative impacts of the interventions on people with protected characteristics.

Evaluation approaches can involve:
• Community surveys: Community surveys carried out in cohorts to capture public feedback and for monitoring
travel behaviour and social impacts.
• Secondary data collation: Collation of information from existing datasets that are collected at regular
intervals to report on progress against objectives.

Evaluating schemes against their objectives can be done using both quantitative and qualitative methods. If, for
example, one objective of a scheme is to ‘Improve residents’ physical and mental health and wellbeing’,
monitoring could be done through community surveys, before and after audits (e.g., Healthy Streets indicators) or
the ‘Quality of Life’ survey. This approach describes a minimum level of monitoring and evaluation to be carried
out for each scheme that is necessary to evidence their success against their intended objectives.

There is a need for flexibility in evaluation approaches given the varying context for projects. Each approach varies
according to existing place-making and travel behaviour, relevant stakeholder groups, community engagement
feedback received from project inception, and differing interventions. The monitoring and evaluation approach
will vary project by project and this could include:

1 Public Sector Equality Duty | Equality and Human Rights Commission (equalityhumanrights.com)
2 Ibid.
3 Ibid.
4 The 2021 Walking and Cycling Index present statistics across Bristol that shows that people in lower socio-

economic groups are less likely to cycle. ‘Socio-economic group is a classification based on occupation maintained by
the Market Research Society. Groups A and B are professional and managerial. Groups D and E are semi-skilled and
unskilled manual occupations, homemakers and people not in employment’. Proportion of residents who cycle at
least once a week: 33% of AB, 26% of C1, 22% of C2, 20% of DE (9% in 2019)4. These statistics highlight that people in
groups A, B and C have higher rates of cycling.
Page 133
• Adaptations to community surveys to capture evaluative feedback on themes identified from the community
engagement within a scheme area (to ensure there is an appropriate feedback loop on issues important to the
local community and which captures a representative evidence base from people with protected
characteristics).
• Widening community surveys within a scheme area to include a broader range of public feedback and/or
include specific stakeholder groups (if there is under-representation from people with protected
characteristics).
• Additional qualitative monitoring, including focus groups with specific stakeholder, disability, or community
groups, or to capture more in-depth evidence from participants of surveys.
• Expanding data collection to include a wider study area if there remains an under-representation of people
with protected characteristics.
• Additional monitoring tools e.g., parking surveys (pedestrians, cyclists, and cars) to understand the varying
groups of people travelling along the route.

Agenda Item 10

Committee Report

KEY DECISION OR FOR INFORMATION: Key decision affects 2 or more wards

COMMITTEE: Transport and Connectivity Committee

DATE: 23 October 2025

TITLE: Cycle Hangar Policy

Ward(s): Citywide

Officer presenting the report: Adam Crowther Job title: Head of City Transport

Committee Chair: Councillor Ed Plowden

Executive Director lead: John Smith: Executive Director for Growth & Regeneration

Proposal origin: Committee Chair

Purpose of Report:

1. To seek approval to enact a target policy, outlined further in the Cycle Hangar Policy
Paper, for the delivery of new cycle hangars up to 2030, and to adopt the proposed position
therein.
2. To seek delegated approval for the Executive Director Growth and Regeneration in
consultation with the Transport and Connectivity Committee Chair and Section 151 Officer
to bid for, accept and commit to any funding in support of the policy up to a total estimated
combined value of £4 million.

Introduction

Bristol City Council would like everyone to have the option to access safe and secure cycle
storage, as part of supporting active and sustainable travel in the city. Bike theft is prevalent in
Bristol, and hangars offer a solution to the key cycling barrier of lacking a secure and
accessible storage location, or any suitable storage at all. With over 500 locations previously
requested in 2023, and 90% of newly installed hangar spaces booked out within two hours of
availability, cycle hangars continue to demonstrate a consistent demand.

A cycle hangar policy aims to make setting off by bike as easy and accessible as by car. By
establishing a minimum target for installations, and the types of areas that are most in need of
secure on-street storage, future funding can be directed towards this essential goal.

The Transport and Connectivity Committee recently approved Active Travel Fund Tranches 5
and 6, setting aside just under £200,000 for the delivery of future cycle hangars, and the policy
will draw from this for the next round of installations. Beyond this, funding is not currently
identified for additional delivery of hangars. As such, the Cycle Hangar Policy seeks delegated

Template approved March 25
Page 141
OFFICIAL
approval to bid for and accept any future funding from various sources outlined
below to enact the policy up to 2030.

Proposed Positions

To make the use and availability of cycle hangars and storage as accessible as possible, the
following positions are proposed:
a. At least 400 additional hangars by 2030, atop of 102 existing, inclusive of those
planned for installation as part of other City Transport projects.
b. Priority delivery for social housing and areas experiencing higher deprivation, but
especially neighbourhood areas with high household occupancy of Terraced, Flat,
Shared, Converted or Commercial property types.
c. Delivery team to review and develop approach for supporting street parking and
specialised hangar provision for those with adapted bikes, cargo bikes and bike
trailers.
d. Accelerated request response, available to make via
www.bristol.gov.uk/cyclehangars.

Evidence Base

1. Please see Appendix A1, Cycle Hangar Policy Paper, for detailed evidence base.
2. Safe cycle storage has been seen to increase bike ownership by 24%, and 94% of users
find cycle hangars both secure and easy to use, increasing trips by 1.44 on average per
week.
3. People living in census-designated Terraced, Flat, Shared, Converted or Commercial
housing often have the most difficulties with storing bikes, and show a high tendency in
2023 requests, but some areas with many residents in these accommodation types are
underrepresented (Appendix A2). As such, targeted delivery will help to provide hangars
where they are needed most.
4. Further analysis to explore intersection with social housing, deprivation and
accommodation will be undertaken when deciding on targeted delivery locations.

Alternative options considered:

1. No new cycle hangars – not recommended due to evident latent demand and requirement
for enabling new cycling journeys. No extra cost, but poor ongoing provision.
2. Limited, local rollouts – not recommended due to city-wide relevance of lack of secure
cycle parking. Whilst more concentrated in the centre, all wards have at least some areas
with many residents living in Terraced, Flat, Shared, Converted or Commercial
accommodation. Newer developments are the exception, often building in cycle storage as
standard, and so will not be a principal target of the policy.
3. Incorporate other cycle parking features into the policy – not pursued due to the upcoming
delivery of Bristol’s Kerbside Strategy, which will detail in the round proposals for
reallocation of kerb space for sustainable transport and other uses, including cycle stands.
Funding, Costs and Administration
Template approved March 25
Page 142
OFFICIAL
Funding and Costs

1. Various pots of funding are expected to be available, including the upcoming Transport City
Region (TCR) fund, future tranches of Active Travel England (ATE) funding, CIL funding,
developer contributions (Section 106), and EU funding streams. Historically, the West of
England Combined Authority (WECA) has attained funding through central government for
schemes such as the West of England Cycle Hangar Project. The Combined Authority
currently hold a scheme promotion and grant assurance role in cycle hangar delivery, and
we will continue to work with WECA to ensure correct governance is in place to deliver
BCC’s cycle hangar target.
2. Based on a full breakdown of the previous round of cycle hangar delivery in Bristol, it is
estimated to cost today £383,460 to deliver 40 cycle hangars. When incorporating this
figure to installing the 400 cycle hangars in line with this policy, it totals £3,834,600.
Including a 4.25% inflation rate, the overall cost is expected to reach £3,997,570, but
changes in management and administration, as well as potential economies of scale with
future delivery, could affect the final figure.

Administration

3. The current cycle hangar contract for Bristol City Council is held by Lock-it-Safe, expiring
towards the end of 2026 but able to be extended for a further two years. A £55 yearly user
fee covers both normal operating maintenance and administration costs, with no Council
contribution. Quarterly reports are given to the Council on the condition of hangars. £10 per
user is held in a dedicated account, with access only for Bristol City Council, for reactive
maintenance not covered by warranty. The approach to administration is not expected to
change with the continuation of the present contract.
4. There are presently no plans to change cycle hangar contract – if this were to be altered
due to, e.g., continued expansion of hangar stock, a hangar maintenance approach would
be developed within City Transport that would not burden Council budgets. Either bidding
would ensure that a pot of funds is held for hangar upkeep and repair, or user fees would
cover this as per the current contract.
5. The relevant City Transport team responsible for future administration and delivery will be
undertaking a review of the contracts and management process to account for the
additional hangars required, and to determine the best approach going forward. This could
include a benchmarking assessment of how other Authorities approach prioritisation of
space allocation.
6. As with any scheme that proposes reallocation of the public highway from parking, there
may be budgetary considerations regarding parking revenue. This is actively under
consideration in the development of the Transport Investment Strategy, which will look
holistically to balance the meeting of health, emissions and environmental targets with
budgetary concerns.
Officer Recommendations:

That the Committee for Transport and Connectivity:
Template approved March 25
Page 143
OFFICIAL
1. Approves the approach to future delivery and 2030 installation target set out
in the attached Cycle Hangar Policy Paper.
2. Authorises the Executive Director of Growth and Regeneration, in consultation with the
Chair of the Transport and Connectivity Committee, to procure and award any future cycle
hangar contracts in line with the maximum combined budget envelope outlined in this
report.

Corporate Strategy alignment:

1. New secure cycle parking is aligned with the Council’s commitment to enable cycling in the
city. To reach the One City target of carbon neutrality by 2030, vehicle mileage must be
reduced by 40%, and sustainable transport methods such as cycling increased. Residents
frequently raise the lack of simple and accessible storage as restricting their ability to cycle,
or to own a bike, an issue that this this policy aims to address.
2. An ambitious cycle hangar policy aligns with the Corporate Strategy‘s Vision, to drive an
inclusive, sustainable, and healthy city by encouraging active travel uptake.
3. It also aligns with its Transport and Connectivity theme which aims to develop a more
efficient, sustainable, and inclusive connection of people to people, people to jobs and
people to opportunity, making it safer and easier to use cycleways and pavements,
regardless of ability.
4. Finally, it aligns with the Environment and Sustainability theme, which aims to decarbonise
the city, by improving active travel infrastructure and reducing reliance on private vehicles.

City Benefits:

1. The government guidance document LTN 1/20 emphasises that cycle parking is “integral to
any cycle network”. The “availability of secure cycle parking” throughout a cyclist’s journey
“has a significant influence on cycle use”.
2. Bristol’s 2023 Quality of Life Survey found a 7% gap between the most deprived decile and
the city average for people cycling at least once a week (reducing to 4.6% in the 2024
survey). As a key barrier to cycling, a lack of safe storage in areas of high deprivation must
be addressed as a matter of equality. Protected groups such as Disabled or older people
are often excluded from cycling when storage would involve lifting over steps, up sets of
stairs, or awkward movements down narrow corridors. A cycle hangar policy aims to make
setting off by bike as easy and accessible as by car.
3. A city which is more active is one that is cleaner, more equitable and better connected. Its
citizens are less lonely due to barriers to independent travel being removed and have the
mental and physical health benefits of regular exercise. With an improved active travel
offer, those without the means to drive are nonetheless able to benefit from a modern, safe
transport network.
4. With fewer people driving for short journeys, air quality improves, and general emissions
decrease. In the long-term, the overall energy usage per capita of the Bristol transport
network will be much lower with greater active travel uptake, and overall emissions of
greenhouse gases will decrease.

Template approved March 25
Page 144
OFFICIAL
5. When implemented alongside other key changes to infrastructure in the city, a
cycle hangar policy will enable more people to choose to travel by bike, and
bring a host of benefits for health, sociability, sustainability and equality.

Consultation Details:

1. The Cycle Hangar Policy has been arrived upon by consulting with relevant disciplines
within City Transport, and learning from previous iterations of cycle hangar delivery.
2. Consultation will include standard engagement questions for targeted monitoring of uptake
in cycling journeys, with a focus on understanding the extent to which car journeys are
shifted to cycling in areas which receive cycle hangers.
3. In line with the approach taken with previous rounds of cycle hangar delivery, consultation
will occur in the form of a city-wide (and Combined Authority-wide should funding be
received at this level) wave of resident requests, which will then be sifted for appropriate
sites for delivery. Requests can also may made via www.bristol.gov.uk/cyclehangars.
4. Informal consultation with residents at the street level, including letter drops, will determine
the most suitable site for installation. This will be especially important in later delivery
waves, where a mix of targeted and request-based delivery is planned, especially for areas
above a certain threshold of households with Terraced, Flat, Shared, Converted or
Commercial properties.
5. Bristol City Council will meet any legal requirements necessary, including consultation, as
dependent on site of installation.

Professional comments section:

1. Finance Advice: This report is requesting the approval of a target policy for the delivery of
new cycle hangars up to 2030. Within the report, the target set is for 400 new hangars at an
estimated cost of £4 million and it is requested that delegation be given to the Executive
Director of Growth & Regeneration, in consultation with the Committee Chair and Section
151 Officer, to spend this amount.

Finance has been provided with a high-level breakdown of the cost of delivery of 40 hangars
based on previous spend. The cost has been estimated at £348,600 plus a 10% contingency
and 4.25% inflation, to give £399,757, which forms the basis of the £4 million estimate for
delivery of 400 hangars. This estimate appears reasonable at this stage.

The team has identified several potential funding sources for delivery of the hangars,
however at this stage there is not a detailed funding plan. Future decisions will be taken in
consultation with the Section 151 Officer, and any spend agreed will be backed by a robust
funding plan. The grant bid protocol will be followed for any funding applications in relation
to delivery of this policy.

Finance Business Partner: Ben Hegarty, Finance Business Partner Growth and
Regeneration, 6 August 2025.

2. Legal Advice: The submission of bids for grant funding raises no particular legal issues. If
successful, the procurement process must be conducted in line with the Procurement Act
2023 and the Councils own procurement rules. Legal services will advise and assist officers
with regard to the conduct of the procurement process and the resulting contractual
arrangements.

A Traffic Regulation Order (“TRO”) will need to be the subject of a statutory public
consultation exercise which will require the consultation to be undertaken at a time when the
proposals can be influenced and changed in response to any feedback / objections received
as a result of the consultation. Any consultation responses must be taken into account by
the decision maker before the TRO is confirmed.

Bristol Council City Transport

Cycle Hangar Policy

Appendix A1 – Cycle Hangar Policy Paper

October 2025

Policy Aim: To make on-street secure bike storage as accessible as possible for
those who need it.
To achieve this, the following is proposed:
a. At least 400 additional hangars by 2030, atop of 102 existing, inclusive of those
planned for installation as part of other City Transport projects.
b. Priority delivery for social housing and areas experiencing higher deprivation, but
especially neighbourhood areas with high household occupancy of Terraced, Flat,
Shared, Converted or Commercial property types.
c. Delivery team to review and develop approach for supporting street parking and
specialised hangar provision for those with adapted bikes, cargo bikes and bike
trailers.
d. Accelerated request response, available to make via
www.bristol.gov.uk/cyclehangars.

Figure 1. Bristol Cycle Hangar (Source: BBC News [1])

Page 148
OFFICIAL
Summary
Modal shift is individual and multifaceted; bike rides begin much earlier than the first
interaction with a cycle route. An ambitious cycle hangar target policy will support the first
and last minute of cycle journeys in Bristol.
Parking a bike inside brings in dirt, causes clutter and reduces living space. Bristol
terraced housing frequently lacks garden access, creating inconvenience and mess even
if opting for outdoor garden storage. Landlords or letting agencies may not permit internal
storage at all; a higher-than-average Bristol renting rate of 45.2% against 37.5% for
England and Wales [2] making this yet more pressing. Flats, Shared Occupancy,
Converted or Commercial properties also each have their own challenges for internal
bicycle storage
Whether stored in the garden, attached to the wall or kept in the hallway, not to mention
navigating stairs or lifts for flat occupants, the extra effort needed to move a bike to the
road in these property types ensures that access to the public highway is almost always
easier for those choosing to drive. For some disabled people that use bikes (including as
mobility aids) and with the increasing popularity, but increased weight, of e-bikes, these
forms of cycle parking may be especially challenging. This becomes especially important
for shorter trips just outside of walking range, where even a minor inconvenience can be
significant for journey time, and lead people to opt for the car.

Key Statistics
• The 2023 West of England Cycle Hangar Project (WECHP) consultation received 541
requests.
• 90% of WECHP spaces were booked out within 2 hours [1], proving demand.
• The Sustrans Walking and Cycling Index 2023 [3] notes that there was one reported
theft for every 167 bicycle owners in Bristol (1,463 reported thefts in 2022/2023). The
necessity of outdoor storage due to limited parking options is a contributing factor.
• Safe cycle storage has been seen to increase bike ownership by 24%, and 94% of
users find cycle hangars both secure and easy to use, increasing trips by 1.44 on
average per week [4].
• Recommendation 9 of the 2021 Bristol Citizen’s Assembly Report, which includes
transferring 3-5% of street car parking spaces over to cycle parking and shared green
space a year, received a combined 89% Strongly Support or Support [5].

Mapping and Analysis

• Figure 2 (also seen in Appendix A2) shows a map of 2023 cycle hangar requests
against accommodation type, i.e. proportion of residents occupying Terraced, Flat,
Shared, Converted or Commercial properties (T/FSCC) in each Lower Layer Super
Output Area in Bristol. There is a clear tendency for requests to cluster around these

Page 149
OFFICIAL
housing types. Existing House in Multiple Occupation (HMO) Licence density is also
overlayed, and shows a similar but not exclusive tendency. Maps splitting into
specifically Flats, Shared, Converted and Commercial (FSCC) and Terraced (T) can
also be seen in Appendices A4-5. This distinction is important to make, as needs of
the one category are not always directly translated to the other.
• Whilst HMOs have been displayed as an indicative contributor to hangar demand,
future HMO licences will require secure cycle storage as standard, reducing this
factor’s salience for targeted delivery. As such they have not been included as a
priority area in the policy.

Figure 2. Map showing select Census 2021 accommodation (T/FSCC) overlayed with WECHP
requests and areas within the highest quintile (20%) of HMO density. (Source: ONS [6];
Internal)
• Important, too, is the distinction between renting and home-owning households.
Homeowners are often able to implement custom solutions for storing bikes, whereas
those renting will likely not have this freedom.

Page 150
OFFICIAL
• It can also be seen that there are large areas of T/FSCC accommodation types where
requests were not received, showing latent demand that may not be met on a
request-basis alone.
• As such, a statistical test was undertaken to investigated further. The test used, a
‘Generalised Linear Regression’, can be employed to understand the relationship
between one main variable, hangar requests in this instance, and one or more other
factors such as accommodation. A map can be found in Appendix A3 that presents
the difference, or ‘deviation’ from what requests may have been expected based upon
accommodation data. This provides a clearer depiction of areas with high occupancy
in Terraced, Flat, Shared, Converted or Commercial properties, yet without many
2023 requests.
• These dark purple areas show that potential demand is highly concentrated in some
under-requested parts of the city centre, but also extends outside of the central ring
where previous requests were largely clustered. Areas area also shown in dark green
where requests were greater than expected by the test, and whilst data is imperfect
due to many zero-values, these potentially indicate where the largest vocal demand
may continue to be found.
• These maps are not intended to directly denote future hangar locations, and more
detailed analysis will be done at later stages of targeted delivery. For example, factors
such as central blocks of flats potentially incorporating cycle storage have not been
included, but could partially explain lack of requests. For the same reason, newer
developments will not be a principle target of the policy.
• Social housing, a priority category for delivery of new hangars, has not been included
within indicative mapping, so will also require more detailed analysis to show how
demand and latent demand intersect. Council rented flats, due to accommodation
type and social factors, may prove of greater priority – multiple factor analysis will
enable these interactions to be considered in prioritisation.

Aspirational, but Achievable
Other authorities, in London especially, but also Glasgow as a key example, are rapidly
increasing cycle hangar availability to keep up with demand. With Bristol’s Victorian
terraced streets, and high numbers of flats and other associated housing types, similar
ambition should be set here. A detailed local authority comparison of hangar delivery
targets and current availability can be found in Appendix A6.

Bristol is, of course, not London. Whereas terraced streets and flats are mutual, Bristol’s
more car-dependent transport network and comparative funding constraints present
obstacles. A proposed delivery of 400 additional hangars will be achieved through a
ramp-up over the years preceding 2030. An indicative representation may be found
above in Figure 4. This assumes a high demand scenario, where cycling mode share

Page 151
OFFICIAL
targets are being met, and increased accessibility of the cycle network has led to a yet
more pressing need for hangars.

Figure 3. Cycle hangar delivery past and projected

This aspirational but achievable target, aiming initially for a ratio of one hangar / 1000
Bristol residents, will not necessarily be evenly distributed, and actual availability for
populations in T/FSCC housing (67% of households [6]) will be higher. Later phases of
delivery will be guided by a mix of targeted and request-based installation. Mapping
exercises such as those seen in the Appendix help to denote where this future hangar
provision could be focused, but the approach will ultimately be decided by the
appropriate delivery team.

Conclusion

With a range of funding options available, expertise in delivery and a pressing need for
on-street parking demand evident, cycle hangars can and will be delivered in increasing
numbers over the next five years. Residents currently limited by the security of on-street
parking will have this barrier overcome through access to a hangar, with the option to
cycle unlocked for more people than ever.

Whilst not the focus of this paper, short-stay on-street storage options are also up for
consideration, with solutions currently forthcoming that charge a small, market-
competitive fee by the minute to park a bike, just as on-street car parking meters charge
by the minute for cars.

By 2030, with an anticipated wider availability of both home and destination storage,
parking a bicycle in Bristol will soon provide the same peace of mind as the private car,
with all its modern security features. The threat of cycle theft will diminish as the key
barrier to entry that it currently is, enabling Bristol to continue to work towards its
aspiration of a safe, accessible, healthy and sustainable transport network.

Equality Impact Assessment [version 3.1]

Refer to EqIA Step by Step Guide for advice on completing this assessment
Title: Cycle Hangar Policy
Type of proposal: Policy Status: New
Directorate: Growth and Regeneration Lead Officer name: Anthony Davies
Service Area: City Transport Lead Officer role: Transport Strategy Officer

Step 1: What do we want to do?

What is the purpose of the proposal?
Bristol City Council would like everyone to have the option to access safe and secure cycle storage, as
part of supporting active and sustainable travel in the city. Bike theft is prevalent in Bristol , and cycle
hangars offer a solution to the key cycling barrier of lacking a secure and accessible storage location, or
any suitable storage at all.

Cycle hangars (pictured below) offer fixed on-street, secure cycle parking for up to 6 bikes which can
only be accessed by lock and key or on an app in some locations. They are about the size of one parking
space and are for an area’s local residents.

Page 161
60,000 homes, 32% of Bristol’s housing stock, are Victorian terraced housing with narrow hallways and
often no garden access. Despite bumper-to-bumper parking on these residential streets, for the driver it
is as straightforward as a key in the ignition and a few careful turns of the wheel to set off. Leaving aside
other preparations that contribute to the relative inconvenience of cycling, a cycle hangar policy aims to
tackle two key equivalents in the parking and departure process, especially in older dwellings or flats:

• Parking a bike inside brings in dirt, causes clutter and reduces living space. Bristol terraced
housing frequently lacks garden access, creating inconvenience and mess even if opting for
outdoor garden storage. Landlords often may not permit internal storage at all.
• Whether stored in the garden, attached to the wall or kept in the hallway, not to mention
navigating stairs or lifts for flat occupants, the extra effort needed to move a bike to the road
ensures that access to the public highway is consistently far easier for the driver than the cyclist.

Modal shift is individual and multifaceted; cycle journeys begin much earlier than the first interaction
with a segregated cycle route.

Proposed position:
• One cycle hangar per 1000 residents by 2030 – i.e. one secure cycle space for every 166 residents
• ~70% on-street, ~30% social housing/ areas with high levels of deprivation (refined with further
work)
• Accelerated request process for residents, aspiring to 12 months from application acceptance
• Commit to supporting people with adapted bikes, cargo bikes and bike trailers by
accommodating these requests on a case-by-case basis, including guidance and assistance for
bespoke storage

Process to determine location of hangars to be arranged by appropriate delivery teams

Additional comments:

Do you have relevant diversity data for the following protected characteristics?
☒ Age ☒ Disability ☒ Gender Reassignment
☐ Marriage and Civil Partnership ☐ Pregnancy/Maternity ☒ Race
☒ Religion or Belief ☒ Sex ☒ Sexual Orientation

Are there any gaps in the evidence base?
Whilst it is a challenge to engage with all our citizens and we know that there are some groups with seldom heard
voices with whom we can do a better job at engaging with, recent surveys do capture a credible snapshot of
feeling on several key issues Bristol continues to face. Results 168
Page from the Quality of Life, Your City Our Future
(related to the Covid-19 pandemic and subsequent lockdowns) and Bristol Citizens Assembly, highlighted many of
the imbalances and feelings of inequality across the city and made recommendations for change.

While we aim to collect diversity data for all relevant characteristics, there are some gaps in the local data
available, particularly for characteristics that haven't always been included in past census or statutory reports.
Additionally, there are gaps in our workforce diversity data due to under-reporting, as this information is
voluntarily provided by staff and remains confidential.

How have you involved communities and groups that could be affected?
No public facing engagement has taken place so far as this policy is for setting a target for cycle hanger
rollout across the city. Officers have engaged internally with the Citywide Initiatives Team, who will be
responsible for the delivery of cycle hangars moving forward. Informative discussions have been had
with project managers of previous rounds of cycle hangar installation in Bristol and when the funding is
secured for the hangars, engagement as outlined in the below box will be carried out.

How will engagement with stakeholders continue?
Engagement will continue with internal teams at the following meetings:

• Executive Director Meeting on the 22nd August 2025
• Policy Committee Chair Briefing on the 18th September 2025
• Agenda Briefing on the 2nd October 2025

In line with the approach taken with previous rounds of cycle hangar delivery, consultation will occur in the form
of a city-wide (and Combined Authority-wide should funding be received at this level) wave of resident requests,
which will then be sifted for appropriate sites of delivery. As cycle hangars require a Traffic Regulation Order,
there will be a statutory consultation period and an objection report will be written up if required. In later delivery
waves, a mix of targeted and request-based delivery is planned. As such where areas are determined to have
above a certain threshold of households with Terraced, Flat, Shared, Converted or Commercial properties, street-
level consultation will take place, with letter drops and other forms of light-touch local engagement.

This EqIA will be updated to reflect any changes or updates to this.

Step 3: Who might the proposal impact? [complete if ‘Yes’ to an equality impact]

Could the proposal affect people disproportionately because of their characteristics?
GENERAL COMMENTS
The interrelated issues that Bristol faces regarding health and economic inequality, and the climate emergency
and need for low-carbon solutions to these problems, create an urgent need for the Council to take actions that
may disproportionately impact residents. Transport is a key part of this, and as such all Bristol citizens will be
impacted by future sustainable transport interventions in the city, as the way we move around changes.

The policy proposal referred to here is a functional and essential change to the city’s cycle infrastructure, any
disproportionate effects on some residents will be captured during the consultation and request processes. The
context within which it is set will continue to improve cycle infrastructure and access to the detriment of the ease
of access of the private car.

All people living and working in Bristol, including those with protected characteristics, will benefit from more
inclusive transport policies such as this one, which encourage the widest layers of the population to move actively,
and safely. Those who still need the car will be quite free to do so, but for those who do not want to, or cannot
drive, an improved and more legible cycle infrastructure will have benefits that greatly outweighs those of a
business-as-usual scenario for climate, health, and equality.

Contractors Page 169
Contractors would be completing and maintaining the cycle hangars.
• Contractors will be made to adhere to equalities guidelines and limit any possible disruptions.
• Whilst we have not identified any significant negative impact from the proposal, we are aware that
disruption during works may have a disproportionate impact on some groups.
• As part of the quality scoring assessment for joining the Framework, providers will be required to
demonstrate a good understanding of Equality Act 2010 requirements and the public sector equality duty;
including that equality of opportunity is central to internal processes / workforce; and services will be
regularly tailored and reviewed to meet the diverse needs of Bristol citizens.

Mitigations

While no immediate negative impacts of this policy have been identified, we are aware of existing
disproportionate impacts with relation to transport as outlined below. Any disproportionate impacts of existing
infrastructure would be mitigated through this policy as this policy aims to improve residents’ access to a
sustainable, affordable transport option. Bristol City Council has a commitment to limit disproportionate impacts
upon any of the protected characteristics listed in the Equality Assessment.

One key mitigation that has been outlined by a recent WECIL report on the East Bristol Liveable Neighbourhood
trial was the placement of handles on cycle hangars, and especially the model of hangar installed with a handle
lower to the ground, which can be difficult to access for some Disabled or older people. Options for more
accessible, higher handle placement will be explored with the hangar operator.

PROTECTED CHARACTERISTICS
Age: Young People Does your analysis indicate a disproportionate impact? Yes ☒ No ☐
Potential impacts: Currently young people in Bristol are:
• Identified as a group at risk of transport poverty [1]
• Vehicle ownership tends to be low among younger age groups
partly due to the costs of learning to drive, as well as maintaining a
vehicle and the associated insurance costs, making this group
increasingly reliant on public transport. [3]
• Transport affordability and availability are key challenges for
younger people relying on public transport to access work, education,
and other activities. [3]
• The availability and affordability of transport can contribute to
children’s access to important resources. [3]
• Active travel presents an opportunity to promote health and
wellbeing among children. This is particularly important for children who
are more likely to develop childhood obesity due to other
characteristics, including deprivation and Black, Asian, and minoritised
ethnic background. [3]

Page 170
• The effects of air pollution are particularly significant for the health
of children. [3]
• Children from a lower socio-economic background are also more
likely to be exposed to high levels of pollution due to living in densely
populated urban areas. [3]

The percentage of young people who cycle to work or cycle at least once a week in
Bristol is broadly similar to the Bristol average, therefore no disproportionate impact
is expected in this regard. However, the percentage of young people for whom lack
of transport options prevents them from leaving their home when they want to is
significantly worse than the Bristol average. Installing cycle hangars in residential
areas could therefore disproportionately, positively impact these young people, by
helping to provide an affordable and secure form of cycle storage which hopes to
broaden their transport options.
Mitigations: No mitigations needed as a positive impact is anticipated.
Age: Older People Does your analysis indicate a disproportionate impact? Yes ☒ No ☐
Potential impacts: • Identified as a group at higher risk of transport poverty [1]
• Identified as a group at risk of poverty [1]
• Older people are more likely to be Disabled or have a long-term health
problem that can affect their ability to use transport, including: mobility
impairments, hearing impairments and cognitive impairments. [3]
• Research from Age UK has found that an improved provision of active
transport (including walking and cycling) could disproportionately benefit
older people. Increased provision of active transport is likely to improve the
amount of physical activity, which is linked to better cognitive performance,
better mental health outcomes and reduce overall morbidity and mortality.
[3]
• Currently only 8% of men and 3% of women over the age of 65 in the UK
cycle, a much lower proportion compared to both the general population in
the UK and those over the age of 65 in European countries. [3]

The percentage of older people who cycle to work or cycle at least once a
week in Bristol are both significantly lower than the Bristol average, which
infers that this policy may be less beneficial to older people than it might be
to the rest of Bristol’s population.

As a cycle hangar uses one parking space, there is a chance that this may have
a negative impact on an older person who is perhaps more reliant on a
vehicle. However, in terms of commuting, a significantly lower percentage of
older people drive to work than the Bristol population.

For those older people who do cycle regularly, there will a positive impact as
there will be provision of easily accessible on-street secure cycle parking.
Mitigations: To mitigate the potential impact of removing a car parking space, there will be
an opportunity for residents to object to the location of a proposed cycle
hangar through the Traffic Regulation Order process and this will be taken
into consideration when deciding the location.
Disability Does your analysis indicate a disproportionate impact? Yes ☒ No ☐
Potential impacts: • Undertaking an analysis of current transport trends among Disabled people
it is important to note that Disabled people are not a homogenous group,
their needs and abilities can vary greatly depending on the nature and
severity of their disability.
Page 171 [3]
• Appropriate transport provision enables Disabled people to participate in
their community, maintain social networks, and access employment,
education, healthcare and other services. [3]
• The unemployment rate in the UK for Disabled people was 6.7% in 2019,
despite this rate having reduced, it is still nearly double the national
unemployment rate. Evidence shows that difficulty in accessing transport is
the second most common barrier to work among Disabled people. [3]
• For neurodiverse people, a lack of routine or unexpected events can
become overwhelming, leading to high levels of stress and anxiety. [3]
• Disabled passengers often travel to, from and between legs of their journey
via various transport modes, sometimes with challenges to the successful
completion of the first and last mile of a journey. [3]
• Air quality depletion linked to traffic exhaust emissions can have detrimental
effects on certain groups of Disabled people. The British Lung Foundation
states those at highest risk to air pollution effects are those already living
with pre-existing health conditions, predominantly those with such lung
conditions as asthma or Chronic Obstructive Pulmonary Disease (COPD). [3]
As a cycle hangar uses one parking space, there is a chance that this may have
a negative impact on a Disabled person who is perhaps more reliant on a
vehicle.

In terms of the hangars themselves, the standard hangar design does not
accommodate for those that use adapted cycles, which could mean that a
Disabled person who uses an adapted cycle would not benefit from a
standard cycle hangar on their street.
Mitigations: To mitigate the potential impact of removing a car parking space, there will be
an opportunity for residents to object to the location of a proposed cycle
hangar through the Traffic Regulation Order process and this will be taken
into consideration when deciding the location.

It could be possible for this to be considered on a case by case or request
basis with the future secured funding and the rollout of hangars across the
city but this will be decided by the BCC delivery team.
Sex Does your analysis indicate a disproportionate impact? Yes ☒ No ☐
Potential impacts: Women:
• Identified as a group at risk of transport poverty [1]
• Having less access to private means of transport such as bicycles,
motorcycles and cars, women are inclined to take work closer to home,
often in the informal sector, which may limit their opportunities for finding
better paid or higher skilled positions. This may be exacerbated by a limited
availability of part-time work or work that fits around school hours. [1]

There may also be a disproportionate positive impact on this group – the
Quality of Life survey shows that less women cycle to work and cycle at least
once a week than men. In providing accessible and secure cycle parking, this
may improve women’s ability to cycle to work and cycle at least once a week.
Mitigations: No mitigations needed as positive impact.

Potential impacts: There is no data to suggest any kind of disproportionate impact on those with this
protected characteristic.

Mitigations:
Pregnancy / Maternity Does your analysis indicate a disproportionate impact? Yes ☒ No ☐
Potential impacts: - Caring responsibilities also tend to disproportionately fall to women and
often require making multiple short journeys during a day – for example, to
drop off children at school, visit family members and shop for food – which
creates an additional challenge if private transport is not available. In such
cases public transport services may not sufficiently interconnected, requiring
journeys with several changes and a long commuting time. [3]
- Research evidences that gender inequality in cycling is common, with low
levels of cycling among women compared to men. This could be due to
cultural factors that remain in place despite an increase in the promotion of
active travel. Promoting gender quality and normalising cycling culturally
could benefit women in increasing the numbers of those cycling regularly [3]
- Women are more likely to make trips with children so may be more
likely to use a cargo bike. Currently, the standard cycle hangar does
not allow enough space for a cargo bike to be stored. In this way, and
due to the loss of a parking space, there may be a disproportionate
impact on females travelling with a child.
- Parents and families are also more likely to lack cycle storage for
multiple bikes. This is important for the policy as this is likely to
increase demand of a particular hangar for one household.
Mitigations: - There is scope for the policy to include that cycle hangars that can
accommodate adapted cycles and cargo bikes on request from
residents although this has not been confirmed at this stage.
- The policy hopes to fulfil demand for all household types, in keeping a
fair and equitable process.
Gender reassignment Does your analysis indicate a disproportionate impact? Yes ☐ No ☒
Potential impacts: There is no data to suggest any kind of disproportionate impact on those with this
protected characteristic.
Mitigations:
Race Does your analysis indicate a disproportionate impact? Yes ☒ No ☐
Potential impacts:
• Black, Asian, and minoritised ethnic households consistently have the highest
rates of poverty, and White British households have the lowest [1]
• Adults from Asian, Black or other ethnic groups took substantially fewer trips
per person in 2017 than those from white or mixed groups. [1]
• In terms of cycling, DfT data suggests that Black and Asian adults are least
likely to cycle [3]
Due to lower rates of cycling among the Black, Asian and minority
communities, the demand for cycle hangar spaces is likely to be stronger in
areas with higher populations of white residents (Walking and Cycling Index).
This can cause the Black, Asian and minority race communities to become
seldom heard voices and keep cycling rates low among this respective
community as the city continues to not provide safe cycle storage spread
across to wide ranges of residents.
Mitigations: However, initially, the policy would aim to fulfil needs on a demand basis. This
process may be able to be controlled by filling out a request questionnaire.
Using this survey, it could be one way in which we provide cycle storage to
minority race residents in hope to increase cycle mobility among the
communities. However, this is not a promise, and BCC will remain fair and
equitable in the roll out of this and all future policies.

Potential impacts: There is no data to suggest any kind of disproportionate impact on those with this

Marriage & Does your analysis indicate a disproportionate impact? Yes ☐ No ☒
civil partnership
Potential impacts: Marriage / civil partnerships are likely to have higher likelihood of sharing a
room in Flats, Shared Occupancy, Converted or Commercial properties. This is
disproportionate to single residents in Bristol, so demands of cycle storage
may be higher for this protected characteristic as they are more likely to lack
the current space to store two or more bikes in their current properties.
Mitigations: However, the policy would aim to fulfil needs on a demand basis. This process
may be able to be controlled by filling out a request questionnaire. Using this
survey, it could be one way in which we provide cycle storage to residents
who lack space in marriages / civil partnerships. However, this is not a
promise, and BCC will remain fair and equitable in the roll out of this and all
future policies.
OTHER RELEVANT CHARACTERISTICS
Socio-economic Does your analysis indicate a disproportionate impact? Yes ☐ No ☒
(deprivation)
Potential impacts: • Income was found to be one of the defining aspects of socio-economic
inequality. Transport costs and affordability are central to the impact of
transport on inequality. If transport is too expensive, then people are not
able to make the journeys they need to get into work or move into
education and training that could improve their prospects [1]
• Transport is important in obtaining a job, keeping a job, or getting a better
job. Improving provision for cycling can also have a positive impact on
employment opportunities. [1]
• Cycling is regarded as a good way to widen travel horizons for
disadvantaged individuals. [1]
• Lower income households have higher levels of non-car ownership – female
heads of house, children, younger and older people, people from a Black,
Asian and minoritised ethnic background and Disabled people are often
concentrated in this statistic. [3]
• Increasing promotion and provision of active transport directly benefits
people who reside in deprived areas by improving the local air quality and
improving their health and wellbeing. For example, obesity rates for
children are highest amongst those in deprived areas. [3]
• Ensuring feelings of safety are increased will encourage more people to
participate in active travel modes. [3]
Socio-economic deprivation is higher among the populations who do not own
a car or live in socially rented or council owned property. However, bike
ownership is also particularly low due to the lack of secure cycle parking
citywide in this accommodation type, leaving the most deprived the most
immobile in the Bristol community
Mitigations: This policy will provide a positive impact to this protected characteristic as it
pledges to provide cycle storage to uplift active mobility. Although this will be
done on a request basis initially, once these have been fulfilled, the location
of hangars could be based on a more targeted approach, providing hangars
for more deprived communities in Bristol.

Potential impacts:
Mitigations:

Does the proposal create any benefits for people based on their characteristics?
Parking a bike inside brings in dirt, causes clutter and reduces living space. Bristol terraced housing
frequently lacks garden access, creating inconvenience and mess even if opting for outdoor garden
storage. Landlords or letting agencies may not permit internal storage at all; a higher-than-average
Bristol renting rate of 45.2% against 37.5% for England and Wales making this yet more pressing. Flats,
Shared Occupancy, Converted or Commercial properties also each have their own challenges for internal
bicycle storage. Safe cycle storage has been seen to increase bike ownership by 24%, and 94% of users
find cycle hangars both secure and easy to use, increasing trips by 1.44 on average per week. For
hangars previously installed at social housing sites, Sustrans’ Cycle Hangars Evaluation Report estimates
£1.77 of economic benefit for every £1 spent, and that those assigned a cycle hangar space increased
the number of cycle trips they make by 62%.
Bristol’s 2023 Quality of Life Survey found a 7% gap between the most deprived decile and the city
average for people cycling at least once a week (reducing to 4.6% in the 2024 survey). As a key barrier to
cycling, a lack of safe storage in areas of high deprivation must be addressed as a matter of equality.
Protected groups such as Disabled or older people are often excluded from cycling when storage would
involve lifting over steps, up sets of stairs, or awkward movements down narrow corridors. A cycle
hangar policy aims to make setting off by bike as easy and accessible as by car.

A city which is more active is one that is cleaner, more equitable and better connected. Its citizens are
less lonely due to barriers to independent travel being removed and have the mental and physical health
benefits of regular exercise. With an improved active travel offer, those without the means to drive are
nonetheless able to benefit from a modern, safe transport network.

With fewer people driving for short journeys, air quality improves, and general emissions decrease. In
the long-term, the overall energy usage per capita of the Bristol transport network will be much lower
with greater active travel uptake, and overall emissions of greenhouse gases will decrease.

When implemented alongside other key changes to infrastructure in the city, a cycle hangar policy will
enable more people to choose to travel by bike, and bring a host of benefits for health, sociability,
sustainability and equality.

Summary of significant beneficial impacts and opportunities to support the Climate, Ecological and Corporate
Strategies (ENV1,2,3,4):
ENV1 Carbon neutral: Emissions of climate changing gases

A cycle hangar policy works towards Bristol’s One City goal of being climate neutral in transport, requiring a 40%
reduction in vehicle miles by 2030. Providing more people with the opportunity to safely store a bike has been
proven to increase ownership and rates of cycling, and contributes to a shift in transport mode prevalence away
from greenhouse-gas-emitting vehicles.

ENV2 Ecological recovery: Wildlife and habitats

Ecological recovery is not within the remit of the cycle hangar policy; other forthcoming wider approaches to the
kerbside space, including the Kerbside Strategy, will be responsible for improving green space and habitat
provision on Council adopted highway.

ENV3 A cleaner, low-waste city: Consumption of resources and generation of waste

As with all efforts towards net zero, consumption of resources per capita is expected to drop with a reduction in
car dependency. The resources required for raw materials, shipping, assembly and eventual disposal is
magnitudes lower for bikes than cars. Cycle hangars, too, are far less resource-intensive than a car to produce and
install, and will remain in place indefinitely, until maintenance issues result in a replacement.

ENV4 Climate resilience: Bristol’s resilience to the effects of climate change

A cycle hangar policy is not expected to directly alleviate the effects of climate change, but will aid in mitigation of
its causes in the form of greenhouse gas emissions reduction, and per capita resource reduction.

Summary of significant adverse impacts and how they can be mitigated:

No significant adverse impacts are anticipated.
