Agenda Item 11

Committee Report

KEY DECISION OR FOR INFORMATION: Key decision affects 2 or more wards

COMMITTEE: Transport and Connectivity Committee

DATE: 23 October 2025

TITLE: Centreline Policy on residential and selected 20 mph roads.

Ward(s): Citywide

Officer presenting the report: Shaun Taylor Job title: Head of Highways

Committee Chair: Councillor Ed Plowden

Executive Director lead: John Smith: Executive Director for Growth & Regeneration

Proposal origin: Committee Chair

Purpose of Report:

1. To seek approval from the Transport and Connectivity Committee on a policy position that
road centrelines on selected 20mph roads are not reinstated as default following
resurfacing so that road safety benefits are realised by this maintenance programme, and
are only reinstated where determined essential by the Road Safety Team. Alongside this,
the safeguarding process for reviewing and implementing changes is presented.

Evidence Base

1. The City Council has adopted a Safe Systems Approach to Road Safety where all
elements of the road system should work together to minimise the chance of a crash, and
in the event of a crash, to prevent someone being killed or seriously injured.
2. One of the key approaches in the Safe Systems Approach to Road Safety is ‘safe speeds’
reflecting research that a reduction in speed helps to reduce the severity and volume of
collisions. Therefore, opportunities are sought to encourage lower speeds through road
safety interventions and other highway projects and workstreams.
3. Research has shown that motorists tend to slow down when a centreline is absent in
certain circumstances as they adjust for their uncertainty over their positioning on the road
and that of other drivers. This has also shown that this has not led to a corresponding
increase in collisions overall on slower residential roads. (Contribution to cycle safety of
centre line removal on 20 mph and 30 mph speed limit roads - Adrian Davis, Alan Tapp,
Stella Warren, Bristol Business School, Faculty of Business & Law, 2019)
4. Other research has shown that the speed of vehicles overtaking cyclists reduces with the
absence of a road centreline. (Influence of road markings, lane widths and driver behaviour
on proximity and speed of vehicles overtaking cyclists - Stella C. Shackel, John Parkin
2014)
Template approved March 25
Page 183
OFFICIAL
5. The resurfacing and surface dressing programmes provide an opportunity to amend road
markings and encourage this behaviour on existing roads by only reinstating centrelines
where they were considered necessary on road safety grounds. In some cases there may
be little need for the centre lines and other road markings, and their presence may be due
to historic reasons meaning they are no longer needed in any case.
6. For example, as can be seen in the picture of a recently surface-dressed road, with the
expansion of car ownership over the last 50 years many roads now have significantly more
parking along both sides of the road and the historic road markings are simply not needed.

As per the legal comments below, it is important to state there is no requirement in law to
provide centre lines on all roads and that all legally required lines, for example at junctions
and traffic signals will be provided.
7. Although there is no legal requirements for 20mph surface roundels to be reinstated for the
speed limit to be enforceable, they will usually be reinstated. Any roundels or other
markings should be placed away from parked vehicles.
8. Removing centrelines is technically straight forward to do when roads are resurfaced
through routine maintenance and can provide an initial saving in capital and reduces future
revenue maintenance costs.
9. After consideration and discussion with a variety of disciplines within Transport and Urban
Design teams, the Quality Assurance Board for transport capital schemes previously
recommended that road centrelines would not be reinstated following resurfacing or
surface dressing of 20mph roads unless, following a case-by-case review, they were
deemed essential to maintaining safety along the route.
10. The process for achieving this involves two separate teams, Highways Maintenance to put
the surfacing programmes together and deliver any changes agreed and Road Safety to
review the programmes and recommend where road centrelines should or should not be
reinstated. Both teams are then involved in handling feedback from residents and
Councillors concerned about road safety in the absence of these road markings following
both programmes.
11. The process to review each section of road due for resurfacing, redesign the road markings
and mark out and supervise the work adds some amount of additional work per site shared
across both teams involved.
12. The newly established Road Safety Team now have additional resources and can review
these programmes and provide appropriate advice to Highway Maintenance. The Highway
Maintenance teams likewise have the skills and processes in place to set out any changes
on site and supervise the contractor accordingly.
Template approved March 25
Page 184
OFFICIAL
13. As the surfacing programmes are annual programmes, the centreline reinstatement
process could move to a two-year programme post the annual resurfacing treatment, which
can reduce the risks and challenges above. This would mean that in year 1, centrelines
are only installed on those higher risk roads where the road safety team identifies a need
for them. These would be monitored over the course of the next 12 months by the Road
Safety Team and if concerns do become apparent or valid complaints are received,
appropriate sections of centreline could be reinstated in year 2 as part of the programme of
road markings across the city.
14. The overall concept of improving safety by removing road centrelines is a difficult one for
people not working in the field to contemplate and so concerns and complaints will also be
received when centrelines are not reinstated following resurfacing work. A short templated
response will be used to respond to these concerns alongside the review process
described above
15. The option above does provide other challenges as the lining is now procured as part of the
dressing so if the decision is reversed this will require a new contract and new contractor
so will add cost to reinstatement and officer resources.
16. The proposed process is shown on page 3 of Appendix A.

Alternative options considered:

1. Maintain the current approach that all road centrelines are reinstated like for like post
resurfacing programmes. This would reduce the staff time required by both teams but
would not help realise the safety and longer terms maintenance cost savings.

2. Assume the same default policy decision of not reinstating centrelines post resurfacing
programmes unless there is a road safety need, but without the year 2 review as part of
this process enabling further amendments post monitoring. This would realise the same
initial benefits as to the current proposal and would reduce the additional work required by
both teams to monitor and implement any changes in year 2. However, if valid concerns
are raised or identified following the non-reinstatement of the centrelines, additional capital
funding and resources would need to be found to make changes outside of the resurfacing
programme. This would be much more difficult to achieve as lining contractors are unlikely
to accept the small scale works orders that would be required.

Officer Recommendations:

That the Committee for Transport and Connectivity
1. Approves the policy the default position of not reinstating centreline road markings post
resurfacing programmes on residential streets and selected 20mph speed limit unless there
is a specific road safety concern.
2. Approves the proposed process set out in Appendix A.

Corporate Strategy alignment:

1. This brief aligns with the Corporate Strategy, including its Vision to “create a sustainable
and equitable Bristol that enables everyone to be safe, well and thrive”, and priority 4
“Making it easier, greener and safer to travel into and around Bristol”.

City Benefits:

Template approved March 25
Page 185
OFFICIAL
1. The City Council has adopted a Safe Systems Approach to Road Safety and has a target
of zero people killed or seriously injured in road traffic collisions by 2030. This proposal will
help to reduce vehicle speeds on residential roads, making it safer for all, in particular the
more vulnerable road users.

Consultation Details:
1. As this is a technical process, no consultation has taken place or would take place on the
overall approach or on a street-by-street basis. However, part of the monitoring of the
programme will include reviewing local concerns raised by local Councillors and residents
where the centrelines have not been reinstated in year 1.

Professional comments section:

1. Finance Advice: This report requests approval to not reinstate centreline road markings
post resurfacing programmes on residential streets with 20mph speed limit unless there is
a specific road safety concern. This will generate a very small saving, less than £1k
citywide per annum, to the council from reduced use of materials.
Finance Business Partner: Ben Hegarty, Finance Business Partner Growth and
Regeneration, 4 August 2025.

2. Legal Advice: The use of centre lines is not an absolute requirement. For residential roads,
the Department for Transport’s guidance ‘Manual for Streets’ advises that there is some
evidence that, in appropriate circumstances, the absence of white lines can encourage
drivers to use lower speeds. It will therefore depend on a case-by-case basis, whether centre
lines should be used for safety reasons.

OFFICIAL
Appendix A: Centreline Reinstatement Policy

Background

The City Council has adopted a Safe Systems Approach to Road Safety where all elements of the road
system should work together to minimise the chance of a crash, and in the event of a crash, to
prevent someone being killed or seriously injured.

One of the key approaches in the Safe Systems Approach to Road Safety is ‘safe speeds’ reflecting
research that a reduction in speed helps to reduce the severity and volume of collisions. Therefore,
opportunities are sought to encourage lower speeds through road safety interventions and other
highway projects and workstreams.

Research has shown that motorists tend to slow down when a centreline is absent in certain
circumstances as they are not sure of their positioning on the road. This has also shown that this has
not led to a corresponding increase in collisions overall on slower residential roads.

Therefore, centrelines are no longer provided as a standard in the design of many new residential
neighbourhoods and as part of new highway schemes unless considered necessary.

It was identified that the resurfacing and surface dressing programmes provided an opportunity to
take the same approach on existing roads by only reinstating centrelines where they were
considered necessary. The idea was that this would help to reduce speeds and the severity of
collisions on these routes, as well as reducing the costs of maintaining these lines over future years.

After careful consideration and discussion with a variety of disciplines within Transport and Urban
Design teams, the Council’s Quality Assurance process recommended that road centrelines would
not be reinstated following resurfacing or surface dressing of 20mph roads unless, following a case-
by-case review, they were deemed essential to maintaining safety along the route.

Therefore, it was agreed that the presumption would be that centrelines would not be reinstated
following resurfacing on 20mph residential roads, unless such a review considered they were critical
to the safety of that section of road.

Current status

The process for achieving this involves two separate teams, Highways Maintenance to put the
surfacing programmes together and deliver any changes agreed and Road Safety to review the
programmes and recommend where road centrelines should or should not be reinstated. Both
teams are then involved in handling complaints from residents and Councillors concerned about
road safety in the absence of these road markings following both programmes.

Therefore, the overall process requires significant resources from both teams to ensure that the final
layout, with or without centrelines, is safe for all users. Over the last few years, both teams have
been stretched for resources and therefore have not been able to prioritise this process. As such,
rather than leave roads with a higher risk of collisions without centrelines, the process has resorted
back to Highway Maintenance reinstating the centrelines like for like.

In response to a question at Member Forum in March 2024, Highways stated that:

Page 188
“When we resurface roads, including preventive maintenance such as surface dressing and micro
asphalt, we reinstate lines on a like for like basis. If there are places that they have not been
reinstated, please give us more information so we can follow up.”

Challenges

Reviewing each section of road due for resurfacing, redesigning the road markings and marking out
and supervising the work adds about 2-3 hours per site shared across both of the teams involved.
Both of the teams have found it difficult to resource this in in recent years given other priorities at
the time of the year when this work needs to be undertaken to fit into the annual programme.

The Highway Maintenance Engineers rely on the Traffic Engineers in the Local Transport teams to
design any changes to the road markings in line with the requirements and specifications within the
Traffic Signs Regulations and General Directions (TSRGD). Therefore, in the absence of a design, the
contractor is instructed to install a like for like replacement.

The scheduling of resurfacing programmes and the subsequent relining of roads post resurfacing is
difficult to coordinate, given the need to fit into contractors’ programmes and the time of year this
has to take place. Therefore, there is limited opportunity to take the time required to review the
surfacing programmes, or to reconsider once the initial surface dressing and relining programmes
have been completed.

The overall concept of improving safety by removing road centrelines is a difficult one for people not
working in the field to contemplate and so concerns and complaints will also be received when
centrelines are not reinstated following resurfacing work.

Opportunities

Removing centrelines is technically straight forward to do when roads are resurfaced through
routine maintenance and can provide an initial saving in capital and reduces future maintenance
costs.

The QA process offers the opportunity for a Safe Systems Approach to be taken and for elements
like this to be identified and for changes to be easily made after the new road surface has been laid
to improve road safety as part of a joined-up approach. The highways maintenance team now work
on a 2 year programme for delivery. 1st year undertaking assessment and structural repairs. The 2nd
year delivers the final preventative treatment. Therefore, the surfacing programmes can be
circulated with sufficient time for the Road Safety Team to review and advise accordingly, then these
opportunities could be realised at relatively little expense.

The newly established Road Safety Team now have additional resources and are able to review these
programmes and provide appropriate advice to Highway Maintenance. Highway Maintenance teams
should likewise have sufficient skills and capacity to set out any changes on site and supervise the
contractor accordingly.

As the surfacing programmes are annual programmes, the centreline reinstatement process can
move to a two-year programme post the annual resurfacing treatment, which could reduce the risks

Page 189
and challenges above. This would mean that in year 1, centrelines are only installed on those higher
risk roads where the road safety team identifies a need for them. These could be monitored over
the course of the next 12 months by the Road Safety Team and Highway Maintenance teams and if
concerns do become apparent or complaints are received, appropriate sections of centreline could
be reinstated in year 2 as part of the programme of road markings.

Proposed Process:

• Highway Maintenance, as programme/project manager, issue resurfacing programmes for
assessment to the Road Safety team and a programme for when any changes need to be
requested.
• Road Safety Team to review the programmes and attend, investigate sites if required on the
assumption that centrelines are not reinstated in full on 20mph roads and are only
reinstated where they address a particular concern such as head on collisions or collisions at
due to driver alignment.
• Road Safety Team to create a design drawing for Highway Maintenance for any roads where
centrelines are proposed to remain, clearly identifying the road marking layout to be
reinstated post resurfacing.
• Highway Maintenance provide the designs to the lining contractor, mark out and supervise
the work as part of the post resurfacing road marking programme for that year.
• Road Safety monitor the site for one year and Road Safety will provide Highway
Maintenance with a design drawing if any additional sections of centreline are deemed to be
required on safety grounds after that period.
• Highway Maintenance include these reinstated road centreline changes as part of its lining
reinstatement works across the city i.e. 1 year after the initial lining work.
• Any further changes after that point are delivered through the capital programme by the
appropriate team in City Transport.

Step 1: What do we want to do?
The purpose of an Equality Impact Assessment is to assist decision makers in understanding the impact of proposals
as part of their duties under the Equality Act 2010. Detailed guidance to support completion can be found here
Equality Impact Assessments (EqIA) (sharepoint.com).
This assessment should be started at the beginning of the process by someone with a good knowledge of the
proposal and service area, and sufficient influence over the proposal. It is good practice to take a team approach to
completing the equality impact assessment. Please contact the Equality and Inclusion Team early for advice and
feedback.

1.1 What are the aims and objectives/purpose of this proposal?
Briefly explain the purpose of the proposal and why it is needed. Describe who it is aimed at and the intended aims /
outcomes. Where known also summarise the key actions you plan to undertake. Please use plain English, avoiding
jargon and acronyms. Equality Impact Assessments are viewed by a wide range of people including decision-makers
and the wider public.
The City Council intending on implementing a new approach to road safety by selectively removing road
centrelines on 20mph residential streets. This forms part of the wider Road Safety strategy, which aims to reduce
the risk and severity of collisions. Research shows that drivers tend to slow down when centrelines are absent,
and this has not led to an increase in crashes on slower roads. By not reinstating centrelines during resurfacing
unless deemed essential for safety, the Council hopes to encourage safer driving behaviour, improve road safety
outcomes, and reduce long-term maintenance costs.

1.2 Who will the proposal have the potential to affect?
☒ Bristol City Council workforce ☐ Service users ☒ The wider community
☐ Commissioned services ☒ City partners / Stakeholder organisations
Additional comments:

1.3 Will the proposal have an equality impact?
Could the proposal affect access levels of representation or participation in a service, or does it have the potential to
change e.g. quality of life: health, education, or standard of living etc.?
If ‘No’ explain why you are sure there will be no equality impact, then skip steps 2-4 and request review by Equality
and Inclusion Team.
If ‘Yes’ complete the rest of this assessment, or if you plan to complete the assessment at a later stage please state
this clearly here and request review by the Equality and Inclusion Team.

☐ Yes ☒ No [please select]

Page 191
OFFICIAL
The proposal to omit centrelines on 20mph residential roads is based on established road safety research and
aligns with the City Council’s Safe Systems Approach. This approach is designed to improve safety outcomes for all
road users, including vulnerable groups, by encouraging lower vehicle speeds and reducing the severity of
collisions.
Key points supporting the decision not to require an EqIA include:
• Universal Safety Benefit: The intervention is intended to reduce speeds and improve safety for all road
users, regardless of age, disability, or background. It does not target or disadvantage any specific group.
• Evidence-Based Design: Research shows that removing centrelines in low-speed environments can lead to
more cautious driving without increasing collision rates. This supports the idea that the change is
beneficial across the board.
• Case-by-Case Review: The Council has committed to reviewing each road individually. Centrelines will still
be reinstated where they are deemed essential for safety, ensuring that specific needs (e.g. visibility,
layout complexity) are considered.
• Low-Risk Context: The proposal applies only to 20mph residential roads, which are already designed to
be low-speed, low-risk environments. These roads typically have lower traffic volumes and fewer complex
manoeuvres, reducing the likelihood of disproportionate impacts.
• No Change to Access or Services: The proposal does not affect access to services, infrastructure, or public
transport, nor does it introduce physical barriers or restrictions that could impact protected groups.

Agenda Item 12

Committee Report

KEY DECISION OR FOR INFORMATION: Key decision over £500k

COMMITTEE: Transport and Connectivity Committee

DATE: 23 October 2025

TITLE: Vehicle Towing Contract

Ward(s): Citywide

Officer presenting the report: Shaun Taylor Job title: Head of Highways

Committee Chair: Councillor Ed Plowden

Executive Director lead: John Smith: Executive Director for Growth & Regeneration

Proposal origin: BCC Staff

Purpose of Report:

1. To seek approval to reprocure the Highway Maintenance Vehicle Towing Contract. The
contract is essential to gain site possession to carryout Statutory Highway Maintenance
and other related works in Bristol’s busy urban environment. The towing contractor
removes parked vehicles which remain in place despite advanced notification of the work,
and places them in a neighbouring street.

2. To reprocure the contract for a longer period of time, i.e. 5 years. A longer contract will
encourage contractors to invest in a more modern fleet and equipment, as our current
supplier uses some older vehicles, some of which are not CAZ compliant. Car lifting
equipment can also be old, which is slow to operate, so having a knock-on effect on
clearing sites and allowing maintenance work to proceed.

Evidence Base

1. Full site possession is often required to allow for Highway Maintenance works to take
place, including carriageway patching works, carriageway preventive maintenance
including surface dressing and micro asphalt, and surfacing works. The contract is also
used by other teams including Parks for when they pollard street trees.

2. Despite advanced notification of works, which includes street signage and letter drops, we
still encounter parked vehicles, which would prevent works from proceeding without the
ability to move them. The problem is more challenging in central and highly populated
areas, where on street parking is in high demand.

3. The contractor will be required to notify BCC Parking Services, BCC Customer Services
and the Police regarding the location of each vehicle moved, so that owners can locate
their vehicles.
Template approved March 25
Page 199
OFFICIAL
4. When undertaking preventative and surfacing programs, it is important to cover the entire
footprint of the road or footway as leaving patches for parked cars would require officers
returning at a later date to undertake more expensive repairs. This was previously
estimated at over ten times the cost of moving a vehicle.

Alternative options considered:

1. The only other option would be not to move parked vehicles and either work around them,
missing areas where vehicles are parked or postpone or cancel the works. It is often not
possible to work around parked vehicles, as the remaining road width may not be sufficient
to allow for safe systems of working. It would not be possible to carryout out some
surfacing works without a site which is totally clear of parked vehicles. Postponing and or
cancelling works may result in a compensation event, where contractors claim their costs
for losses incurred.

2. Option to join with the parking services contract was considered but the contracts work
under different legislation and contracts as the parking services contract is a concession
contract at no cost to the council under Traffic Regulations and the Highways contract is at
our cost and under Highways Act.

Officer Recommendations:

That the Committee for Transport and Connectivity

1. Authorises the Executive Director for Growth & Regeneration in consultation with Chair
of the Transport and Connectivity Committee to re-procure and award the Vehicle
Towing Contract, in-line with the procurement routes and maximum budget envelopes
outlined in this report being £1million over 5 years.
2. Authorises the Executive Director for Growth & Regeneration in consultation with Chair
of the Transport and Connectivity Committee to invoke any subsequent
extensions/variations specifically defined in the contract(s) being awarded, up to the
maximum budget envelope outlined in this report.

Corporate Strategy alignment:

1. This brief aligns with the Corporate Strategy, including its Vision to “create a sustainable
and equitable Bristol that enables everyone to be safe, well and thrive”, and in particular
priority 4 “Making it easier, greener and safer to travel into and around Bristol” and
objective 3 “Maintain essential city infrastructure for safety, longevity and accessibility.”

City Benefits:

1. The contract facilitates Highway Maintenance works, allowing them to take place in a safe
and controlled environment. The subsequent works ensure that the highway is maintained
to a safe and reasonable standard. This offers a clear benefit to residents, the travelling
public, businesses and the transportation of goods.

Consultation Details:

1. As this is a contract to facilitate other works, forming part of the Council’s statutory duty to
maintain the highway, no consultation has taken place.

Professional comments section:

1. Finance Advice: The report is seeking approval to reprocure a longer contract period for
the Highway Maintenance Vehicle Towing Contract. The procurement of the contract will
need to follow the legal and procurement advice contained within this report.

Currently spend is c. £0.2 million a year on this contract, so over a five-year term, the total
cost would be around £1 million.

A significant portion of the contract funding is secured from various capital programmes
within Traffic & Highways and a few other departments such as Parks. The costs form part
of the capital surfacing programs on carriageways and footways, with the funding coming
from the City Region Sustainable Transport Settlement (CRSTS) maintenance block.

Regular monitoring and reporting of key performance indicators (KPI’s) and capital
spending forecasts will allow early identification of cost pressures or performance issues,
so action can be taken quickly, and risks managed.

A longer contract will need to include provision for periodic reviews of pricing to reflect
market conditions, reducing the risk of financial strain due to inflation or cost fluctuations.
Any increases in costs will need to be swiftly identified and mitigated so the service stays
within its budget envelope.

Finance Business Partner: Ben Hegarty, Finance Business Partner Growth and
Regeneration, 12 September 2025.

Act 2023 and the Councils own procurement rules. Legal services will advise and assist
officers with regard to the conduct of the procurement process and the resulting contractual
arrangements.

Agenda Item 13

Committee Report

KEY DECISION OR FOR INFORMATION: For Information

COMMITTEE: Transport and Connectivity Committee

DATE: 23 October 2025

TITLE: Workplace Parking Levy Update

Ward(s): Citywide

Officer presenting the report: Adam Crowther Job title: Head of City Transport

Committee Chair: Ed Plowden

Executive Director lead: John Smith: Executive Director for Growth & Regeneration

Proposal origin: Committee Chair

Purpose of Report: To update the committee on progress with the Workplace Parking Levy
Outline Business Case (OBC) and to outline progress on key project milestones.

Evidence Base:

1. In September 2024, the Transport and Connectivity Committee approved to spend up to
£1million on the Stage One development of a Workplace Parking Levy (WPL) project
including preparation of an OBC. In May 2025, an update report was brought to the
Committee to provide an overview of the planned key project milestones. It was agreed
that a report would be brought back to committee once key project milestones were met.

2. A WPL forms a potential part of Bristol City Council’s transport strategy and is outlined in
Joint Local Transport Plan 4 and the Bristol Transport Strategy as a key demand
management measure aimed at tackling congestion, particularly at peak times, and as a
means of providing significant additional revenue for transport improvements across the
city. The introduction of a WPL would help discourage car commuting and help increase
the use of alternative modes such as walking, cycling and public transport which would in
turn, improve people’s health and the local environment and can free up land for more
productive use.

3. There are three main stages to the development of a WPL scheme which are outlined
below. An estimated timescale is provided to indicate how long it should take to complete
each stage.

Template approved March 25
Page 210
OFFICIAL
Stage Timescale
Outline Business Case April 2025 – April 2026 (1 year)
Public Consultation, Full Business Case May 2026 – December 2027 (1 year to 18
and Scheme Approval to Secretary of months)
State for Transport
Implementation and Operation January 2028 – December 2029 (1-2
years)

4. The Stage One programme which is currently underway, is split into five key workstreams,
with a planned end date for all workstreams by April 2026. The timescales for some tasks
have shifted since last reported in May 2025 due to procurement and contractual issues
associated with appointing a consultant to assist with the project. However, the work is still
set to conclude by April 2026.

Stage Original timescale Revised date

1. Supporting studies April to June 2025 April to Sept 2025
and information
2. Surveys and data June to September 2025 October to November
collection 2025
3. Modelling, September to November November to January

4. WPL scheme October to March 2026 November to March

5. In stage 2, data will be gathered about existing businesses in Bristol, as well as
employment projections for allocated growth sites, and this will be used to estimate the
number of potentially liable Workplace Parking Places (WPPs) that might be included in a
WPL scheme for the city. Initial engagement will take place with employer representative
groups to gather their input on the approach being taken in subsequent stages of the
project, such as the geographical areas in scope for the modelling.

6. The data gathered in stage 2 will be used to undertake the modelling, forecasting and
impact assessments in stage 3. This work will help to forecast potential revenue to be
generated by the scheme, as well as identifying other likely impacts on factors such as
congestion, air quality and economic outcomes.

7. At the end of the stage 4 workstream, a draft scheme definition will be put forward, which
will include the potential boundaries of the scheme, proposed charge, parking space
thresholds, possible exemptions and hours of operation. Engagement will take place with
key employer representative groups in this stage, following on from initial engagement in
earlier stages of the project. Wider public consultation is not typically undertaken for a
WPL project at the OBC stage.

8. In terms of progress to date, the Project Team has been formed and worked has started on
the Strategic Case for the OBC. Supporting data and information has been collated for the
OBC, and to assist with the planned modelling and forecasting work. This includes datasets
Template approved March 25
Page 211
OFFICIAL
on air quality, congestion, carbon emissions, existing public parking stock, travel to work
patterns and employment statistics.

9. A competitive tendering exercise has been completed to appoint a consultant to assist with
the project. Five consultants were invited to tender, and two consultants submitted a bid.
Systra have been awarded the work, and an inception meeting was held with them on 17th
September 2025.

10. We have officially engaged Nottingham City Council to provide expert advice and act as a
critical friend to help guide the project with us. To date they have contributed to the
development of the project plan, the preparation and evaluation of the tender
documentation, the initial communications and marketing strategy, and stakeholder
management. Officers and some committee members will be undertaking a study visit to
Nottingham late October to learn more about their WPL scheme and the benefits it has
brought to the city.

11. The Communications Team at BCC is engaged and are currently drawing up a
Communications Plan for the project. This will be firmed up once the consultant has
confirmed when the workplace surveys are likely to commence.

12. In terms of current and forecast spend, approximately £100k is allocated to be spent on the
project in 2025/26.

Alternative options considered:

No alternative options are currently recommended as the approach is in line with advice
received from Nottingham City Council who are the only local authority in the country to
successfully implement at WPL so far.

When the project comes to committee for approval at subsequent stages of the programme,
different options will be presented from the modelling.

Officer Recommendations:

That the Committee for Transport and Connectivity notes this update report.

Corporate Strategy alignment:

1. Children and young people - improved access to transport schemes and initiatives will
help ensure all children and young people have a good start in life.
2. Economy and skills – the scheme will help tackle congestion which can have a significant
negative impact on economic growth. Revenue from a WPL scheme will help ensure
people have improved access to jobs, skills and training.
3. Environment and sustainability – the scheme will help reduce congestion and in turn will
work towards BCC carbon and air quality ambitions. It will also contribute to BCC climate
and ecological emergencies.
4. Healthcare and wellbeing – the scheme will help tackle health inequalities by providing
people with improved access to a range of sustainable transport opportunities such as
walking and cycling that will help improve their health and wellbeing.
5. Homes and communities – the scheme can help increase revenue towards transport
schemes that can provide more healthy, resilient and inclusive neighbourhoods.
Template approved March 25
Page 212
OFFICIAL
6. Transport and connectivity – a WPL can enable investment in a more reliable,
convenient and safer network of public transport and infrastructure for walking and cycling.
7. Effective development organisation – the project aligns with our One City approach of
working with our partners to deliver long-term ambitions for the city.

City Benefits:

1. The introduction of a WPL has benefits associated with a reduction in car use and a
subsequent increase in the use of alternative modes such as walking, cycling and public
transport. In turn this will have a positive impact on people’s health.
2. A reduction in car trips into the city centre is likely to result in a reduction in the amount of
carbon emissions.
3. A reduction in the number of car trips will have a beneficial impact on air quality.
4. Improved air quality and a reduction in traffic noise will have a beneficial impact on health.
5. A reduction in car-based trips will help reduce congestion and the negative impacts
associated with this such as delays and the negative impact this has on economic growth.
6. The greatest potential benefit will be through the transport measures/schemes that can be
funded through the revenue raised by a WPL. The revenue should be substantial,
predictable and locally generated and will significantly help BCC to develop and deliver
transport infrastructure and initiatives in the longer term.
7. The additional revenue generated could fund more sustainable transport options which are
particularly beneficial to younger, older people and disabled people who tend to have more
limited access to a car and rely more on public transport to access key services such as
education and healthcare.

progresses beyond the OBC stage.

progresses.

and Connectivity Policy Committee for approval and to progress to the next stage.

Professional comments section:

1. Finance Advice: This report is providing an update on the development of a workplace
parking levy (WPL) within Bristol. The WPL is intended to reduce congestion, improve air
quality, and generate revenue for sustainable transport improvements. Previous approval
was given to use £1 million of CAZ funding to develop this scheme and this is captured within
CAZ funding monitoring. It is anticipated that the levy will replenish the CAZ funding pot within
a few years of operation, however finance has not been provided with detail to support this.
To date, approximately £26,000 has been spent developing the levy and it is presumed that
most of the £1 million allocated will be spent within the 2026/27 financial year.

Future implementation will require IT systems to manage and enforce the levy. No systems
have yet been scoped, which could pose integration or cost risks later.
In addition, effective consultation is critical, especially given the potential impact on
employers and commuters. Reputational risk for the council, along with risk of public
resistance is high, if engagement is not well managed.

Finance Business Partner: Vivi Sargioti, Finance Business Partner - Growth &
Regeneration, 15 September 2025

legal implications arising from the report.

Legal Team Manager: Joanne Mansfield 11 September 2025

I.T. are supportive of the proposal and can be engaged through the existing work request
process.
